The Proof of Permission (Ibaha) Before Revelation
Presents two proofs for permission (ibaha) and the response to the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs for permission (ibaha) of acts before revelation and the Ash'ari response that intellect only judges suitability, not moral obligation.

Core Idea
Permission is argued by harmless use and God's creation, but responses show reliance on revelation.

Having discussed the prohibition argument, we now turn to the position that acts before revelation are permissible (ibaha). Two proofs are offered: first, an analogy to harmless uses of another's property (like shade from a wall), and second, an argument from God's creation of desire and the beneficial object. The Ash'ari response to the first proof is that the permissibility of those uses is known from revelation, not pure reason; the intellect only judges suitability. The second proof is incomplete in the text, but the pattern suggests it will be similarly countered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two proofs
Identify the first analogy (harmless use) and the second argument from creation (though incomplete).
2
Examine the response to the first proof
Understand why the analogy fails: the permissibility of harmless use is revealed, not rational.
3
Consider the second proof
Even though incomplete, think about how it might be refuted based on the pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل الحظر أنه تصرف في ملك الغير بلا إذنه لأن الكلام فيما قبل الشرع فيحرم كما في الشاهد والجواب : الفرق بتضرر الشاهد دون الغائب وأيضاً حرمة التصرف في ملك الشاهد مستفادة من الشرع دليل الإباحة وجهان : أحدهما : أنه تصرف لا يضر المالك فيباح كالاستظلال بجدار الغير والاقتباس من ناره والنظر في مرآته . والجواب أن الأصل ثبت بالشرع وحكم العقل فيه أي في الأصل بالمعنى المتنازع فيه ممنوع بل إنما يحكم فيه بمعنى الملاءمة وموافقة الغرض والمصلحة وثانيهما : أنه تعالى خلق العبد وخلق الشهوة فيه وخلق المنتفع به من
Proof of prohibition (hazr): It is disposal of another's property without his permission, because the discussion concerns before revelation, so it is forbidden as in the witnessed case. The answer: The difference is the harm (tadarur) of the witnessed person, unlike the unseen; also, the prohibition of disposal of the witnessed person's property is derived from revelation. Proof of permission (ibaha): Two aspects. The first: It is a disposal that does not harm the owner, so it is permitted, like shading oneself by another's wall, borrowing his fire, and looking in his mirror. The answer: The principle (al-asl) is established by revelation, and the intellect's judgment regarding it—i.e., in the principle—in the disputed sense is denied; rather, it only judges in the sense of suitability and agreement with purpose and interest. The second: That He, exalted, created the servant, created desire in him, and created the thing from which benefit is derived...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage examines the debate over the ruling of acts before revelation—specifically for those acts whose moral aspect is not rationally perceived. It presents the argument for prohibition (hazr): such acts are like disposing of another's property without permission, which is forbidden in worldly matters. The answer notes a difference: in worldly cases, harm affects the owner, but here the owner (God) is not harmed; also, the prohibition in worldly cases is itself from revelation. Then the argument for permission (ibaha) is given, with two proofs. The first proof analogizes to harmless uses of another's property like shade, fire, or mirror. The answer: the permissibility of those uses is established by revelation, not pure intellect; intellect only perceives suitability, not moral obligation. The second proof (incomplete) appeals to God's creation of desire and the object of benefit, implying permission. The text breaks off, but the pattern continues.

Key Terms
الإباحة
الاستظلال
الاقتباس
شهوة
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an action causes no harm, it is automatically permissible by reason alone.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that even harmless actions require revelation to be deemed permissible; reason only sees suitability.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first proof, what does the intellect judge regarding acts before revelation?
A) Moral obligation
B) Permissibility
C) Suitability and agreement with purpose
D) Prohibition

Key Takeaway
The permission of acts before revelation cannot be proven by reason alone because even harmless-use analogies rely on revelation for their moral ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when do you assume an action is permissible simply because it seems harmless? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you change starting today?
?
Think of a recent decision where you relied on your own reason to determine right and wrong without consulting revelation. Does the response in this passage convict you? What is one concrete step you can take to prioritize revelation over reasoning in that area?
?
If the intellect can only judge suitability and not moral permission, how should that reshape the way you seek guidance for your actions? Will you commit to pausing and asking 'What does revelation say?' before acting on what seems reasonable?

The Five Rulings Before Revelation: Ash'ari and Mu'tazila Views
The Mu'tazila classification of pre-revelation actions into five rulings based on reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila classification of pre-revelation actions into five rational rulings and the Ash'ari position that there are no such rulings before revelation.

Core Idea
For Mu'tazila, reason alone divides actions into five rulings before revelation.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the theological dispute over whether actions have inherent moral value known by reason before revelation. The Ash'ari position is that no rulings apply before revelation, while the Mu'tazila argue that reason can perceive good and evil and thus assign five rulings based on harm and benefit. Let's unpack their classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Read the opening lines that set the context: the Ash'ari view that God's consideration of interests is a favor, and the Mu'tazila view that it is obligatory.
2
Identify the five divisions
Focus on the Mu'tazila classification: actions are divided based on the presence of harm (mafsada) or benefit (maslaha). Trace each ruling from the text.
3
Note the exception
Observe that not all actions are covered: those whose moral aspect is not rationally perceived receive no specific ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح ولا نزاع في أنه عقلي نعم رعايته تعالى في أحكامه مصالح العباد ودفع مفاسدهم تفضل منه عندنا وواجب عليه عندكم بناء على أصلنا وأصلكم. ( وقد يحتج بلزوم إفحام الأنبياء ) وعجزهم عن إثبات نبوءتهم على تقدير كون الحسن والقبح شرعيين . ( وقد مر في باب النظر ) من الموقف الأول : (تفريع إذا ثبت أن الحاكم بالحسن والقبح هو الشرع دون العقل ) ثبت أن لا (حكم من الأحكام الخمسة وما ينتمي إليها ( للأفعال قبل الشرع وأما المعتزلة فقالوا : ما يدرك جهة حسنه أو قبحه بالعقل من الأفعال التي ليست اضطرارية ( ينقسم إلى الأقسام الخمسة لأنه إن اشتمل تركه على مفسدة فواجب أو فعله فحرام وإلا فإن اشتمل فعله على مصلحة فمندوب أو تركه فمكروه وإلا ) أي وإن لم يشتمل شيء من طرفيه على مفسدة ولا مصلحة فمباح وأما ما لا يدرك جهته (بالعقل لا في حسنه ولا في قبحه ( فلا يحكم فيه ) قبل الشرع ( بحكم خاص تفصيلي في فعل فعل) إذ لم يعرف فيه جهة تقتضيه قوله : ( وما ينتمي إليها ) أي من الثواب والعقاب . قوله : ( في فعل فعل الظاهر أن فعل الثاني تأكيد للأول ويحتمل حذف المضاف أو العاطف دون فعل أو وفعل .
The Sixth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: On Good and Evil. There is no dispute that it is rational; rather, His consideration—Exalted is He—in His rulings of the interests of servants and repelling their harms is a favor from Him according to us and obligatory upon Him according to you, based on our principle and your principle. (And one may argue by the necessary occurrence of silencing the prophets) and their inability to prove their prophethood on the assumption that good and evil are revealed. (And it has passed in the chapter on reasoning) from the First Station: (a corollary: if it is established that the judge of good and evil is revelation to the exclusion of reason) then it is established that (there is no ruling from the five rulings and what pertains to them) for actions before revelation. As for the Mu'tazila, they said: actions that are not involuntary and whose aspect of goodness or badness is perceived by reason (are divided into the five divisions, because if its omission entails harm then it is obligatory, or its commission entails harm then it is forbidden; otherwise, if its commission entails benefit then it is recommended, or its omission entails benefit then it is disliked; otherwise) i.e., if neither of its two sides includes harm nor benefit, then it is permissible. As for that whose aspect (is not perceived by reason—neither in its goodness nor in its badness—then no particular detailed ruling is made for it before revelation for any specific action), since the aspect that would necessitate it is not known. His saying: (and what pertains to them) i.e., reward and punishment. His saying: (in a specific action—it appears that the second 'action' is a confirmation of the first; and it is possible that there is an omission of the annexed or the conjunction without 'action' or 'and action'.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage defines the issue of al-husn wa al-qubh (good and evil) and sets out the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions. The author first notes that there is no dispute that moral judgment is rational in some sense, but the disagreement is about God's own acts: Ash'aris say His consideration of interests is a favor (tafaddul), while Mu'tazila say it is obligatory upon Him. Then it mentions an objection: if good and evil are only known through revelation, prophets would be unable to prove their prophethood rationally. The passage then revisits the corollary from the First Station: if the judge of good and evil is revelation, then there are no pre-revelation rulings. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, divide actions into those whose moral aspect is rationally perceived and those not. For the first type, they assign the five rulings based on the presence of harm or benefit: action whose omission entails harm is obligatory (wajib), commission of harm is forbidden (haram), benefit in commission is recommended (mandub), benefit in omission is disliked (makruh), and if neither harm nor benefit, it is permissible (mubah). For actions not rationally known, no specific ruling is given. The comments explain the terms: 'what pertains to them' means reward and punishment, and the phrase 'in a specific action' is analyzed grammatically.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح
الأحكام الخمسة
التحسين والتقبيح
المعتزلة
الأشاعرة
قبل الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'aris reject reason entirely in ethics.
Correction: The text shows Ash'aris accept that moral judgment is rational in some sense; the disagreement is about the basis of God's actions, not human reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the ruling for an action whose commission entails benefit but whose omission entails neither harm nor benefit?
A) Wajib (obligatory)
B) Mandub (recommended)
C) Mubah (permissible)
D) Makruh (disliked)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila hold that reason alone can determine a five-fold classification of actions before revelation based on the presence of harm or benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral decision where revelation seems silent, do you trust your rational assessment of harm and benefit, or do you fear that your reason might lead you astray? What does your automatic response reveal about your view of God's guidance?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God must act for the benefit of His servants, while the Ash'aris said it is a favor. Have you ever felt entitled to understand God's wisdom in a trial you faced? How does the debate between obligation and favor challenge the way you relate to God's decrees?
?
This passage shows that some actions are beyond rational perception—they wait for revelation. In your life, are there areas where you insist on rational answers instead of submitting to divine command? What would it cost you to surrender that need for certainty?

The General Ruling and Its Refutation of an Objection
Sanusi defines the general ruling and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what a general ruling is in kalam and how it refutes the objection that lack of moral perception necessitates suspension.

Core Idea
General rulings apply to actions whose moral aspect reason cannot perceive.

Sanusi clarifies a subtle distinction: when an action is considered in its specific details, no ruling can be made because reason sees no moral quality. But when considered under the general description of being rationally unknown, a general ruling such as permissibility or prohibition can be applied. This lesson breaks down that concept and shows how it answers a key objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two perspectives
Read the passage and note the difference between considering an action in its specific particularities versus under the general description of being rationally unknown.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the general ruling.
2
Examine the analogy
The author uses the example of knowing that every believer is in paradise but suspending judgment on a specific individual. Understand how this illustrates the general ruling.
3
Understand the refutation
The passage refutes the claim that lack of perception leads only to suspension, not to a definite ruling like hazr or ibaha. See how the general ruling solves this.
This is the key insight of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما على سبيل الإجمال حاصل كلامه أنه إذا لوحظ خصوصيات تلك الأفعال لم يحكم فيها بحكم خاص لعدم إدراك جهة تقتضيه وأما إذا لوحظت بهذا العنوان أعني بكونها مما لا يدرك بالعقل جهة حسنها وقبحها يحكم فيها بهذا وهذا هو معنى الحكم على سبيل الإجمال، وبالجملة لا شك في اختلاف الأحكام باختلاف العنوان فيجوز أن لا يدرك جهة حسن فعل أو قبحه إذا لوحظ بخصوصه، فيتوقف حكمه ويدرك جهة واحدة منهما إذا لوحظ بالعنوان المذكور، وهذا كالحكم بأن كل مؤمن في الجنة وكل كافر في النار مع التوقف في المعين منهما، وبهذا التقرير اندفع ما قيل : عدم إدراك الجهة يقتضي التوقف فكيف قيل بالحظر أو الإباحة، فتأمل. الثمار المطعومة وغيرها ) فالحكمة تقتضي إباحته ) أي إباحة الانتفاع وإلا كان حقه عبثاً ( وكيف يدرك تحريمه بالعقل وما هو إلا كمن يغترف غرفة من بحر لا ينزف ليدفع به عطشه المهلك. أترى العقل يحكم بمنع إكرام الأكرمين منه وتكليفه التعرض للهلاك ؟ كلا والجواب ربما خلقه ليصبر عنه ويمنع هواه وشهوته ( فيثاب ) على ذلك وهذه منفعة جليلة ( أو ) خلقه ( لغرض آخر لا نعلمه وأما التوقف فيفسر تارة بعدم الحكم ومرجعه الإباحة إذ ما لا منع فمباح إلا أن يشترط في الإباحة ( الإذن فيرجع إلى كونه ) حكماً ( شرعياً ) لا عقلياً وكلامنا فيه وإنما يتجه هذا اشترط إذن الشارع لا إذن العقل وربما يقال : هذا التفسير جزم بعدم الحكم لا توقف إلا أن يراد توقف العقل عن الحكم (و) يفسر ) تارة بعدم العلم ) أي هناك حظر أو إباحة لكنا لا نعلمه ( وهذا أمثل ) من التفسير الأول المشتمل على نوع تكلف في معنى التوقف كما عرفت لكن عدم العلم ( لا) لتعارض (الأدلة إذ قد تبين بطلانها بل لعدم الدليل ) على أحد هذين الحكمين بعينه
His saying: (As for the general view), the gist of his speech is that when the particularities of those actions are considered, no specific ruling is made due to lack of perception of the requisite aspect; but when they are considered under this description—namely, being such that reason does not perceive the aspect of their goodness or badness—then a ruling is made by this, and this is the meaning of the general ruling. In summary, there is no doubt that rulings differ according to differences in description; thus it is possible that the aspect of goodness or badness of an action is not perceived when considered specifically, so its ruling is suspended, but one aspect is perceived when considered under the mentioned description. This is like the ruling that every believer is in paradise and every disbeliever is in hell, with suspension regarding a particular individual. By this explanation, what was said—'lack of perception of the aspect necessitates suspension, so how can hazr or ibaha be asserted?'—is refuted. Reflect. (Eatable fruits and other things) then wisdom necessitates its permission, i.e., permission of benefit, otherwise His right would be futile. (And how can its prohibition be perceived by reason? It is only like one who takes a handful from a sea that is not depleted to quench his fatal thirst. Do you think reason would forbid the most generous from giving and impose upon him exposure to destruction? No.) And the answer: perhaps He created it so that he might exercise patience and restrain his desire and lust, and thus be rewarded for that, and this is a great benefit; or He created it for another purpose we do not know. As for suspension (tawaqquf), it is interpreted once as absence of ruling, and its referent is permission, because what is not prohibited is permissible—unless permission requires authorization, in which case it reverts to being a revealed ruling, not rational. And our discussion here concerns this only if the authorization of the Lawgiver is required, not the intellect. And it may be said: this interpretation is a decisive statement of no ruling, not suspension, unless what is meant is the intellect's suspension from ruling. (And) it is interpreted (another time as lack of knowledge), i.e., there is either prohibition or permission but we do not know it. (And this is more appropriate) than the first interpretation, which involves a kind of strain in the meaning of suspension as you have seen. However, lack of knowledge is not due to conflicting proofs—since their falsehood has been shown—but rather due to lack of proof for either of these two specific rulings.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage explains the general ruling for actions whose moral aspect is not known by reason. The author first clarifies the meaning of 'general ruling' (al-hukm 'ala sabīl al-ijmāl): when actions are considered under the description of being rationally unknown, a general ruling applies. He uses the analogy of knowing that every believer enters paradise but suspending judgment on a specific individual. The text then presents the argument for ibaha (permissibility) based on the example of fruits: wisdom demands permission, and it asks rhetorically how reason could forbid taking a handful from a vast sea to quench thirst. The answer is that God may have created it for the servant to resist desire and gain reward, or for another unknown purpose. Then the discussion turns to tawaqquf (suspension) with two interpretations: (1) absence of ruling, which reduces to permission because what is not prohibited is permissible—but this requires that the Lawgiver's authorization is needed, making it a revealed ruling; (2) lack of knowledge, meaning we do not know whether it is prohibited or permitted. The second interpretation is deemed more appropriate, as the first strains the meaning of suspension. It notes that this lack of knowledge is not due to conflicting proofs (which are invalid) but due to absence of proof. This completes the refutation of the claim that lack of perception necessitates suspension but not hazr or ibaha.

Key Terms
الحكم الإجمالي
التوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that if reason cannot perceive the moral quality of an action, it must suspend judgment and cannot assert any definite ruling like hazr or ibaha.
Correction: The passage shows that a general ruling (al-hukm al-ijmali) is still possible, based on the category of the action, even when specific moral quality is unknown.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason no specific ruling is made when an action is considered with its particularities?
A) Because the action is inherently ambiguous.
B) Because reason does not perceive the requisite aspect of goodness or badness.
C) Because the action is prohibited by default.
D) Because the action is always permissible.

Key Takeaway
The general ruling allows a definite judgment on a class of actions even when the specific moral quality is unknown to reason.

The Rational Argument for Permissibility (Ibaha)
Sanusi presents the rational argument for ibaha and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for ibaha based on wisdom and the possible answers to the objection that God may have created things for other purposes.

Core Idea
Wisdom demands that beneficial things are permissible unless prohibited.

The author presents a strong argument for ibaha using the example of eatable fruits: since God created them for benefit, reason sees permission as the default. The lesson also addresses a possible objection that God might have created the fruit for the sake of testing patience or some unknown purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the fruit example
Read the passage about eatable fruits and the rhetorical question about taking a handful from a vast sea. Understand the argument from wisdom.
The argument is that denying permission would be futile (عبث).
2
Consider the objection and answers
The text gives two possible answers to why God might have created something without immediate permission: for patience and reward, or for an unknown purpose.
These answers show that the argument for ibaha is not absolute but can be countered.
3
Evaluate the strength
Reflect on whether the rational argument for ibaha is conclusive or if it requires revealed knowledge.
The passage does not settle the issue but presents the debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما على سبيل الإجمال حاصل كلامه أنه إذا لوحظ خصوصيات تلك الأفعال لم يحكم فيها بحكم خاص لعدم إدراك جهة تقتضيه وأما إذا لوحظت بهذا العنوان أعني بكونها مما لا يدرك بالعقل جهة حسنها وقبحها يحكم فيها بهذا وهذا هو معنى الحكم على سبيل الإجمال، وبالجملة لا شك في اختلاف الأحكام باختلاف العنوان فيجوز أن لا يدرك جهة حسن فعل أو قبحه إذا لوحظ بخصوصه، فيتوقف حكمه ويدرك جهة واحدة منهما إذا لوحظ بالعنوان المذكور، وهذا كالحكم بأن كل مؤمن في الجنة وكل كافر في النار مع التوقف في المعين منهما، وبهذا التقرير اندفع ما قيل : عدم إدراك الجهة يقتضي التوقف فكيف قيل بالحظر أو الإباحة، فتأمل. الثمار المطعومة وغيرها ) فالحكمة تقتضي إباحته ) أي إباحة الانتفاع وإلا كان حقه عبثاً ( وكيف يدرك تحريمه بالعقل وما هو إلا كمن يغترف غرفة من بحر لا ينزف ليدفع به عطشه المهلك. أترى العقل يحكم بمنع إكرام الأكرمين منه وتكليفه التعرض للهلاك ؟ كلا والجواب ربما خلقه ليصبر عنه ويمنع هواه وشهوته ( فيثاب ) على ذلك وهذه منفعة جليلة ( أو ) خلقه ( لغرض آخر لا نعلمه وأما التوقف فيفسر تارة بعدم الحكم ومرجعه الإباحة إذ ما لا منع فمباح إلا أن يشترط في الإباحة ( الإذن فيرجع إلى كونه ) حكماً ( شرعياً ) لا عقلياً وكلامنا فيه وإنما يتجه هذا اشترط إذن الشارع لا إذن العقل وربما يقال : هذا التفسير جزم بعدم الحكم لا توقف إلا أن يراد توقف العقل عن الحكم (و) يفسر ) تارة بعدم العلم ) أي هناك حظر أو إباحة لكنا لا نعلمه ( وهذا أمثل ) من التفسير الأول المشتمل على نوع تكلف في معنى التوقف كما عرفت لكن عدم العلم ( لا) لتعارض (الأدلة إذ قد تبين بطلانها بل لعدم الدليل ) على أحد هذين الحكمين بعينه
His saying: (As for the general view), the gist of his speech is that when the particularities of those actions are considered, no specific ruling is made due to lack of perception of the requisite aspect; but when they are considered under this description—namely, being such that reason does not perceive the aspect of their goodness or badness—then a ruling is made by this, and this is the meaning of the general ruling. In summary, there is no doubt that rulings differ according to differences in description; thus it is possible that the aspect of goodness or badness of an action is not perceived when considered specifically, so its ruling is suspended, but one aspect is perceived when considered under the mentioned description. This is like the ruling that every believer is in paradise and every disbeliever is in hell, with suspension regarding a particular individual. By this explanation, what was said—'lack of perception of the aspect necessitates suspension, so how can hazr or ibaha be asserted?'—is refuted. Reflect. (Eatable fruits and other things) then wisdom necessitates its permission, i.e., permission of benefit, otherwise His right would be futile. (And how can its prohibition be perceived by reason? It is only like one who takes a handful from a sea that is not depleted to quench his fatal thirst. Do you think reason would forbid the most generous from giving and impose upon him exposure to destruction? No.) And the answer: perhaps He created it so that he might exercise patience and restrain his desire and lust, and thus be rewarded for that, and this is a great benefit; or He created it for another purpose we do not know. As for suspension (tawaqquf), it is interpreted once as absence of ruling, and its referent is permission, because what is not prohibited is permissible—unless permission requires authorization, in which case it reverts to being a revealed ruling, not rational. And our discussion here concerns this only if the authorization of the Lawgiver is required, not the intellect. And it may be said: this interpretation is a decisive statement of no ruling, not suspension, unless what is meant is the intellect's suspension from ruling. (And) it is interpreted (another time as lack of knowledge), i.e., there is either prohibition or permission but we do not know it. (And this is more appropriate) than the first interpretation, which involves a kind of strain in the meaning of suspension as you have seen. However, lack of knowledge is not due to conflicting proofs—since their falsehood has been shown—but rather due to lack of proof for either of these two specific rulings.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage explains the general ruling for actions whose moral aspect is not known by reason. The author first clarifies the meaning of 'general ruling' (al-hukm 'ala sabīl al-ijmāl): when actions are considered under the description of being rationally unknown, a general ruling applies. He uses the analogy of knowing that every believer enters paradise but suspending judgment on a specific individual. The text then presents the argument for ibaha (permissibility) based on the example of fruits: wisdom demands permission, and it asks rhetorically how reason could forbid taking a handful from a vast sea to quench thirst. The answer is that God may have created it for the servant to resist desire and gain reward, or for another unknown purpose. Then the discussion turns to tawaqquf (suspension) with two interpretations: (1) absence of ruling, which reduces to permission because what is not prohibited is permissible—but this requires that the Lawgiver's authorization is needed, making it a revealed ruling; (2) lack of knowledge, meaning we do not know whether it is prohibited or permitted. The second interpretation is deemed more appropriate, as the first strains the meaning of suspension. It notes that this lack of knowledge is not due to conflicting proofs (which are invalid) but due to absence of proof. This completes the refutation of the claim that lack of perception necessitates suspension but not hazr or ibaha.

Key Terms
الإباحة
الحكمة
العبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the rational argument for ibaha is absolutely conclusive and that any prohibition must be irrational.
Correction: The passage shows that the argument can be countered by considering possible divine purposes like testing patience, so revealed knowledge may be needed.

Checkpoint
What is the analogy used in the passage to support the argument for ibaha?
A) A king giving a gift to his subject.
B) A person taking a handful of water from an inexhaustible sea.
C) A farmer harvesting his crops.
D) A scholar writing a book.

Key Takeaway
The rational argument for ibaha is based on wisdom, but it is not conclusive because God may have other purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with a clear benefit, do you instinctively assume it is permissible? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What does it reveal about your need for divine guidance over your own reasoning?
?
The analogy of the thirsty person suggests reason would never forbid a necessary benefit. Yet are there areas where your desires convince you something is a 'necessary benefit' when it might actually be a test? How can you discern the difference in your daily life?
?
Sanusi shows that even a rational argument can be weakened by considering God's hidden purposes. How does this affect your confidence in your own reasoning about religious matters? What specific step will you take to humble your intellect before revelation starting today?

The Two Interpretations of Tawaqquf (Suspension)
Sanusi analyzes two meanings of tawaqquf and defends the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of tawaqquf (suspension) and why the second interpretation (lack of knowledge) is preferred.

Core Idea
Suspension of judgment either means no ruling (which collapses into permission) or lack of knowledge.

The term 'tawaqquf' can be ambiguous. The author distinguishes two interpretations: one that reduces to permission and one that simply admits ignorance. The lesson explains why the second interpretation is more accurate and how it differs from the objection based on conflicting proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
The passage lists two interpretations of tawaqquf: (1) absence of ruling, and (2) lack of knowledge. Read carefully.
Notice that the first interpretation implies that no ruling exists, which the author argues collapses into ibaha.
2
Understand the problem with the first interpretation
If tawaqquf means 'no ruling', then whatever is not prohibited is considered permissible, unless authorization is required. This makes tawaqquf essentially ibaha.
The author says this interpretation involves a strain (تكلف).
3
Appreciate the second interpretation
The second interpretation, 'lack of knowledge', means we know there is either prohibition or permission, but we do not know which. This is more appropriate because it maintains suspension of judgment.
The author emphasizes that this lack of knowledge is not due to conflicting proofs but due to absence of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما على سبيل الإجمال حاصل كلامه أنه إذا لوحظ خصوصيات تلك الأفعال لم يحكم فيها بحكم خاص لعدم إدراك جهة تقتضيه وأما إذا لوحظت بهذا العنوان أعني بكونها مما لا يدرك بالعقل جهة حسنها وقبحها يحكم فيها بهذا وهذا هو معنى الحكم على سبيل الإجمال، وبالجملة لا شك في اختلاف الأحكام باختلاف العنوان فيجوز أن لا يدرك جهة حسن فعل أو قبحه إذا لوحظ بخصوصه، فيتوقف حكمه ويدرك جهة واحدة منهما إذا لوحظ بالعنوان المذكور، وهذا كالحكم بأن كل مؤمن في الجنة وكل كافر في النار مع التوقف في المعين منهما، وبهذا التقرير اندفع ما قيل : عدم إدراك الجهة يقتضي التوقف فكيف قيل بالحظر أو الإباحة، فتأمل. الثمار المطعومة وغيرها ) فالحكمة تقتضي إباحته ) أي إباحة الانتفاع وإلا كان حقه عبثاً ( وكيف يدرك تحريمه بالعقل وما هو إلا كمن يغترف غرفة من بحر لا ينزف ليدفع به عطشه المهلك. أترى العقل يحكم بمنع إكرام الأكرمين منه وتكليفه التعرض للهلاك ؟ كلا والجواب ربما خلقه ليصبر عنه ويمنع هواه وشهوته ( فيثاب ) على ذلك وهذه منفعة جليلة ( أو ) خلقه ( لغرض آخر لا نعلمه وأما التوقف فيفسر تارة بعدم الحكم ومرجعه الإباحة إذ ما لا منع فمباح إلا أن يشترط في الإباحة ( الإذن فيرجع إلى كونه ) حكماً ( شرعياً ) لا عقلياً وكلامنا فيه وإنما يتجه هذا اشترط إذن الشارع لا إذن العقل وربما يقال : هذا التفسير جزم بعدم الحكم لا توقف إلا أن يراد توقف العقل عن الحكم (و) يفسر ) تارة بعدم العلم ) أي هناك حظر أو إباحة لكنا لا نعلمه ( وهذا أمثل ) من التفسير الأول المشتمل على نوع تكلف في معنى التوقف كما عرفت لكن عدم العلم ( لا) لتعارض (الأدلة إذ قد تبين بطلانها بل لعدم الدليل ) على أحد هذين الحكمين بعينه
His saying: (As for the general view), the gist of his speech is that when the particularities of those actions are considered, no specific ruling is made due to lack of perception of the requisite aspect; but when they are considered under this description—namely, being such that reason does not perceive the aspect of their goodness or badness—then a ruling is made by this, and this is the meaning of the general ruling. In summary, there is no doubt that rulings differ according to differences in description; thus it is possible that the aspect of goodness or badness of an action is not perceived when considered specifically, so its ruling is suspended, but one aspect is perceived when considered under the mentioned description. This is like the ruling that every believer is in paradise and every disbeliever is in hell, with suspension regarding a particular individual. By this explanation, what was said—'lack of perception of the aspect necessitates suspension, so how can hazr or ibaha be asserted?'—is refuted. Reflect. (Eatable fruits and other things) then wisdom necessitates its permission, i.e., permission of benefit, otherwise His right would be futile. (And how can its prohibition be perceived by reason? It is only like one who takes a handful from a sea that is not depleted to quench his fatal thirst. Do you think reason would forbid the most generous from giving and impose upon him exposure to destruction? No.) And the answer: perhaps He created it so that he might exercise patience and restrain his desire and lust, and thus be rewarded for that, and this is a great benefit; or He created it for another purpose we do not know. As for suspension (tawaqquf), it is interpreted once as absence of ruling, and its referent is permission, because what is not prohibited is permissible—unless permission requires authorization, in which case it reverts to being a revealed ruling, not rational. And our discussion here concerns this only if the authorization of the Lawgiver is required, not the intellect. And it may be said: this interpretation is a decisive statement of no ruling, not suspension, unless what is meant is the intellect's suspension from ruling. (And) it is interpreted (another time as lack of knowledge), i.e., there is either prohibition or permission but we do not know it. (And this is more appropriate) than the first interpretation, which involves a kind of strain in the meaning of suspension as you have seen. However, lack of knowledge is not due to conflicting proofs—since their falsehood has been shown—but rather due to lack of proof for either of these two specific rulings.

Misconception: Some think that tawaqquf simply means no ruling exists, and therefore the action is permissible by default.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the second interpretation of tawaqquf (lack of knowledge) considered more appropriate than the first (absence of ruling)?
A) Because the first interpretation contradicts revelation.
B) Because the first interpretation involves a kind of strain in the meaning of suspension.
C) Because the second interpretation is based on conflicting proofs.
D) Because the second interpretation is easier to understand.

Tawaqquf properly understood is not the absence of a ruling but our lack of knowledge about the actual ruling due to absence of proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a religious issue for which you have no clear evidence, do you honestly admit your ignorance or do you rush to declare it permissible or forbidden based on weak reasoning? This lesson forces you to examine whether your 'suspension' is genuine humility or a hidden opinion.
?
The first interpretation of tawaqquf collapses into permission—effectively saying 'if it's not proven haram, it's halal.' Have you ever used this logic to justify something you wanted to do, even though you suspected it might be wrong? What would it cost you to say, 'I do not know'?
?
Lack of knowledge here is due to absence of proof, not equal proofs. How often do you pretend that two opposing views are equally valid when in reality you simply haven't sought the evidence? Will you commit to actually searching for proof instead of staying comfortable in ambiguity?

Two Interpretations of Tawaqquf
Explains two interpretations of tawaqquf and prefers lack of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) regarding divine acts and why the second interpretation is preferred.

Core Idea
Tawaqquf is either lack of judgment (ibaha) or lack of knowledge; the latter is preferred.

After discussing rational judgments, the author turns to the concept of tawaqquf—suspension of judgment—concerning divine acts. He clarifies that this term can be understood in two distinct ways, each with different implications for legal rulings. This passage explains both interpretations and argues for the superiority of the second.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question and analogy of the inexhaustible sea.
2
Identify the first interpretation
The passage presents 'tawaqquf interpreted as lack of judgment (adam al-hukm).' Notice how this leads to permissibility (ibaha) and requires legal permission.
This interpretation is criticized as being definitive rather than suspended.
3
Identify the second interpretation
The passage then presents 'tawaqquf interpreted as lack of knowledge (adam al-ilm).' This means we do not know whether there is prohibition or permission.
The author says this interpretation is 'more appropriate' (amthal).
4
Compare the two interpretations
Contrast the two: the first is a rational judgment of no ruling (which reduces to ibaha), the second is acknowledging ignorance of the divine ruling.
The second avoids the affectation (takalluf) of the first.
5
Reflect on the commentary
Read the commentary notes, especially the refutation of al-Taftazani's objection. Note how the author defends the second interpretation.
This reinforces why lack of knowledge is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيف يدرك تحريمه بالعقل وما هو إلا كمن يغترف غرفة من بحر لا ينزف ليدفع به عطشه المهلك. أترى العقل يحكم بمنع إكرام الأكرمين منه وتكليفه التعرض للهلاك ؟ كلا والجواب ربما خلقه ليصبر عنه ويمنع هواه وشهوته ( فيثاب ) على ذلك وهذه منفعة جليلة ( أو ) خلقه ( لغرض آخر لا نعلمه وأما التوقف فيفسر تارة بعدم الحكم ومرجعه الإباحة إذ ما لا منع فمباح إلا أن يشترط في الإباحة ( الإذن فيرجع إلى كونه ) حكماً ( شرعياً ) لا عقلياً وكلامنا فيه وإنما يتجه هذا اشترط إذن الشارع لا إذن العقل وربما يقال : هذا التفسير جزم بعدم الحكم لا توقف إلا أن يراد توقف العقل عن الحكم (و) يفسر ) تارة بعدم العلم ) أي هناك حظر أو إباحة لكنا لا نعلمه ( وهذا أمثل ) من التفسير الأول المشتمل على نوع تكلف في معنى التوقف كما عرفت لكن عدم العلم ( لا) لتعارض (الأدلة إذ قد تبين بطلانها بل لعدم الدليل ) على أحد هذين الحكمين بعينه

قوله : ( من بحر لا ينزف ) أي لا ينقص من نزف السرداب أي ذهب ماؤها .
قوله : ( لغرض آخر هذا على تقدير أن يكون أفعال الله تعالى معللة بالأغراض.
قوله : (أي هناك خطراً وإباحة لكنا لا نعلمه فيه دفع لما ذكره المحقق التفتازاني في التلويح من أن تفسير التوقف بعدم العلم بأن فيه حكم الله أم لا باطل لأنا نعلم قطعاً أن الله تعالى في كل فعل حكماً إما بالمنع أو بعدمه.
And how can its prohibition be perceived by reason? It is only like one who scoops a handful from an inexhaustible sea to quench his deadly thirst. Do you think reason rules to prevent honoring the most honorable of them and to burden him with exposure to destruction? No. The answer is: perhaps He created it so that he may be patient against it and restrain his desire and lust, and thus be rewarded for that; this is a great benefit. Or He created it for another purpose that we do not know. As for suspension (tawaqquf), it is sometimes interpreted as lack of judgment, and its outcome is permissibility (ibaha), since what is not prohibited is permissible, unless permissibility requires permission, in which case it becomes a legal ruling, not a rational one, and our discussion concerns it. This applies only if the permission of the Lawgiver is required, not the permission of reason. It may be said: this interpretation is a definitive judgment of no ruling, not suspension, unless what is meant is the suspension of reason's judgment. And it is sometimes interpreted as lack of knowledge, i.e., there is either prohibition or permission but we do not know it. This is more appropriate than the first interpretation, which involves a kind of affectation in the meaning of suspension, as you have seen. But the lack of knowledge is not due to conflicting proofs, for their invalidity has been shown, but rather due to the absence of proof for one of these two specific rulings. His saying: (from an inexhaustible sea) i.e., not diminishing; from 'nazafa al-sardab' i.e., its water is gone. His saying: (for another purpose) this is on the assumption that the actions of Allah are caused by purposes. His saying: (i.e., there is prohibition or permission but we do not know it) in it is a refutation of what al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani mentioned in al-Talwih, that the interpretation of suspension as lack of knowledge of whether there is a ruling of Allah or not is invalid, because we know with certainty that Allah has a ruling for every act, either prohibition or its absence.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) regarding the ruling on divine acts. It begins with a rhetorical question criticizing rational perception of prohibition, using an analogy of a person scooping from an inexhaustible sea. The author then presents two interpretations of tawaqquf: first, as no rational judgment, which reduces to ibaha (permissibility) and requires a legal permission; second, as lack of knowledge of the actual ruling, which is deemed more appropriate. The commentary notes clarify key points: the sea analogy implies inexhaustibility; the second purpose presumes divine acts have purposes; and the second interpretation is defended against al-Taftazani's objection that we cannot lack knowledge of Allah's ruling.

Correction: The passage clarifies that tawaqquf is better understood as lack of knowledge of the ruling, not absence of ruling. Allah has a ruling for every act, but we may not know it.

B) Lack of knowledge (adam al-ilm)

Key Takeaway
Tawaqquf is best understood as lack of knowledge of the divine ruling, not as a definitive judgment of permissibility.

?

What would it cost you to sincerely say 'I do not know' and wait for clarity?

How does this affect your relationship with Allah and your sense of dependence on Him?
?
The author prefers lack of knowledge over pretending to know. How does your pride resist admitting ignorance about the rulings of Allah? The Mu'tazili Argument: Punishment Must Be Obligatory
Al-Jurjani presents the Mu'tazili argument for obligatory punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that punishment for sin must be obligatory to avoid equalization between the obedient and disobedient and to prevent implicit encouragement of sin.

Core Idea
Punishment must be certain, or God would treat the obedient and disobedient equally.

Before we evaluate the Mu'tazili position, we must hear it clearly. This passage presents their rational argument that God is obliged to punish sin, drawing on analogies from human justice and the psychology of deterrence. Notice how the argument rests on two claims: that leaving punishment equalizes the obedient and disobedient, and that it gives implicit permission for sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the Mu'tazili argument. Note that it starts with 'The third: among those matters is...' and ends with '...ugly, impossible from the Exalted.'
The argument is part of a larger discussion on 'rational judgments'.
2
Identify the two reasons
The argument gives two reasons why leaving punishment is ugly: first, it equalizes the obedient and disobedient; second, it amounts to permission and encouragement for sin. Find each in the text.
3
Understand the analogy
Notice that the argument uses an analogy from human masters with two slaves. This is meant to show that any rational being would judge equal treatment of obedient and disobedient as ugly.
This is a key example of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the witnessed to the unseen).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : ( من تلك الأمور ( العقاب على المعصية زجراً عنها فإن في تركه التسوية بين المطيع والعاصي ( وهو قبيح كما في الشاهد إذا كان له عبدان : مطيع وعاص ( وفيه ) أي في تركه أيضاً ( إذن للعصاة في المعصية وإغراء لهم بها ) وذلك لأنه تعالى ركب فيهم شهوة القبائح، فلو لم يجزم المكلف بأنه يستحق على ارتكاب القبيح عقاباً لا يجوز الإخلال به بل جوز ترك العقاب لكان ذلك إذناً من الله سبحانه وتعالى للعصاة في ارتكاب الشهوات بل إغراء بها، وهو قبيح يستحيل صدوره من تعالى ( فيقال لهم : العقاب حقه والإسقاط فضل فكيف يدرك امتناعه بالعقل) وترك العقاب لا يستلزم التسوية فإن المطيع مثاب دون العاصي . ( وحديث الإذن والإغراء مع رجحان ظن العقاب بمجرد تجويز مرجوح ضعيف جدا) يعني أنه ليس يلزم من جواز ترك العقاب على المعصية إذن وإغراء وإنما يلزم ذلك إذا لم يكن ظن العقاب رجحان على تركه إذ مع رجحانه لا يلزم من مجرد تجويز تركه تجويزاً مرجوحاً الإذن والإغراء. كما أن جواز تركه بل وجوبه على تقدير إثابته التي يمكن صدورها عنه لا يستلزمهما .
The third: Among those matters is [the idea that] punishment for sin is [obligatory] as a deterrent, for in abandoning it there is equalization between the obedient and the disobedient, which is ugly, as in the witnessed [world] when a man has two slaves: obedient and disobedient. And in it (i.e., in abandoning it) also there is permission for sinners in sin and encouragement for them to it. This is because He, exalted, has ingrained in them desire for ugly deeds. So if the responsible person were not certain that he deserves for committing the ugly [deed] a punishment whose omission is not permissible, but rather permitted the omission of punishment, that would be permission from God, glorified and exalted, for sinners to indulge desires, indeed encouragement to them, and that is ugly, impossible from the Exalted. Then it is said to them: Punishment is His right, and forgiveness is a favor; so how can its impossibility be grasped by reason? And abandoning punishment does not entail equalization, for the obedient is rewarded without the disobedient. And the talk of permission and encouragement, given the preponderance of the expectation of punishment, with mere permitting of a preponderated [possibility], is very weak. Meaning that the permissibility of abandoning punishment for sin does not entail permission and encouragement; rather, that is entailed only when the expectation of punishment is not preponderant over its abandonment, for with its preponderance, mere permitting of its abandonment as a preponderated possibility does not entail permission and encouragement. Likewise, the permissibility of abandoning it, indeed its obligation on the assumption of rewarding him (which can issue from Him), does not entail them both.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the third Mu'tazili argument for the obligation of punishment on God, followed by a refutation. The argument states that punishment for sin is wajib as a deterrent because leaving it would equate the obedient and disobedient, which is qabih (evil) by human analogy. Moreover, if the responsible person (mukallaf) is not certain that punishment is inevitable, then allowing the possibility of escaping punishment would amount to divine permission and encouragement of sin—also qabih and impossible for God. The refutation responds: punishment is a right (haqq) of God, and forgiving is a favor (fadl); reason cannot dictate the impossibility of forgiveness. Abandoning punishment does not necessitate equalization since the obedient receive reward (thawab) while the disobedient do not. Furthermore, the claim that permission and encouragement follow from mere possibility is weak, because if the expectation of punishment is preponderant (rajan), then a remote possibility does not constitute permission or encouragement. Even if punishment were obligatory on a certain assumption, that does not imply enticement.

Key Terms
العقاب
المعصية
التسوية
القبيح
الإذن
الإغراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazili argument is purely theological and not based on reason.
Correction: In fact, the argument uses rational analogies from human justice (the case of two slaves) and psychological deterrence to claim that God must punish.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazili argument, what is the first reason why leaving punishment is ugly?
A) It would be an injustice to the disobedient.
B) It would equalize the obedient and disobedient.
C) It would deprive God of His right.
D) It would make God seem weak.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili argue that unless punishment is obligatory, God would be guilty of both unequal treatment and tacit encouragement of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew for certain that a particular sin would not be punished by God, would you be more likely to commit it? The Mu'tazili argument says yes, because uncertainty gives permission. Is that true of your own heart? What does your reaction reveal about your relationship with God?
?
The argument uses the analogy of a master and two slaves. Do you view God's relationship with you as that of a master whose commands require enforcement? Or does this analogy feel too transactional? How does your actual view affect the way you obey or disobey?
?
This passage claims that leaving punishment is 'ugly' (qabih) and impossible for God. Do you ever assume that God's forgiveness means He will not punish you for ongoing sin? How does this argument challenge that assumption, and what must you change today?

Refuting the Mu'tazili Argument: Punishment is a Right, Not an Obligation
Al-Jurjani presents the Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazili argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni response that punishment is God's right, forgiveness is a favor, and that the Mu'tazili claims of equalization and encouragement are unfounded.

Core Idea
Punishment is God's right; forgiveness is a favor beyond reason's dictate.

Having heard the Mu'tazili argument, we now turn to the Sunni refutation as presented by al-Jurjani. The response dismantles each point: first, that reason cannot mandate punishment because it is a divine right; second, that equalization is avoided through rewards; third, that encouragement requires certainty, not mere possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the passage starting from 'فيقال لهم' to the end. Note the three main counterarguments.
2
Identify the first counterargument
The text says punishment is a right (haqq) of God and forgiveness is a favor (fadl). This means reason cannot declare punishment obligatory.
3
Identify the second counterargument
The text denies that leaving punishment equalizes the obedient and disobedient, because the obedient receives reward (thawab) while the disobedient does not.
4
Analyze the third counterargument
The text argues that permission/encouragement would only follow from certainty, not from a remote possibility that punishment might be omitted, especially when the expectation (zann) of punishment is preponderant.
This is a key point: a remote possibility does not amount to permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : ( من تلك الأمور ( العقاب على المعصية زجراً عنها فإن في تركه التسوية بين المطيع والعاصي ( وهو قبيح كما في الشاهد إذا كان له عبدان : مطيع وعاص ( وفيه ) أي في تركه أيضاً ( إذن للعصاة في المعصية وإغراء لهم بها ) وذلك لأنه تعالى ركب فيهم شهوة القبائح، فلو لم يجزم المكلف بأنه يستحق على ارتكاب القبيح عقاباً لا يجوز الإخلال به بل جوز ترك العقاب لكان ذلك إذناً من الله سبحانه وتعالى للعصاة في ارتكاب الشهوات بل إغراء بها، وهو قبيح يستحيل صدوره من تعالى ( فيقال لهم : العقاب حقه والإسقاط فضل فكيف يدرك امتناعه بالعقل) وترك العقاب لا يستلزم التسوية فإن المطيع مثاب دون العاصي . ( وحديث الإذن والإغراء مع رجحان ظن العقاب بمجرد تجويز مرجوح ضعيف جدا) يعني أنه ليس يلزم من جواز ترك العقاب على المعصية إذن وإغراء وإنما يلزم ذلك إذا لم يكن ظن العقاب رجحان على تركه إذ مع رجحانه لا يلزم من مجرد تجويز تركه تجويزاً مرجوحاً الإذن والإغراء. كما أن جواز تركه بل وجوبه على تقدير إثابته التي يمكن صدورها عنه لا يستلزمهما .
The third: Among those matters is [the idea that] punishment for sin is [obligatory] as a deterrent, for in abandoning it there is equalization between the obedient and the disobedient, which is ugly, as in the witnessed [world] when a man has two slaves: obedient and disobedient. And in it (i.e., in abandoning it) also there is permission for sinners in sin and encouragement for them to it. This is because He, exalted, has ingrained in them desire for ugly deeds. So if the responsible person were not certain that he deserves for committing the ugly [deed] a punishment whose omission is not permissible, but rather permitted the omission of punishment, that would be permission from God, glorified and exalted, for sinners to indulge desires, indeed encouragement to them, and that is ugly, impossible from the Exalted. Then it is said to them: Punishment is His right, and forgiveness is a favor; so how can its impossibility be grasped by reason? And abandoning punishment does not entail equalization, for the obedient is rewarded without the disobedient. And the talk of permission and encouragement, given the preponderance of the expectation of punishment, with mere permitting of a preponderated [possibility], is very weak. Meaning that the permissibility of abandoning punishment for sin does not entail permission and encouragement; rather, that is entailed only when the expectation of punishment is not preponderant over its abandonment, for with its preponderance, mere permitting of its abandonment as a preponderated possibility does not entail permission and encouragement. Likewise, the permissibility of abandoning it, indeed its obligation on the assumption of rewarding him (which can issue from Him), does not entail them both.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the third Mu'tazili argument for the obligation of punishment on God, followed by a refutation. The argument states that punishment for sin is wajib as a deterrent because leaving it would equate the obedient and disobedient, which is qabih (evil) by human analogy. Moreover, if the responsible person (mukallaf) is not certain that punishment is inevitable, then allowing the possibility of escaping punishment would amount to divine permission and encouragement of sin—also qabih and impossible for God. The refutation responds: punishment is a right (haqq) of God, and forgiving is a favor (fadl); reason cannot dictate the impossibility of forgiveness. Abandoning punishment does not necessitate equalization since the obedient receive reward (thawab) while the disobedient do not. Furthermore, the claim that permission and encouragement follow from mere possibility is weak, because if the expectation of punishment is preponderant (rajan), then a remote possibility does not constitute permission or encouragement. Even if punishment were obligatory on a certain assumption, that does not imply enticement.

Key Terms
الحق
الفضل
الثواب
الظن
المرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God forgives sin, then obedience is meaningless.
Correction: The refutation shows that the obedient receive reward, maintaining a distinction even if punishment is forgiven.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does leaving punishment not entail permission and encouragement?
A) Because punishment is always certain.
B) Because the expectation of punishment is preponderant.
C) Because encouragement only comes from explicit statements.
D) Because God never forgives.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response affirms that punishment is God's right, not a rational necessity, and that the Mu'tazili's concerns about equalization and encouragement are unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you commit a sin, do you fear God's punishment because you see it as a necessary consequence, or do you fear it because you recognize it is His right? How does that difference affect the humility of your repentance?
?
When you hear that God could forgive you despite your sins, does that thought ever give you a sense of permission to sin? What does your answer reveal about the strength of your preponderant expectation of punishment?
?
How does the teaching that punishment is God's right and forgiveness is a favor change the way you view your own sins? Will you now treat both His justice and His mercy with greater awe, or will you take one for granted?

The Mu'tazili Principle of Compensation for Pains
Al-Jurjani expounds the Mu'tazili rule for when compensation for pain is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which the Mu'tazila held that compensation for pain is obligatory and how they defined compensation.

Core Idea
Compensation is owed for pain that is not punishment, and it must be equivalent or greater.

The Mu'tazila argued that any pain not deserved as punishment must be compensated. This lesson explains the scenarios they envisioned: when the inflicter is a responsible agent and when God Himself is the cause. The definition of compensation as a deserved benefit free from veneration sets it apart from reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule for compensation
Read the passage from 'الخامس' to 'لا عما فوقه' and identify the conditions under which compensation is required.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا ينقص عن إيلامه' which means no less.
2
Understand when compensation is not required
Note that pain which is a punishment for sin (like hudud) does not require compensation according to the Mu'tazila.
3
Distinguish compensation from reward
The definition of compensation as 'نفع مستحق خال عن التعظيم والإجلال' shows it is a deserved benefit without veneration, unlike reward.
This distinction will be important later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : ) من تلك الأمور ( العوض عن (الآلام فإنهم (قالوا : الألم إن وقع جزاء لما صدر عن العبد من سيئة ) كألم الحد . ( لم يجب على الله عوضه وإلا أي وإن لم يقع جزاء ( فإن كان الإيلام من ) وجب العوض ( عليه ) فإن كان مكلف آخر فإن كان له حسنات أخذ من حسناته وأعطي المجني عليه عوضاً لإيلامه له، وإن لم يكن له حسنات وجب على الله إما صرف المؤلم عن إيلامه أو تعويضه من عنده بما يوازي إيلامه ) أي لا ينقص عن إيلامه، فهو احتراز عما دونه لا عما فوقه ( ولهم بناء على هذا الأصل) الذي هو وجوب العوض المعروف عندهم أنه نفع مستحق خال عن التعظيم والإجلال (اختلافات) ركيكة ( شاهدة بفساده ( أي بفساد الأصل ( الأول : قال طائفة ) كأبي هاشم وأتباعه : ( جاز أن يكون العوض في الدنيا ) إذ لا يجب دوامه، (وقال آخرون ) كالعلاف . قوله : ( فإن كان الإيلام من الله تعالى إلخ ) يدخل في هذا كل ما فعله غير المكلف كالسبع والصبي بقرينة ،قسميه وفيه بحث لأنه يخالف قوله عليه السلام : إنه تعالى يأخذ للجماء من القرناء، اللهم إلا أن يفرق بين تعويض المكلف وغيره وقد يجاب بأن الحديث خبر واحد في مقابلة القطع مع أنه لا يدل على كيفية الإنصاف، فلعلها تكون بإيتاء العوض من من عنده تعالى . قوله : ( وإن لم يكن له حسنات إلخ كانهم لا يجوزون أن يؤخذ من سيئات المجنى عليه ويحمل على الجاني على ما ورد في الأحاديث فلعلهم نظروا إلى ظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٦٤]، أي لا يؤاخذ أحد بذنب غيره لكنا نقول : هذا وزر إيلامه المظلوم . قوله : ( فهو احتراز عما دونه لا عما فوقه لما سيجيء من أنهم صرحوا بأن العوض من تعالى يجب أن يكون زائداً.، قوله: (مستحق خال عن التعظيم والإجلال احترز بالمستحق عن النفع المتفضل به لكونه غير مستحق وبالخالي عن التعظيم عن الثواب ومن هنا يظهر أنه لا يجب إعلام المستحق للعوض بإيصاله عوضا له بخلاف الثواب، فإنه يجب أن يقارن التعظيم ولا يحصل التعظيم إلا بأن يشعر بأنه ثواب له كذا في التجريد وشرحه .
The fifth: Among those matters is compensation for pains. For they said: Pain, if it occurs as recompense for what issued from the servant of an evil deed (like the pain of the hadd punishment), then God is not obligated to compensate for it. Otherwise, i.e., if it does not occur as recompense, then if the infliction of pain is from someone other than God, compensation is obligated upon him. If it is another responsible agent, then if he has good deeds, they are taken from his good deeds and given to the victim as compensation for his inflicting pain on him. And if he has no good deeds, then God is obligated either to turn away the one who inflicts pain from inflicting it, or to compensate [the victim] from His own [store] with something equivalent to the pain inflicted, meaning not less than the pain—so this is a precaution against less, not against more. And according to them, building upon this principle (which is the obligation of compensation, known to them as a deserved benefit free from veneration and honor), there are ridiculous differences, which testify to its corruption, i.e., the corruption of the principle. The first: a group (like Abu Hashim and his followers) said: It is permissible for compensation to be in this world, since its permanence is not obligatory. And others (like al-'Allaf) said... The commentator says: (If the infliction of pain is from God, etc.) This includes everything done by a non-responsible agent, like a wild animal or a child, by the indication of its two divisions. There is a problem with this because it contradicts the hadith: 'God will take for the hornless [sheep] from the horned.' However, a distinction could be made between compensation of a responsible agent and that of another. And it might be answered that the hadith is a solitary report (khabar wahid) against certainty, and it does not indicate the manner of justice; perhaps it occurs by granting compensation from His [store]. The commentator says: (And if he has no good deeds, etc.) It seems they do not allow taking from the victim's evil deeds and placing them on the aggressor, as is reported in hadiths; perhaps they consider the apparent meaning of His saying: 'And no bearer of burdens shall bear the burden of another' (Quran 6:164), meaning no one is taken to account for another's sin. But we say: This is the burden of his inflicting pain on the oppressed. The commentator says: (So it is a precaution against less, not against more) because it will come that they explicitly stated that compensation from God must be greater. The commentator says: (Deserved benefit free from veneration and honor) – by 'deserved' he distinguishes it from benefit granted as a favor, since it is not deserved; and by 'free from veneration' he distinguishes it from reward. Hence it becomes clear that it is not obligatory to inform the one deserving compensation that it is given to him as compensation, unlike reward, which must be accompanied by veneration, and veneration is only achieved by making him aware that it is a reward for him. This is as in al-Tajrid and its commentary.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth Mu'tazili argument concerning compensation (al-'iwad) for pains that are not recompense for sin. The doctrine states: Pain that is a penalty (like hudud) requires no compensation. Pain that is not a penalty requires compensation from the one who inflicted it if he is a responsible agent; if he has good deeds, those are taken, otherwise God must either prevent the pain or compensate with an equivalent (not less). The passage then notes that the Mu'tazila have 'ridiculous differences' (ikhtilafat rakika) based on this principle, which show its corruption. One difference: Abu Hashim and followers said compensation can be in this world, while al-'Allaf's group differed (text cuts off). The commentary (hashiya) expands on the main text: first, it notes that 'if the infliction is from God' includes actions of non-responsible agents like animals and children, which contradicts the hadith about God taking for the hornless from the horned, but a distinction is made. Second, regarding taking good deeds from the aggressor, it notes that they do not allow taking from the victim's own evil deeds onto the aggressor, citing Quran 6:164, but the commentator objects that this is about the burden of causing pain. Third, the phrase 'precaution against less' is explained because compensation must be greater. Fourth, the definition of 'iwad as a deserved benefit free from veneration distinguishes it from favor (tafaddul) and from reward (thawab), implying that the recipient need not be informed, unlike reward where veneration requires awareness.

Key Terms
العوض
الآلام
الجزاء
الحسنات
السيئات
المستحق
التعظيم
الإجلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That compensation (iwad) is the same as reward (thawab).
Correction: The passage defines iwad as a deserved benefit free from veneration, whereas thawab is accompanied by veneration and requires informing the recipient.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, when is compensation not required for pain?
A) When the pain is a punishment for sin
B) When the pain is from God
C) When the victim has good deeds
D) When the pain is in this world

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila held that any undeserved pain must be compensated, either by the inflicter or by God, and compensation is a deserved benefit without veneration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you experienced pain that you believe was undeserved. According to the Mu'tazili view, you are owed compensation from either the inflicter or God. Do you truly feel that this compensation has been or will be given? If not, how does this affect your reliance on God's justice?
?
If you have ever caused pain to someone unjustly, the Mu'tazila say compensation could be taken from your good deeds. Are you willing to lose your recorded good deeds for the sake of another's comfort? What does this say about your responsibility for your actions?
?
The Mu'tazila insist that God must compensate any undeserved pain. Does this belief make you feel more secure in God's justice, or does it create pressure to expect compensation in this life? How does your belief about divine compensation shape your patience in suffering?

Internal Mu'tazili Disputes on Compensation and Their Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the Mu'tazili internal disagreements on compensation and the commentator's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreements among Mu'tazili scholars regarding the timing and nature of compensation, and how these disagreements undermine their principle.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila disagreed on whether compensation can be in this world, and their differences show the principle's weakness.

The Mu'tazila themselves could not agree on the details of compensation. Abu Hashim and his followers allowed it in this world, while al-'Allaf's group held a different view. The commentator also raises issues with applying the doctrine to non-responsible agents and scriptural evidence, revealing contradictions that refute the original principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions on compensation timing
Read the section starting with 'ولهم بناء على هذا الأصل' and note the two groups: Abu Hashim's group allows compensation in this world; al-'Allaf's group disagrees.
2
Analyze the commentator's critique
The commentator raises issues with applying the doctrine to non-responsible agents (animals, children) and scriptural evidence.
Note the contradiction with the hadith about the hornless sheep.
3
Understand the refutation of the principle
The internal disagreements and scriptural problems are presented as evidence that the original principle is flawed.
The phrase 'شاهدة بفساده' is key.

Source Text

( لم يجب على الله عوضه وإلا أي وإن لم يقع جزاء ( فإن كان الإيلام من ) وجب العوض ( عليه ) فإن كان مكلف آخر فإن كان له حسنات أخذ من حسناته وأعطي المجني عليه عوضاً لإيلامه له، وإن لم يكن له حسنات وجب على الله إما صرف المؤلم عن إيلامه أو تعويضه من عنده بما يوازي إيلامه ) أي لا ينقص عن إيلامه، فهو احتراز عما دونه لا عما فوقه ( ولهم بناء على هذا الأصل) الذي هو وجوب العوض المعروف عندهم أنه نفع مستحق خال عن التعظيم والإجلال (اختلافات) ركيكة ( شاهدة بفساده ( أي بفساد الأصل ( الأول : قال طائفة ) كأبي هاشم وأتباعه : ( جاز أن يكون العوض في الدنيا ) إذ لا يجب دوامه، (وقال آخرون ) كالعلاف . قوله : ( فإن كان الإيلام من الله تعالى إلخ ) يدخل في هذا كل ما فعله غير المكلف كالسبع والصبي بقرينة ،قسميه وفيه بحث لأنه يخالف قوله عليه السلام : إنه تعالى يأخذ للجماء من القرناء، اللهم إلا أن يفرق بين تعويض المكلف وغيره وقد يجاب بأن الحديث خبر واحد في مقابلة القطع مع أنه لا يدل على كيفية الإنصاف، فلعلها تكون بإيتاء العوض من من عنده تعالى . قوله : ( وإن لم يكن له حسنات إلخ كانهم لا يجوزون أن يؤخذ من سيئات المجنى عليه ويحمل على الجاني على ما ورد في الأحاديث فلعلهم نظروا إلى ظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٦٤]، أي لا يؤاخذ أحد بذنب غيره لكنا نقول : هذا وزر إيلامه المظلوم . قوله : ( فهو احتراز عما دونه لا عما فوقه لما سيجيء من أنهم صرحوا بأن العوض من تعالى يجب أن يكون زائداً.، قوله: (مستحق خال عن التعظيم والإجلال احترز بالمستحق عن النفع المتفضل به لكونه غير مستحق وبالخالي عن التعظيم عن الثواب ومن هنا يظهر أنه لا يجب إعلام المستحق للعوض بإيصاله عوضا له بخلاف الثواب، فإنه يجب أن يقارن التعظيم ولا يحصل التعظيم إلا بأن يشعر بأنه ثواب له كذا في التجريد وشرحه .

Pain that is not a penalty requires compensation from the one who inflicted it if he is a responsible agent; if he has good deeds, those are taken, otherwise God must either prevent the pain or compensate with an equivalent (not less). The passage then notes that the Mu'tazila have 'ridiculous differences' (ikhtilafat rakika) based on this principle, which show its corruption. One difference: Abu Hashim and followers said compensation can be in this world, while al-'Allaf's group differed (text cuts off). The commentary (hashiya) expands on the main text: first, it notes that 'if the infliction is from God' includes actions of non-responsible agents like animals and children, which contradicts the hadith about God taking for the hornless from the horned, but a distinction is made. Second, regarding taking good deeds from the aggressor, it notes that they do not allow taking from the victim's own evil deeds onto the aggressor, citing Quran 6:164, but the commentator objects that this is about the burden of causing pain. Third, the phrase 'precaution against less' is explained because compensation must be greater. Fourth, the definition of 'iwad as a deserved benefit free from veneration distinguishes it from favor (tafaddul) and from reward (thawab), implying that the recipient need not be informed, unlike reward where veneration requires awareness.

Key Terms
العوض
الاختلافات
أبو هاشم
العلاف
المستحق
التعظيم
الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila were unified on compensation theory.
Correction: They had internal disagreements (e.g., on timing) that the author uses to refute the principle.

Checkpoint
What was the view of Abu Hashim and his followers regarding the timing of compensation?
A) It must be in the afterlife
B) It can be in this world
C) It can only be from God
D) It is never given

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili internal disputes on compensation expose the weakness of their principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you suffer an injustice, do you demand immediate compensation from God, or do you trust that His justice may unfold in ways beyond your expectation? How does the Mu'tazili insistence on exact compensation mirror your own impatience?
?
The commentator distinguishes between reward (with veneration) and compensation (without veneration). When you do good deeds, are you seeking the honor of God's recognition or merely a transactional benefit? Which do you truly desire, and why?
?
The Mu'tazila misused Quran 6:164 to justify their position. Have you ever twisted a Quranic verse to fit your own understanding of justice? What safeguards will you put in place to avoid selective interpretation?

First Disagreement: Location of Compensation
Discusses whether compensation must be in the afterlife or may be in this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili disagreement on whether compensation must be in the afterlife and the argument from infinite regress.

Core Idea
Compensation must be continuous; otherwise, endless compensation would be needed.

This passage introduces a key internal debate among the Mu'tazilites about where compensation (al-'iwad) must occur. One school holds that it can be in this world, while another insists it must be in the afterlife and continuous like reward. The argument hinges on avoiding an infinite regress of compensations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two opposing views and the reasoning behind each.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the group favoring worldly compensation (Abu Hashim) and the group requiring afterlife compensation (al-'Allaf, al-Jubba'i). Note their key difference: necessity of continuity.
3
Understand the argument for continuity
The second group argues that if compensation ends, it causes pain, requiring more compensation, leading to infinite regress. See how they use this to conclude compensation must be eternal in the afterlife.
This is the core logical move.
4
Examine the rebuttal
The author mentions a counter: the possibility that the recipient might not feel the cessation, which would break the regress. Consider whether this rebuttal is convincing.
This shows the debate was not settled.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this disagreement matters for the broader Mu'tazili theory of divine justice and compensation.
It reveals how rational arguments shaped theological positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الأول : قال طائفة ) كأبي هاشم وأتباعه : ( جاز أن يكون العوض في الدنيا ) إذ لا يجب دوامه، (وقال آخرون ) كالعلاف والجبائي وكثير من متقدميهم بل يجب أن يكون في الآخرة) الوجوب دوامه ( كالثواب ) وذلك لأن انقطاعه يوجب ألماً فيستحق بهذا الألم عوضاً آخر ويتسلسل، ورد بجواز عدم شعوره بالانقطاع.
(The first:) A group, such as Abu Hashim and his followers, said: It is permissible for compensation to be in this world, since its continuity is not required. And others, such as al-`Allaf, al-Jubba'i, and many of their predecessors, said: Rather it must be in the afterlife, requiring its continuity like reward. This is because its cessation would cause pain, and for that pain another compensation would be deserved and it would lead to an infinite regress. This was refuted by the possibility that he would not feel the cessation.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the first internal Mu'tazili disagreement about compensation. Two opinions: one group (Abu Hashim et al.) allows compensation in this world because it need not be continuous. The other group (al-`Allaf, al-Jubba'i, etc.) insists it must be in the afterlife and must be continuous like reward. Their argument: if compensation ends, the recipient would feel pain of loss, requiring further compensation, leading to infinite regress. The author notes this was rebutted by the possibility that the person might not feel the cessation.

Correction: Both groups agree God can give compensation; the disagreement is about whether it must be continuous and therefore only in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Compensation's pleasure may be fleeting or eternal—the Mu'tazila disagreed.

The Mu'tazila engaged in several internal debates about the nature of compensation (al-'iwad).
