Third Disagreement: Nullification of Compensation by Sins
Debates whether sins cancel out compensation just as they cancel reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing views on whether sins nullify compensation just as they nullify reward, and the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Compensation may be nullified by sin or simply reduced imperceptibly.

Theologians disagree on whether sins cancel out compensation (al-iwad) in the afterlife just as they cancel out reward. Some argue that a sinful disbeliever cannot simultaneously enjoy compensation and suffer punishment, so compensation must be nullified. Others propose that compensation is not nullified but rather realized by an imperceptible reduction of punishment over time. This lesson examines both positions and their underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Notice the structure: it presents two opposing views with their arguments.
The passage is concise; both views are given in a few lines.
2
Identify the two positions
The first position states that sins nullify compensation; the second denies nullification. Mark the key phrases that introduce each view.
3
Analyze the argument for nullification
The argument is based on the impossibility of simultaneously experiencing bliss (from compensation) and punishment (for sin/disbelief). Ask yourself: why is this combination impossible?
This is a rational argument (muhal) in kalam.
4
Analyze the argument against nullification
The opposing view explains how compensation can be applied without nullification: by reducing a part of punishment imperceptibly over time. Consider how this avoids the contradiction.
They distribute the removed part over time so that there is no abrupt relief.
5
Consider the common misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is that nullification would completely destroy compensation. The second view shows that compensation can still be given in a different manner.
This helps clarify the stakes of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : هل يحبط العوض بالذنوب كما يحبط الثواب ) أو لا فمن قال بالإحباط تمسك بأنه لولاه لكان الفاسق والكافر في كل وقت من أوقات الآخرة في نعيم العوض وعقاب الفسق أو الكفر ، والجمع بينهما محال، ومن لم يقل به ذهب إلى أن عوض أهل النار بإسقاط جزء من عقابهم بحيث لا يظهر لهم التخفيف وذلك بتفريق الجزء الساقط على الأوقات كيلا يتألم بانقطاع التخفيف .
(The third:) Is compensation nullified by sins as reward is nullified, or not? Those who said it is nullified argued that otherwise the sinner and disbeliever would at every moment of the afterlife be in the bliss of compensation and the punishment of sin or disbelief, and combining them is impossible. Those who did not say that held that the compensation of the people of the Fire is by removing a part of their punishment such that relief does not appear to them, and that by distributing the removed part over the times so that they do not feel pain from the cessation of relief.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents the third disagreement: whether sins nullify compensation (ihbat) just as they nullify reward. One group says yes, because otherwise a sinful disbeliever would simultaneously experience both compensation bliss and punishment, which is impossible. The other group rejects nullification, arguing that compensation for hell's inhabitants is effected by reducing a portion of their punishment imperceptibly over time, thus avoiding the contradiction.

Key Terms

العوض
الثواب
الفسق
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nullification means compensation is completely destroyed and never given.
Correction: Nullification means compensation is not given at all; the opposing view holds that compensation is still given but in an imperceptible way that avoids combining bliss and punishment.

Checkpoint

A) They are given a different kind of pleasure that overrides pain
B) A part of their punishment is removed over time so they don't feel relief ending
C) They receive compensation only after exiting hell
D) Compensation is given to their families instead

Key Takeaway
The disagreement over nullification of compensation hinges on whether the simultaneous experience of bliss and punishment is possible; the second view avoids it by making compensation imperceptible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Compensation Without Pain: The Fourth Disagreement
Raises the question: can compensation be given without prior pain?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth disagreement in kalam about whether compensation can be given without prior pain, and why this question matters for divine justice.

Core Idea
Can God give compensation before pain? This question tests the nature of divine justice.

This lesson presents a short but pivotal question: Is it permissible for God to give compensation for pains without the pains actually occurring? At first glance, this may seem odd, but it opens up deeper discussions about whether compensation is inherently tied to pain or can be a gratuitous gift. The passage simply frames the two sides, and we will explore the implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the question carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice it is the fourth disagreement, phrased as a yes/no question.
The passage does not give an answer; it only raises the issue.
2
Identify the two positions
The question implies two sides: those who say compensation can be given initially, and those who say it must follow pain. Think about what each position assumes about God's justice.
3
Consider the theological stakes
If compensation can be given without pain, then pain is not a necessary condition. That raises questions about why God allows suffering if He could compensate without it. This relates to the problem of evil.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
This is the fourth disagreement among several. Understand how it leads to further sub-issues about the nature of compensation.

Source Text

(The fourth:) Is it permissible to deliver that which would be given as compensation for pains initially without preceding pain, or is it not permissible?
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This short passage raises the fourth disagreement: can God give compensation for pain without the pain occurring first? That is, can He simply bestow the compensation as a gift? The text only frames the question, leaving the two positions to be understood: some say yes, some no. This leads to the fifth and sixth sub-issues.

Fifth Disagreement: Purpose of Causing Pain for Compensation
Presents two views on the purpose of causing pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on why God causes pain (for compensation or as a gratuitous favor) and the objection that the latter contradicts wisdom.

Core Idea
Pain is justified either as a means to compensation or as a favor, but the latter is contrary to wisdom.

This lesson explores the fifth disagreement among theologians on the issue of causing pain. Under the assumption that causing pain is permissible, the question arises whether it is done specifically to grant compensation afterward or whether compensation could be given as a favor without causing pain at all. The passage captures this debate with a concise formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the concise question format and the phrase 'contrary to wisdom' at the end.
The passage is a single sentence; let its structure guide your initial understanding.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two alternatives separated by 'or'. Identify what each alternative claims about the relationship between pain, compensation, and favor.
The first alternative is 'to compensate'; the second is 'as a favor without pain'.
3
Understand the wisdom objection
The phrase 'contrary to wisdom' is attached to the second position. Ask yourself why giving compensation as a favor without causing pain would be considered unwise.
The commentary explains that causing pain without a purpose contradicts divine wisdom, so the second view is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الخامس على الجواز : هل يؤلم ليعوض أو يكون ذلك مع إمكان الابتداء به على طريق التفضل ) مخالفاً للحكمة
(The fifth, on the permissibility view:) Is he caused pain in order to compensate, or could that be with the possibility of initiating it [the compensation] as a favor, contrary to wisdom?
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
Assuming permissibility (from the fourth issue), the fifth disagreement asks: does God cause pain specifically to give compensation, or could He simply give the compensation as a favor without causing pain? The latter view is considered contradictory to wisdom (hikma) because causing pain unnecessarily would be pointless. The other position sees the pain as a means to grant compensation, implying a wise purpose.

Sixth Disagreement: Pain for Extra Compensation as Grace
Poses the sixth disagreement on whether pain causes extra compensation as grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth kalam disagreement: whether on the prohibition view, God causes pain to grant extra compensation as a divine grace (lutf).

Core Idea
On the prohibition view, pain may be a means to extra compensation as grace.

This lesson examines a subtle kalam debate: assuming it is impermissible for God to cause pain without compensation, could He cause pain precisely to give an extra compensation that serves as a grace (lutf) to the sufferer and others? The passage poses this question and hints at its impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic phrase and its literal translation to grasp the wording and the question being posed.
Note the parentheses and the phrase 'since its impossibility' which is key.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the three key terms: al-lutf (grace), al-za'id (extra), and al-imtina' (impossibility/prohibition). Understand how they frame the debate.
3
Understand the logical structure
The passage assumes the prohibition view (from a prior issue) and asks: does God cause pain in order to give extra compensation that serves as grace? The phrase 'since its impossibility' suggests that without the pain, the compensation (grace) would be impossible.
This is a question, not a statement of belief.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters: it explores the rationale behind divine actions and the concept of grace (lutf) in kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( السادس على المنع هل يؤلم ليعوض عوضاً زائداً ليكون لطفاً له ولغيره إذ استحالته )
(The sixth, on the prohibition view:) Is he caused pain to compensate with an extra compensation, to be a grace (lutf) for him and for others, since its impossibility?
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
Assuming permissibility is prohibited (from the fourth issue), the sixth disagreement asks: does God cause pain to then give an extra compensation beyond the equivalent, as a divine grace (lutf) for the sufferer and others? The phrase 'since its impossibility' likely refers to the impossibility of initiating compensation without pain (the prohibition). Thus, on this view, the pain is a means to a greater benefit: extra compensation that serves as grace.

Key Terms

الزائد
الامتناع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This disagreement debates whether causing pain is permissible.
Correction: No, the prohibition view is already assumed (from issue 4). The debate is about the purpose: if pain is caused, is it to give extra compensation as a grace?

Checkpoint

A) To punish the person
B) To give an extra compensation as a grace
C) To prove that God is just

Key Takeaway
On the prohibition view, the possibility that God causes pain to give extra compensation as a grace remains a debated question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Permissibility of Imposing the Impossible
Defines the doctrine, its levels, and addresses a key objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite position on the permissibility of imposing what is unbearable, the two levels of impossibility, and the theological objection regarding divine power.

Core Idea
God is not bound by human notions of obligation; what is impossible for creatures is no limit on His command.

This lesson examines a central Ash'arite doctrine: that God may impose what is beyond human capacity. The Mu'tazila rejected this as rationally evil, but the Ash'arites argue from God's absolute sovereignty. The passage defines levels of impossibility and addresses a key objection about divine power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'arite principle
Read the opening lines stating that nothing is obligatory or evil for God. This is the foundation for the permissibility of imposing the impossible.
Recall the earlier proposition about God's will being unbounded.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
Note the analogy of commanding a blind man to dot copies of the Qur'an. This illustrates their rational objection.
This objection is based on analogical reasoning from human experience.
3
Study the levels of impossibility
The lowest level is based on God's knowledge, will, or informing. The highest is intrinsically impossible. Understand the proof for the lowest level using the concept of originated power.
The proof relies on the Ash'arite view that power accompanies the act.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection argues that if power is with the act, then all obligations would be impossible. The answer clarifies that power does not relate to the impossible act at any time.
Reflect on the necessity of preserving God's attributes from inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد السابع : جواز تكليف ما لا يطاق. أنه تكليف ما لا يطاق جائز عندنا لما قدمنا آنفاً في المقصد السادس من لا يجب عليه شيء ولا يقبح منه شيء إذ يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد لا معقب ومنعه المعتزلة لقبحه عقلا كما في الشاهد فإن من كلف الأعمى نقط المصاحف والزمن المشي إلى أقاصي البلاد وعبده الطيران إلى السماء عد سفيهاً، وقبح ذلك في بداية العقول وكان كأمر الجماد الذي لا شك في كونه سفها. واعلم أن ما لا يطاق على مراتب أدناها أن يمتنع الفعل لعلم الله بعدم وقوعه أو تعلق إرادته أو إخباره بعدمه فإن مثله لا تتعلق به القدرة الحادثة لأن القدرة الحادثة مع الفعل لا قبله ولا تتعلق بالضدين بل لكل واحد منهما قدرة على حدة تتعلق به حال وجوده عندنا والتكليف بهذا جائز بل واقع إجماعاً وإلا لم يكن العاصي بكفره وفسقه مكلفاً بالإيمان وترك الكبائر، بل لا يكون تارك المأمور به عاصيا أصلا وذلك معلوم بطلانه من الدين ضرورة وأقصاها أن يمتنع لنفس مفهومه كجمع الضدين وقلب الحقائق وإعدام القديم وجواز التكليف به فرع تصوره مختلف فيه فمنا من قال : لو لم يتصور الممتنع لذاته لامتنع الحكم عليه بامتناع تصوره وامتناع طلبه إلى غير ذلك من الأحكام الجارية عليه. ومنهم من قال : طلبه يتوقف على تصوره واقعاً أي ثابتاً لأن الطالب لثبوت شيء لا بد أن يتصور مطلوبه على الوجه الذي يتعلق به طلبه ثم يطلبه. قوله : ( ولا يقبح منه شيء ) ومن هذا تكليف تحصيل الحاصل. قوله : ( لا معقب لحكمه أي لا راد له وحقيقته الذي يعقب الشيء بالإبطال . قوله : ( لأن القدرة الحادثة مع الفعل إلخ) اعترض عليه الأستاذ المحقق بأن عدم تعلق القدرة إذا كان من جهة أن القدرة مع الفعل لم يبق لامتناع الفعل بعلم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه ولا لإرادته لذلك واختياره إياه، دخل، ولزم أن يكون كل مكلف به مما لا يطاق سواء علم الله تعالى وقوعه أو علم عدم وقوعه لأن التكليف قبل الفعل بالضرورة والقدرة معه ولا قائل به لأن مرادهم بالقدرة في هذا المقام سلامة الأسباب والآلات لا الاستطاعة التي مع الفعل. وجوابه أن معنى قوله : فإن مثله لا تتعلق به القدرة الحادثة إلخ، أنه لا يتعلق به في زمان من الأزمان أي ولو في الزمان المستقبل بالنظر إلى زمان التكليف لأن القدرة مع الفعل والفعل ممتنع في جميع الأزمان لأن وقوعه يستلزم انقلاب علم الله تعالى جهلاً أو تخلف المراد عن الإرادة أو انقلاب أخباره كذباً، فتأمل.
The sixth observation - the seventh intent: The permissibility of imposing what is unbearable. That imposing what is unbearable is permissible according to us because of what we presented earlier in the sixth intent: that nothing is obligatory for Him and nothing is evil from Him, for He does what He wills and judges what He desires, with no reviewer. The Mu'tazila prohibited it because it is rationally evil, as in the witnessed: if one were to charge a blind man to dot the copies of the Qur'an, a paralytic to walk to distant lands, and his slave to fly to the sky, he would be considered foolish, and that is evil in the beginnings of intellects and is like commanding an inanimate object, which is undoubtedly foolish. Know that what is unbearable has levels: the lowest is that the act is impossible due to God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, or the attachment of His will, or His informing of its non-occurrence. For such an act, the originated power does not relate to it because the originated power is with the act, not before it, and it does not relate to opposites; rather, for each of the two opposites there is a separate power that relates to it at the time of its existence according to us. Imposing this is permissible, indeed it actually occurs by consensus. Otherwise, the sinner through his disbelief and immorality would not be obligated to faith and avoidance of major sins; rather, the one who leaves the commanded would not be a sinner at all, and it is known from the religion by necessity that this is false. The highest level is that which is impossible due to its very concept, like combining two opposites, changing realities, and annihilating the eternal. The permissibility of imposing it depends on its being conceptualizable, which is disputed. Among us are those who say: if the intrinsically impossible were not conceptualized, then judgment upon it of the impossibility of its conceptualization and the impossibility of seeking it, among other judgments that apply to it, would be impossible. And among them are those who say: seeking it depends on its conceptualization as actual, i.e., established, because the seeker of the establishment of something must conceptualize his object of seeking in the manner in which his seeking relates to it, then seek it. His statement: 'And nothing is evil from Him' – from this is the imposition of obtaining what has already been obtained. His statement: 'No reviewer for His judgment' – meaning no repeller for it, and its reality is the one who follows something to nullify it. His statement: 'Because the originated power is with the act, etc.' The master investigator objected that if the non-relation of power is due to the fact that power is with the act, then there remains no [basis] for the impossibility of the act due to God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, nor for His will for it and His choice of it, and it would follow that every obligated act is unbearable, whether God knows its occurrence or its non-occurrence, because the imposition is before the act necessarily, and power is with it, and no one says that. For their intention by 'power' in this context is the soundness of causes and instruments, not the capability that is with the act. The answer is that the meaning of his statement 'for such an act the originated power does not relate to it, etc.' is that it does not relate to it in any time, i.e., even in the future time relative to the time of imposition, because power is with the act, and the act is impossible in all times because its occurrence would entail the change of God's knowledge into ignorance, or the failure of the intended to follow the will, or the change of His reports into lies. So reflect.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification regarding the permissibility of imposing the impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). It begins by stating that the doctrine of the Ash'arites is that such imposition is permissible, based on the principle established in the sixth intent: God is not obligated to do anything, and nothing is evil from Him. He does as He wills and there is no reviewer of His judgment. The Mu'tazila denied this because it is rationally evil, citing the analogy of ordering a blind man to dot Qur'an copies, which is clearly foolish. The passage then explains that the 'unbearable' has levels. The lowest level is when the act is impossible due to God's knowledge that it will not occur, or His will or informing that it will not occur. The originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) does not relate to such an act because according to the Ash'arites power accompanies the act and does not precede it. Despite this, such imposition is permissible and actually occurs by consensus; otherwise the sinner would not be obligated to believe and avoid major sins. The highest level is the intrinsically impossible, like combining opposites or annihilating the eternal. The permissibility of imposing such acts depends on whether they are conceptualizable (taṣawwur), which is disputed. Some say they are not conceptualizable, so no judgment applies; others say seeking them depends on conceptualizing them as actual. The passage then comments on specific phrases from the original text, including an objection from 'the master investigator' (al-ustādh al-muḥaqqiq) regarding the proof about power: if power is always with the act, then the impossibility from God's knowledge would not be relevant, and all obligations would be unbearable. The answer clarifies that the intended meaning is that power does not relate to the impossible act at any time, because the act is impossible in all times due to the necessary entailments that would contradict God's attributes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that 'impossible' in this context always refers to logical impossibility, and that the Ash'arites affirm imposing truly impossible tasks.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between levels of impossibility. The lowest level is not logically impossible but contingent on divine attributes; the highest level is intrinsically impossible, but its imposition is debated.

Checkpoint

C) Because the act is always within human ability
D) Because the Mu'tazila also allow it

Key Takeaway
Imposing the impossible is permissible because God is sovereign and not bound by human notions of good and evil, and the division of impossibility into levels clarifies the doctrine.

?

?

The Lowest Rank of the Impossible: Acts Known by Allah Not to Occur
Describes the lowest rank of acts impossible due to divine attributes, yet commanded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first and lowest rank of what cannot be borne, specifically acts that are impossible due to Allah's foreknowledge, will, or informing, and why such commands are still valid and actually occur.

Core Idea
Even impossible commands are real when they stem from divine knowledge or will.

The author begins by explaining that the category of 'what cannot be borne' has multiple ranks. The lowest is when an act is impossible because Allah knows it will not happen, or because His will or informing affirms its non-occurrence. At first glance, commanding such an act seems pointless, but the Ash'ari tradition defends its validity to maintain the reality of moral responsibility. This passage unpacks the reasoning behind this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage lists three reasons for impossibility: divine knowledge, will, or informing.
The parentheses in the Arabic indicate explanatory insertions.
2
Understand 'originated power'
The author explains that the created power (al-qudra al-hāditha) is simultaneous with the act, not before it. This is a key Ash'ari principle.
Power does not precede the act; it coexists.
3
Grasp why such acts are impossible for the servant
Because power only attaches when the act exists, and since the act never occurs (due to divine knowledge/will), the power never attaches. Hence, the act is impossible for the servant.
4
Recognize the validity of the command
Despite the impossibility, the command is still valid and actually occurs by consensus. The passage gives the example of the disbeliever commanded to believe.
If not valid, no one would be truly disobedient.
5
Reflect on the theological consequence
If commanding such acts were not permissible, then a disbeliever would not be commanded to believe, and the one who leaves an obligation would not be disobedient. This is contrary to necessary religious knowledge.
This shows the necessity of real commands even when the outcome is known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ما لا يطاق على مراتب أدناها أن يمتنع الفعل لعلم الله بعدم وقوعه ( أو ) تعلق ( إرادته أو إخباره) بعدمه ( فإن مثله لا تتعلق به القدرة الحادثة لأن القدرة) الحادثة (مع الفعل لا قبله ( ولا تتعلق بالضدين بل لكل واحد منهما قدرة على حدة تتعلق به حال وجوده عندنا ( والتكليف بهذا جائز بل واقع إجماعاً وإلا لم يكن العاصي بكفره وفسقه مكلفاً بالإيمان وترك الكبائر، بل لا يكون تارك المأمور به عاصيا أصلا وذلك معلوم بطلانه من الدين ضرورة
Know that what cannot be borne has ranks. The lowest of them is that the act is impossible because of God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, or because of the attachment of His will or His informing of its non-occurrence. For such an act is not attached by the originated power (al-qudra al-hāditha), because the originated power is with the act, not before it, and it does not attach to two opposites; rather, for each of them there is a separate power that attaches to it at the time of its existence according to us. And commanding this is permissible, indeed actually occurring by consensus. Otherwise, the disobedient person in his disbelief and sin would not be commanded to have faith and abandon major sins; indeed, the one who leaves the commanded act would not be a disobedient at all. The falsity of that is known from religion by necessity.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage classifies the impossible into ranks and details the lowest rank: an act that is impossible because God knows it will not happen, or because His will or informing dictates its non-occurrence. The author explains that such an act cannot be attached by the created power (qudra) because, according to the Ash'ari view, power is simultaneous with the act, not before it. He also notes that power does not attach to two opposites simultaneously; each act requires its own power at its moment. Despite this, commanding such an act is permissible and in fact occurs: for example, the disbeliever is commanded to believe even though God knows he will not. If such commanding were not valid, then no one would be categorized as a disobedient, which is contrary to the necessary knowledge of religion.

Misconception: If God knows an act will not happen, commanding it is pointless and the command is not real.
Correction: The command is real and establishes accountability, even though compliance is impossible due to divine foreknowledge. The passage emphasizes that commanding such acts is permissible and actually occurs.

Objection and Answer on Power and Impossibility
Objection and answer on power and impossibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if power is simultaneous with the act then all acts would be impossible, and the answer that impossibility is eternal due to divine attributes.

Core Idea
The impossible act is eternally impossible, not just at the moment of power.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the Ash'ari position on power and the impossible. The objector argues that if power is only with the act, then every commanded act would be impossible, contradicting the fact that some acts occur. The answer clarifies that the impossibility is not temporal but eternal, rooted in the impossibility of changing God's knowledge or will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the premise: power is contemporaneous with the act. See why this leads to the absurdity that all commanded acts are impossible.
The objector says 'no one holds this' – so it's a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the objector's suggestion
Notice the objector's alternative: perhaps by 'power' the author meant soundness of causes, not the capacity that accompanies the act.
3
Follow the answer
The answer explains that the author's statement means the act is impossible in all times, not just at the moment of command. The act's occurrence would entail changing God's attributes.
The answer invokes the impossibility of changing divine knowledge, will, or truthfulness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Realize that the impossibility is eternal and intrinsic to the act, not merely due to the timing of power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأن القدرة الحادثة مع الفعل إلخ) اعترض عليه الأستاذ المحقق بأن عدم تعلق القدرة إذا كان من جهة أن القدرة مع الفعل لم يبق لامتناع الفعل بعلم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه ولا لإرادته لذلك واختياره إياه ،دخل، ولزم أن يكون كل مكلف به مما لا يطاق سواء علم الله تعالى وقوعه أو علم عدم وقوعه لأن التكليف قبل الفعل بالضرورة والقدرة معه ولا قائل به لأن مرادهم بالقدرة في هذا المقام سلامة الأسباب والآلات لا الاستطاعة التي مع الفعل. وجوابه أن معنى قوله : فإن مثله لا تتعلق به القدرة الحادثة إلخ، أنه لا يتعلق به في زمان من الأزمان أي ولو في الزمان المستقبل بالنظر إلى زمان التكليف لأن القدرة مع الفعل والفعل ممتنع في جميع الأزمان لأن وقوعه يستلزم انقلاب علم الله تعالى جهلاً أو تخلف المراد عن الإرادة أو انقلاب أخباره كذباً، فتأمل.
His saying: 'Because the originated power is with the act, etc.' – The verifying master objected to him: that if the non-attachment of power is due to the fact that power is with the act, then there remains no reason for the impossibility of the act due to God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, nor due to His will for that and His choice of it. It would enter and necessarily every commanded thing would be something that cannot be borne, regardless of whether God knows its occurrence or knows its non-occurrence, because command is before the act necessarily, while power is with it. No one holds this, because what they mean by 'power' in this context is the soundness of causes and instruments, not the capacity that is with the act. The answer: the meaning of his saying 'such an act is not attached by the originated power, etc.' is that it is not attached by it at any time whatsoever, even in the future time relative to the time of command, because power is with the act, and the act is impossible in all times because its occurrence would necessitate the change of God's knowledge into ignorance, or the failure of the desired to be in accordance with will, or the change of His informing into falsehood. So reflect.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents an objection raised by 'the verifying master' (al-ustādh al-muḥaqqiq) to the previous explanation about the first rank. The objector argues: if the reason the power does not attach is that power is simultaneous with the act, then the impossibility resulting from God's knowledge, will, or informing would be irrelevant, because the power's non-attachment would already apply to all acts (both those that occur and those that do not) since command precedes the act while power is with the act. This would make every commanded act impossible, which no one holds. The objector suggests that by 'power' the author meant the soundness of causes and instruments, not the capacity that accompanies the act. The answer clarifies that the author's statement means that the power never attaches to the act in any time (past, present, future) because the act is eternally impossible due to the consequences of it occurring (such as changing God's knowledge). Thus the impossibility is not merely due to the simultaneity of power with the act, but due to the act's inherent impossibility in all times.

الاستطاعة
علم الله
إرادة الله
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection shows that the Ash'ari position leads to the absurd conclusion that all commanded acts are impossible.
Correction: The answer demonstrates that the impossibility is not temporal but eternal, based on the nature of the act itself, not the simultaneity of power.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the objection?
A) Power is only with the act and never before it.
B) The act is impossible in all times, not just at the moment of command.
C) Power means soundness of causes, not capacity.
D) The objector misunderstood the definition of impossibility.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an act is eternal, rooted in the impossibility of change in divine attributes, not merely in the timing of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conceiving the Intrinsically Impossible
Debate on how the intrinsically impossible is conceived and judged.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how intrinsically impossible things (like combining opposites) can be conceived only negatively or analogically, and why this distinction matters for theological commands.

Core Idea
The impossible is conceived only through negation or analogy, not as actual.

This passage tackles a subtle but critical question: Can we truly conceive something that is impossible in itself, like making a square circle? If we can't conceive it, how can we judge it as impossible or command it? The author presents the debate and offers a resolution: we conceive such impossibilities only indirectly, through negation or analogy, never as actual states. This subtlety safeguards both rational discourse and theological accuracy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice the two opposing views and the author's resolution with two modes of conception.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two disputed positions
The passage first states that some say the impossible must be conceivable for us to judge it, while others say it must be conceived as actual for us to command it. Trace these two arguments in the text.
3
Understand the two modes of conception
The author then explains that the impossible is conceived either negatively (recognizing it doesn't exist) or by analogy (conceiving a similar but not identical combination). Focus on why these modes are sufficient for judgment but not for command.
The analogy example: conceiving blackness and sweetness together as a likeness, not as the actual impossible combination.

وأقصاها أن يمتنع لنفس مفهومه كجمع الضدين وقلب الحقائق وإعدام القديم وجواز التكليف به فرع تصوره مختلف فيه فمنا من قال : لو لم يتصور الممتنع لذاته لامتنع الحكم عليه بامتناع تصوره وامتناع طلبه إلى غير ذلك من الأحكام الجارية عليه . ومنهم من قال : طلبه يتوقف على تصوره واقعاً أي ثابتاً لأن الطالب لثبوت شيء لا بد أن لا مطلوبه على الوجه الذي يتعلق به طلبه ثم يطلبه وهو أي التصور على وجه الوقوع والثبوت منتف هاهنا أي في الممتنع لنفس مفهومه فإنه يستحيل تصوره ثابتا وذلك لأن ماهيته من حيث هي هي تقتضى انتفاءه وتصور الشيء على خلاف ما تقتضيه ذاته لذاته لا يكون تصوراً له بل لشيء آخر، كمن يتصور أربعة ليست بزوج، فإنه لا يكون متصوراً للأربعة قطعاً بل الممتنع لذاته إنما يتصور على أحد وجهين: إما منفياً بمعنى أنه ليس لنا شيء موهوم أو محقق هو اجتماع الضدين أو بالتشبيه بمعنى أن يتصور اجتماع المتخالفين كالسواد والحلاوة، ثم يحكم بأن مثله لا يكون بين الضدين، وذلك أي تصوره على أحد هذين الوجهين كاف في الحكم عليه دون طلبه لأنه غير تصور وقوعه وثبوته ولا مستلزم له، صرح ابن سینا به أي بأن تصوره كذلك كما نقلناه عنه في باب العلم ولعله معنى قول أبي هاشم العلم بالمستحيل علم لا معلوم له كما أشرنا إليه هناك أيضاً ولعله مراد من قال : المستحيل لا يعلم أي لا يعلم من حيث ذاته وماهيته.
And the highest of them is that it is impossible by its own concept, like combining opposites, altering realities, and annihilating the eternal. The permissibility of commanding it is based on its conception, and it is disputed. Among us are those who said: if the intrinsically impossible were not conceived, then it would be impossible to judge it as impossible to conceive, impossible to request, and other judgments applied to it. And among them are those who said: requesting it depends on its conception as actual, i.e., as established, because the requester of the establishment of something must have his requested object in the manner in which his request attaches to it, then request it. But conception in the manner of occurrence and establishment is absent here, i.e., in the case of the impossible by its own concept, for it is impossible to conceive it as established. That is because its essence, in itself, necessitates its negation. And conceiving a thing contrary to what its essence in itself requires is not a conception of it, but of something else, like one who conceives four that is not even: he is certainly not conceiving four. Rather, the intrinsically impossible is conceived in one of two ways: either negatively, meaning that we have no imagined or verified thing that is the combining of opposites; or by analogy, meaning that one conceives the combining of things that are dissimilar, like blackness and sweetness, then judges that the like of that does not occur between opposites. And that – i.e., its conception in one of these two ways – is sufficient for judging it, but not for requesting it, because it is not a conception of its occurrence and establishment, nor does it entail that. Ibn Sina explicitly stated this – i.e., that its conception is as we have transmitted from him in the chapter on knowledge. Perhaps this is the meaning of Abu Hashim's statement: 'Knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without an object,' as we pointed out there as well. And perhaps it is the intended meaning of those who said: 'The impossible is not known,' i.e., it is not known in terms of its essence and quiddity.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage describes the highest rank of the impossible: that which is impossible by its own concept (al-mumtani' li-dhātihi), such as combining opposites, altering realities, or annihilating the eternal. The author notes that the permissibility of commanding this depends on whether it can be conceived (tasawwur). There is a dispute: Some say that if it were not conceivable, we could not judge it as impossible to conceive or request. Others say that requesting it depends on conceiving it as actual and established, which is impossible here because its essence necessitates its negation. To illustrate, the author explains that conceiving something contrary to its essence (e.g., four that is not even) is not a true conception of the thing. However, the impossible can be conceived in two ways: (1) negatively—recognizing that there is no imagined or real thing that is a combining of opposites; (2) by analogy—conceiving the combining of dissimilar things (like blackness and sweetness) and then judging that this is not like the actual impossible combination. Such a conception is sufficient for judging (e.g., that it is impossible) but not for requesting it, because it does not entail conception of its actual occurrence. The author cites Ibn Sina and Abu Hashim in support: knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without a known object; the impossible is not known in terms of its essence.

Key Terms
الممتنع لذاته
جمع الضدين
قلب الحقائق
إعدام القديم
تصور
ابن سينا
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since we can talk about the impossible, we must conceive it truly as an actual state.
Correction: The passage clarifies that we conceive it only negatively or analogically, not as an actual occurrence. Our conception is indirect and does not capture its essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the intrinsically impossible conceived?
A) As an actual state in the mind.
B) Only through negation or analogy.
C) By imagining it as a possible object.
D) It cannot be conceived at all.

Key Takeaway
The intrinsically impossible can only be conceived indirectly—negatively or by analogy—not as an actual state, which allows judgment but not legitimate command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something you believe is absolutely impossible in your life—a change, an event, a personal failure. Do you conceive of it as a real possibility (even if remote) or as a pure absence? What does this lesson reveal about your real belief about that impossibility? Will you examine your assumptions today?
?
When you say 'God cannot do X' (like making a square circle), do you understand what you are judging? How does knowing that your conception is only negative or analogical affect the certainty you feel? Does it make you more humble or more confident in your theology? What will you do with that awareness?
?
The passage shows that we can judge the impossible without truly grasping its essence. Are there judgments you make about others or yourself that are similarly based on a shallow conception? How can you push beyond mere negative or analogical thinking to truly understand people and situations, even if they seem impossible?

The Middle Rank: Usual Impossibility
Defines usual impossibility and states the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of usual impossibility and the Ash'ari stance that such a burden is permissible for God to impose.

Core Idea
Usual impossibility is not absolute; God can demand it, though He doesn't.

Sanusi now defines the middle rank of impossibility—what is usually beyond human power. This category is central to the debate with the Mu'tazila, who deny its permissibility. Let's read his precise definition and the scriptural basis for the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the middle rank
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note how Sanusi distinguishes two types of usual impossibility: one where the act is not of the genus that power attaches to, and one where it is of the type but too heavy.
Examples: creating bodies vs carrying a mountain.
2
Identify the Ash'ari stance
Notice that Sanusi says 'we permit it' (nujawwizuhu) despite its non-occurrence, citing Quran 2:286. This is the Ash'ari position.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila view
The Mu'tazila prevent it because they deem it ugly (qabih). Understand that the disagreement is moral, not textual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرتبة الوسطى من مراتب ما لا يطاق أن لا يتعلق به القدرة الحادثة عادة سواء امتنع تعلقها به لا لنفس مفهومه بأن لا يكون من جنس ما تتعلق به كخلق الأجسام فإن القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلق بإيجاد الجوهر أصلا أم لا بأن يكون من ما تتعلق به لكن يكون من نوع أو صنف لا تتعلق به كحمل الجبل والطيران إلى السماء فهذا أي التكليف بما لا يطاق عادة نجوزه نحن وإن لم يقع بالاستقراء ولقوله تعالى : ولا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٨٦]، وتمنعه المعتزلة لكونه قبيحاً عندهم وبه أي بما ذكرناه من التفصيل وتحرير المتنازع فيه يعلم أن كثيراً من أدلة أصحابنا مثل ما قالوه في إيمان أبي لهب وكونه مأموراً بالجمع بين المتناقضين نصب للدليل في غير محل النزاع إذ لم يجوزه أحد، ولقائل أن يقول : ما ذكره من أن جواز التكليف بالممتنع لذاته فرع تصوره وإن بعضاً منا قالوا بوقوع تصوره يشعر بأن هؤلاء يجوزونه. قوله : ( ولقائل أن يقول : ما ذكره إلخ يرد عليه أن هذا الاعتراض إنما يتوجه على ما حمل هو كلام المصنف عليه من أن المراد بما قالوا في إيمان أبي لهب : إنه مكلف بأن يؤمن بجملة ما أتى به النبي عليه السلام ومن جملتها أنه لا يؤمن فيكون تكليفاً بالجمع بين المتناقضين وأما إذا حمل على أن المراد بما قالوه أنه تعالى علم عدم إيمانه وأخبر به ومع هذا كلفه بالإيمان فيكون تكليفاً بمالا يطاق، فصح قول المصنف أنه ليس بمحل النزاع لأنه من القسم الأول ولم يرد عليه ما أورده .
The middle rank of the ranks of what cannot be borne is that the originated power does not attach to it usually, whether the non-attachment is because it is not of the genus of things it attaches to—like creating bodies, for the originated power does not attach to producing substance at all—or because it is of what it attaches to but of a type or species that it does not attach to, like carrying a mountain and flying to the sky. This—i.e., commanding what cannot be borne usually—we permit, even if it has not occurred by induction, and due to His saying: 'And Allah does not burden a soul except according to its capacity' (Quran 2:286). The Mu'tazila prevent it because it is ugly in their view. And by it—i.e., by the detailed distinction and clarification of the point of dispute—it is known that many of the proofs of our companions, such as what they said about Abu Lahab's faith and that he was commanded to combine two contradictories, are presenting evidence in a place that is not the subject of dispute, since no one permitted it. And someone may say: what he mentioned that the permissibility of commanding the intrinsically impossible depends on its conception, and that some of us said that its conception occurs, suggests that these people permit it. His saying (and someone may say: what he mentioned etc.) is refuted by the fact that this objection only applies to the interpretation he placed on the author's words—that what they said about Abu Lahab's faith is that he was commanded to believe in all that the Prophet brought, and among that is that he would not believe, so it is a command to combine two contradictories. But if interpreted that what they said is that God knew and informed of his lack of faith, yet still commanded him to have faith, then it becomes a command of something that cannot be borne, not a conceptual impossibility. So the author's statement that it is not the subject of dispute is correct, because it falls under the first division, and the objection does not apply.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines the middle rank of the impossible: what is usually beyond human power, either because it is not within the kind of acts that created power can affect (e.g., creating bodies) or because it is of a type or species that power does not reach (e.g., carrying a mountain, flying). The Ash'ari position is that such a burden is permissible for God to impose, although it has not occurred empirically, based on the Qur'anic verse (2:286) which indicates that God does not burden beyond capacity—implying that He could but does not. The Mu'tazila reject this due to ugliness. The passage then addresses an argument from some Ash'ari scholars about Abu Lahab: they said that he was commanded to believe, which includes believing that he would not believe, thus a command to combine contradictories. The author argues that this is not the real point of dispute because no one permits commanding a conceptual impossibility. An objection is raised: if the permissibility of commanding the intrinsically impossible depends on its conception, and some Ash'aris say it can be conceived, then they might permit it. The refutation clarifies that the Abu Lahab case is not about conceptual impossibility but about the first rank (impossibility due to divine knowledge). So the author's classification holds.

Key Terms
المرتبة الوسطى
القدرة الحادثة
عادة
خلق الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the Ash'aris permit commanding what is usually impossible?
A) Because it has occurred before.
B) Because God's command is not limited by human capacity.
C) Because the Quran implies God could but does not.
D) Because the Mu'tazila also permit it.

Key Takeaway
Usual impossibility is a real category: God could command it, but He does not, and the Mu'tazila wrongly deem it ugly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a command from Allah (e.g., in the Quran or through prophetic example) as 'impossible for me to follow' in the usual sense? What act have you labeled as 'too heavy' that Allah has not actually burdened you with, but which you could still strive toward with reliance on Him? Will you identify one such command today and commit to seeking Allah's help to approach it?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected the possibility of God commanding the usual impossible because they deemed it ugly. In your own life, do you ever resist submitting to a divine command because your human reasoning labels it as unwise or futile? Which command (e.g., patience in hardship, forgiving someone repeatedly) do you struggle to accept as potentially 'impossible' yet permissible for Allah to ask? How will you silence that reasoning and trust His wisdom?
?
If Allah can command what is usually impossible, does that fear or liberate you? Fear because you feel accountable beyond your capacity, or liberation because you realize that even if a duty seems overwhelming, Allah’s command is an opportunity for reliance, not a demand for self-sufficiency. Which feeling dominates your heart in the most difficult aspect of your deen, and how will you recalibrate your trust in Allah’s mercy?

Refuting the Abu Lahab Objection
Refutes the Abu Lahab argument as a proof for conceptual impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Abu Lahab argument does not prove divine command of conceptual impossibilities and how the author refutes it.

Core Idea
The Abu Lahab case involves knowledge-based impossibility, not conceptual contradiction.

Having established the Ash'ari position on usual impossibility, Sanusi now addresses a potential objection based on the case of Abu Lahab. Some scholars argued that commanding Abu Lahab to believe while knowing he will not is a command to combine contradictories. Sanusi shows this is a misunderstanding—it falls under the first rank of impossibility, not the middle or conceptual one.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage that presents the Abu Lahab argument and the author's response. Notice how the author first states the objection, then refutes it by distinguishing interpretations.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The objection only works if the command is interpreted as 'believe that you will not believe' (contradiction). But the correct interpretation is that God commanded faith despite knowing he would not believe, which is a command of something impossible due to divine knowledge, not conceptual impossibility.
3
Apply the lesson to the broader debate
Recognize that many arguments against the Ash'ari position mistakenly attack conceptual impossibility, when the real issue is about usual impossibility or knowledge-based impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Abu Lahab argument proves God can command conceptual impossibilities like combining belief and non-belief.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Abu Lahab argument as a proof for conceptual impossibility?
A) Because Abu Lahab was not a real person.
B) Because the command is based on God's knowledge, not a logical contradiction.
C) Because the Mu'tazila also accept it.
D) Because the Qur'an does not mention Abu Lahab.

The Abu Lahab argument is a red herring; it belongs to the first rank, not the dispute about conceptual impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a deep theological truth because you found a single seemingly contradictory example, like the opponents did with Abu Lahab? Are there areas in your faith where you cling to a surface-level objection instead of seeking the nuanced answer that Allah has provided? Resolve today to dig deeper into verses and hadeeth that initially confuse you, rather than letting them become an excuse for doubt.
?
The author distinguishes between what is impossible in itself and what is impossible due to Allah's knowledge. In your own life, do you sometimes claim something is 'impossible' when it is actually only difficult and within your ability, but you lack trust in Allah's decree? Are you using a false sense of helplessness to avoid obeying a clear command? Identify one command you have been neglecting under the excuse of impossibility, and take a concrete step to act on it this week.
?
You believe that Allah knows everything, yet you still feel the weight of your free choices. The lesson shows that a command can be sincere even when the outcome is known. How does this affect your understanding of personal accountability? When you sin, do you truly own your choice, or do you subtly blame Allah's foreknowledge? Pledge to never use qadr as a shield for your sins, and instead embrace the responsibility that comes with being commanded.

The Intrinsically Impossible: Its Conception and Knowledge
Defines the intrinsically impossible and how it is conceived

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intrinsically impossible cannot be conceived as existent and how it can be conceived negatively or by analogy.

Core Idea
The intrinsically impossible is known only by its negation, not as a real existent.

Before discussing whether God can impose an impossible task, we must clarify what 'impossible' means. The author begins by analyzing the intrinsically impossible, something whose very nature entails its non-existence. This passage shows that such a thing can only be conceived in two limited ways, and even that conception does not imply its possible occurrence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the later debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own explanation of the intrinsically impossible come through.
Notice the dense style: terms like 'quiddity' and 'conception' are central.
2
Identify the definition
Find the definition of the intrinsically impossible. The passage says it is something whose occurrence and existence are negated because its essence entails negation.
3
Note the two ways of conception
The passage gives two ways to conceive the intrinsically impossible: negatively and by analogy. Identify both and the example provided for each.
The example of a 'four that is not even' illustrates the point.
4
Understand the sufficiency for judgment
The author states that this type of conception is sufficient for judging about the impossible, but it does not entail its occurrence. See how Ibn Sina and Abu Hashim are cited in support.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why is it important that the impossible cannot be conceived as existent? How does this prepare the ground for the subsequent discussion on imposing tasks?
This connects to the learning objective.

المرصد السادس - المقصد السابع: جواز تكليف ما لا يطاق
وجه الوقوع والثبوت منتف (هاهنا أي في الممتنع لنفس مفهومه ( فإنه ) يستحيل تصوره ثابتا وذلك لأن ماهيته من حيث هي هي تقتضى انتفاءه وتصور الشيء على خلاف ما تقتضيه ذاته لذاته لا يكون تصوراً له بل لشيء آخر، كمن يتصور أربعة ليست بزوج، فإنه لا يكون متصوراً للأربعة قطعاً بل الممتنع لذاته (إنما يتصور) على أحد وجهين: (إما منفياً بمعنى أنه ليس لنا شيء موهوم أو محقق هو اجتماع الضدين أو بالتشبيه بمعنى أن يتصور اجتماع المتخالفين كالسواد والحلاوة، ثم يحكم بأن مثله لا يكون بين الضدين، وذلك ) أي تصوره على أحد هذين الوجهين كاف في الحكم عليه دون طلبه لأنه ( غير تصور وقوعه) وثبوته (ولا مستلزم له، صرح ابن سینا به ) أي بأن تصوره كذلك كما نقلناه عنه ه في باب العلم ( ولعله معنى قول أبي هاشم العلم بالمستحيل علم لا معلوم (له كما أشرنا إليه هناك أيضاً (و) لعله مراد) من قال : المستحيل لا يعلم أي لا يعلم من حيث ذاته وماهيته. المرتبة الوسطى ( من مراتب ما لا يطاق ) أن لا يتعلق به القدرة الحادثة عادة سواء امتنع تعلقها به لا لنفس مفهومه بأن لا يكون من جنس ما تتعلق به ( كخلق الأجسام) فإن القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلق بإيجاد الجوهر أصلا ( أم لا ) بأن يكون من ما تتعلق به لكن يكون من نوع أو صنف لا تتعلق به ) كحمل الجبل والطيران إلى السماء (فهذا) أي التكليف بما لا يطاق عادة ( نجوزه) نحن ( وإن لم يقع بالاستقراء ولقوله تعالى : ولا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٨٦]، وتمنعه المعتزلة ) لكونه قبيحاً عندهم ( وبه ) أي بما ذكرناه من التفصيل وتحرير المتنازع فيه ( يعلم أن كثيراً من أدلة أصحابنا مثل ما قالوه في إيمان أبي لهب وكونه مأموراً بالجمع بين المتناقضين ( نصب للدليل في غير محل النزاع إذ لم يجوزه أحد، ولقائل أن يقول : ما ذكره من أن جواز التكليف بالممتنع لذاته فرع تصوره وإن بعضاً منا قالوا بوقوع تصوره يشعر بأن هؤلاء يجوزونه.
جنس
قوله : ( ولقائل أن يقول : ما ذكره إلخ يرد عليه أن هذا الاعتراض إنما يتوجه على ما حمل هو كلام المصنف عليه من أن المراد بما قالوا في إيمان أبي لهب : إنه مكلف بأن يؤمن بجملة ما أتى به النبي عليه السلام ومن جملتها أنه لا يؤمن فيكون تكليفاً بالجمع بين المتناقضين وأما إذا حمل على أن المراد بما قالوه أنه تعالى علم عدم إيمانه وأخبر به ومع هذا كلفه بالإيمان فيكون تكليفاً بمالا يطاق، فصح قول المصنف أنه ليس بمحل النزاع لأنه من القسم الأول ولم يرد عليه
ما أورده .
The Sixth Observatory - The Seventh Purpose: The Permissibility of Imposing What Cannot Be Borne. The aspect of occurrence and existence is negated (here, in the impossible by its own concept (for it) is impossible to conceive it as existent, because its quiddity as such entails its negation, and conceiving a thing contrary to what its essence requires is not a conception of it but of something else, like one who conceives of a four that is not even, then he is certainly not conceiving of the four. Rather, the impossible by itself (is conceived) in one of two ways: (either negatively, meaning that we have no imagined or real thing that is the conjunction of two opposites, or by analogy, meaning that one conceives of the conjunction of two conflictive things like blackness and sweetness, then judges that the like of that does not occur between opposites. And that) i.e., its conception in one of these two ways is sufficient for judging about it without seeking it, because it (is not a conception of its occurrence) and existence (nor does it entail that. Ibn Sina explicitly stated this) i.e., that its conception is such as we have transmitted from him in the chapter on knowledge. (And perhaps this is the meaning of Abu Hashim's statement: 'Knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without a known object' as we also indicated there. (And) perhaps it is the intent) of those who said: 'The impossible is not known,' i.e., not known in terms of its essence and quiddity. The middle rank (of the ranks of what cannot be borne) is that created power does not pertain to it habitually, whether its non-attachment is not due to its own concept, as in (creating bodies) — for created power does not pertain to the creation of a substance at all — (or whether) it is of what power does pertain to but is of a type or species to which it does not pertain (like carrying a mountain and flying to the sky. So this) i.e., imposing what is habitually unbearable (we permit) we (even if it has not occurred by induction and due to His saying: 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' [2:286], and the Mu'tazila forbid it) because it is ugly according to them. (And by it) i.e., by what we have mentioned of detail and clarification of the point of dispute (it is known that many of the proofs of our companions, like what they said about the faith of Abu Lahab and his being commanded to combine two contradictories (are setting up a proof in a non-locus of dispute, since no one permits it. And one may say: What he mentioned — that the permissibility of imposing the intrinsically impossible is contingent on its conception, and that some of us said it occurs in conception — suggests that these people permit it. Gender? His statement: ('And one may say: what he mentioned etc.') The reply to it is that this objection is only directed at what he interpreted the author's speech to mean: that what they said about the faith of Abu Lahab is that he is obligated to believe in everything the Prophet brought, including that he will not believe, so it is imposing a combination of contradictories. But if it is interpreted to mean that they said: God knew he would not believe and informed of it, and yet obligated him to believe, then it becomes imposing what cannot be borne. So the author's statement that it is not a locus of dispute is correct, because it is of the first category, and the objection he raised does not apply to it.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the concept of 'impossible to bear' (تكليف ما لا يطاق) and the Ash'ari position on it. The author begins by distinguishing the intrinsically impossible (الممتنع لذاته), whose occurrence and existence are impossible. He explains such a thing cannot be conceived as existent because its essence entails its negation; to conceive it as other than it is would be to conceive something else. He gives the example of someone trying to conceive of a four that is not even. Then he states that the intrinsically impossible can only be conceived in two ways: either negatively (conceiving the negation of the conjunction of opposites) or by analogy (conceiving a conjunction of disparate qualities and then judging that the like does not exist between opposites). This type of conception suffices for judgment about it but does not entail conceiving its actual occurrence. He cites Ibn Sina and Abu Hashim to reinforce that knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without a known object. Next, he moves to the middle rank of what cannot be borne: that which created power habitually does not affect, either because it is not of the genus (e.g., creating a substance) or because it is of a type beyond normal capacity (e.g., carrying a mountain). The Ash'ari permit imposing such tasks, even if not observed, citing the verse 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' to show that God can do so, while the Mu'tazila forbid it as ugly. The author then notes that many Ash'ari proofs—such as the example of Abu Lahab being commanded to believe while his unbelief was decreed—are not relevant to the real dispute, since no one permits the intrinsically impossible. An objection is raised: saying that permissibility of imposing the intrinsically impossible depends on its conception, and some Ash'aris affirm its conception, suggests that they do permit it. The answer clarifies that the objection only applies to one interpretation; if the Ash'ari claim about Abu Lahab is understood as imposing belief while knowing he will not believe, it falls under habitually impossible, not intrinsically impossible. Thus the author's point stands.

Key Terms
ممتنع لذاته
تصور
اجتماع الضدين
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the intrinsically impossible (الممتنع لذاته) conceived?
A) As an actual existent in the mind
B) Negatively or by analogy, not as an existent
C) Only through scriptural revelation
D) It cannot be conceived at all

Key Takeaway
The intrinsically impossible can only be conceived through negation or analogy, and such conception does not imply its possible occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim something is impossible in your own life—like changing a bad habit or forgiving someone—are you certain that your conception is based on the thing's true essence, or are you merely using a flawed analogy? This passage teaches that true impossibility is rooted in the nature of the thing itself. Have you ever dismissed a difficult task as impossible without honestly examining its true nature? What would it take for you to reconsider?
?
This lesson shows that we cannot conceive the impossible as it truly is; we only conceive it via negation or analogy. How often do you mistake your mental images of difficult concepts or commands from Allah as accurate representations, when in reality you have only formed a shadow? Will you commit to seeking genuine knowledge—especially of what Allah has commanded—rather than relying on cheap analogies that lead you to think some things are impossible for you to achieve?

Rational Judgments

Imposing the Habitually Impossible: The Real Dispute
Defines the middle rank of impossible tasks and clarifies the dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view on imposing what is habitually impossible, how it differs from the Mu'tazila objection, and the clarification regarding the Abu Lahab example.

Core Idea
God may impose what is habitually impossible, but not what is intrinsically impossible.

Having clarified the intrinsically impossible, the author now turns to the middle rank: what created power cannot normally do. The Ash'ari permit God to impose such tasks, while the Mu'tazila forbid it. The passage also addresses a common misunderstanding using the example of Abu Lahab's unbelief, showing that the real dispute is not about intrinsically impossible commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the middle rank
Read the Arabic passage for the middle rank and its translation. Notice the two types of habitual impossibility: something not of the genus (like creating bodies) and something beyond normal power (like carrying a mountain).
2
Identify the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions
The passage states that the Ash'ari permit imposing such tasks, citing the Quranic verse, while the Mu'tazila forbid it as ugly. Mark these two positions.
The Ash'ari rely on the verse 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' to argue that He can, but does not necessarily.
3
Understand the clarification with Abu Lahab
The author says many Ash'ari proofs, like the one about Abu Lahab, are misapplied. Read how the passage distinguishes between two interpretations: one that involves commanding belief in everything including his own unbelief (which would be intrinsically impossible), and another that merely involves commanding belief despite foreknowledge (which is habitually impossible).
The objection raised is answered by showing the correct interpretation falls under the first category, not the disputed one.
4
Reflect on the real point of dispute
Ask yourself: what is the actual disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila? It is about habitually impossible tasks, not intrinsically impossible ones. Why is this distinction important?
This connects to the learning objective.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari deny God can impose any impossible task.
Correction: The Ash'ari affirm that God can impose what is habitually impossible, but not what is intrinsically impossible. The misconception arises from conflating these two categories.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on imposing what is habitually impossible (تكليف ما لا يطاق عادة)?
A) They forbid it
B) They permit it
C) They are silent
D) They only permit it if it occurs

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari permit God to impose what is habitually impossible, but not what is intrinsically impossible; the real dispute with the Mu'tazila centers on the former.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson distinguishes between what is intrinsically impossible and what is merely habitually impossible. Are there areas in your life where you have given up on a command of God, convincing yourself it's impossible, when in reality you were only facing habitual difficulty? What would change if you honestly assessed whether the task is truly impossible or just beyond your normal capacity?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that burdening beyond capacity is 'ugly' and incompatible with God's justice. Have you ever felt resentment toward God for asking you to do something that seemed too hard? How does the Ash'ari position—that God may command what is habitually impossible but does so justly—challenge your assumptions about fairness and trust in His wisdom?
?
The author criticizes those who misapply the Abu Lahab proof to the wrong type of impossibility. Reflect on a time you misapplied a theological principle to justify your own inaction or to avoid a difficult responsibility. What would it mean for you to correctly identify the real nature of the challenge you face?

Rational Arguments for Angel Superiority
The author presents two rational arguments for angelic superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments presented by some theologians for the superiority of angels over prophets: their extraordinary power and their comprehensive knowledge.

Core Idea
Angels are argued to be superior due to their power and knowledge.

In the ongoing debate about the relative rank of prophets and angels, some theologians have advanced rational arguments for the superiority of angels. Two such arguments are presented here: one based on the angels' ability to perform difficult acts without fatigue, and another based on their comprehensive knowledge of past, present, and future. These arguments draw on Qur'anic verses and philosophical notions of abstract beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth argument about power
Examine how the author describes angels' power over earthquakes and clouds, and their immunity to fatigue.
Note the Qur'anic support.
2
Read the sixth argument about knowledge
Understand the claim that angels have universal, actual, and innate knowledge.
3
Ask: What makes these arguments rational?
Consider the underlying assumptions about the nature of angels as abstract spiritual beings.
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : تفضيل الأنبياء على الملائكة
الأنوار الربانية ) والجسمانيات مركبة من المادة والصورة والمادة ظلمانية مانعة ) عن تلك المشاهدة المستمرة (الخامس الروحانيات قوية على أفعال شاقة كالزلازل والسحب ) فإن الزلازل توجد بتحريكاتها والسحاب يعرض ويزول بتصريفاتها، والآثار العلوية تحدث بمعوناتها، وقد نطق به الكتاب الكريم حيث قال : فالمقسمات أمراً ﴾ [الذاريات : ٤ ] ، وقال : فالمدبرات أمراً ﴾ [النازعات : ٥ ]، ( لا يلحقها بذلك فتور لأن قدرتهم على تغيير الأجسام وتقليب الأجرام وتحريكاتها ليست من جنس القوى المزاجية حتى يعرض لها كلال ولغوب بخلاف الجسمانيات ) . ( السادس : الروحانيات أعلم لإحاطتها بما كان في الأعصر الأول وبما سيكون في الأزمنة الآتية وبالأمور الغائبة عنا في الحال ) وعلومهم كلية)، إذ لا حواس لهم ترتسم فيها المثل الجزئية (فعلية) لأنها مباد للحوادث في عالم الكون والفساد ( فطرية)، أي حاصلة في ابتداء فطرتهم لكونها مجردة بريئة عن القوة آمنة من الغلط، والجسمانيات بخلافه والجواب أن ذلك كله مبني على القواعد الفلسفية التي لا نسلمها ولا نقول بها على أن النزاع من المتكلمين في الأفضلية بمعنى كثرة الثواب فتدبر . 
قوله: (الروحانيات قوية إلخ) لا يخفى أن هذا الوجه وكذا بعض الوجوه المذكورة منقوضة بالملائكة الأرضية التي لا نزاع في فضل الأنبياء عليهم السلام . قوله : مبنى القواعد (الفلسفية فإن الملائكة عندنا أجسام لطيفة لا من قبيل المجردات وكون كمالاتهم كلها بالفعل ممنوع ، وأيضاً علمهم بالكوائن ماضيها وآتيها غير مسلم . قوله : ( فتدبر) أي لتطلع على أن ما ذكر من الوجوه على تقدير تمامها لا يدل على أفضليتهم بمعنى كثيرة ثوابهم .
The First Observatory - The Eighth Objective: The Preference of Prophets over Angels. [Fourth:] The divine lights, and corporeal beings are composed of matter and form, and matter is darkness preventing from that continuous witnessing. Fifth: Spiritual beings are powerful over difficult acts like earthquakes and clouds. For earthquakes occur by their movements, and clouds appear and disappear by their dispositions, and the higher effects occur by their aids, and the Noble Book has spoken of them where He said: "And those who distribute [things] by command" [al-Dhariyat: 4], and said: "And those who manage [things] by command" [al-Nazi'at: 5]; they are not affected by fatigue because their power to change bodies and overturn objects and move them is not of the kind of temperamental powers so that weariness and exhaustion would occur to them, unlike corporeal beings. Sixth: Spiritual beings are more knowledgeable because they encompass what was in the first ages and what will be in future times and matters hidden from us at present, and their sciences are universal, for they have no senses in which particular forms are imprinted; they are actual because they are principles of events in the world of generation and corruption; they are innate, i.e., obtained at the beginning of their creation because they are abstract, free from potentiality, safe from error, unlike corporeal beings. The answer is that all of that is built upon philosophical principles which we do not affirm nor adopt, although the dispute among the theologians is about superiority in the sense of abundance of reward, so ponder. [Commentator says:] His statement: "Spiritual beings are powerful, etc." It is not hidden that this aspect and some of the mentioned aspects are invalidated by the earthly angels concerning whom there is no dispute about the superiority of prophets, peace be upon them. His statement: built upon philosophical principles; for angels with us are subtle bodies, not of the category of abstract entities, and that all their perfections are actual is denied, and also their knowledge of past and future events is not granted. His statement: "so ponder" means that you may realize that what was mentioned of the aspects, even if complete, does not indicate their superiority in the sense of abundance of their reward.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents two rational arguments for the superiority of angels over prophets, followed by a refutation and then a commentator's remarks. The first argument (fifth point) states that spiritual beings (angels) are powerful over difficult acts like earthquakes and clouds, as evidenced by Qur'anic verses, and they do not experience fatigue, unlike corporeal beings. The second argument (sixth point) states that spiritual beings are more knowledgeable because they encompass past, present, and future, and their knowledge is universal, actual, and innate, free from error. The author then answers that these arguments are based on philosophical principles that the theologian does not accept, noting that the dispute among theologians is about superiority in terms of reward, not these attributes. The commentator then clarifies: the argument is invalidated by the existence of earthly angels who are not superior to prophets; angels in the Islamic view are subtle bodies, not abstract entities; their knowledge of the unseen is not granted; and even if the arguments were sound, they do not prove superiority in reward.

Key Terms
الروحانيات
الجسمانيات
الأفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that these rational arguments are accepted as definitive proof of angel superiority.
Correction: The passage is presenting arguments that will later be refuted; they are not the author's own position.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a characteristic of angels according to the sixth argument?
A) Their knowledge is universal.
B) Their knowledge is actual.
C) Their knowledge is acquired.
D) Their knowledge is innate.

Key Takeaway
The two rational arguments for angel superiority are based on their extraordinary power and comprehensive knowledge, as articulated by some theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The angels are described as never experiencing fatigue in their tasks. In what area of your worship or duty do you most easily tire or give up? What would change in your life if you approached that task with the same tireless consistency as the angels? Commit to one specific action this week to increase your endurance.
?
Angelic knowledge is actual, innate, and free from error. Your knowledge is often theoretical and prone to mistakes. Where in your life do you rely on your own limited understanding instead of seeking Allah's guidance through revelation? What is one belief or assumption you hold that you have never tested against the Qur'an and Sunnah? Make a decision today to investigate it.
?
The passage shows that angels are agents of immense power, controlling earthquakes and clouds. Do you truly believe that every natural event is under Allah's control via His angels, or do you sometimes feel that things happen randomly? How would your fear or anxiety about future events change if you internalized this truth? Write down one worry you will consciously entrust to Allah today.

Refuting the Arguments: Philosophical Foundations and True Superiority
The author refutes the arguments and redefines the criterion for superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the rational arguments for angel superiority and the clarification that the dispute is about abundance of reward.

Core Idea
The arguments are built on unaccepted philosophy; true superiority is measured by reward.

Having presented the rational arguments for angel superiority, the author now delivers his response. He dismisses the arguments as resting on philosophical principles that theologians do not accept. Furthermore, he reframes the entire debate: the real issue is not about inherent attributes but about who receives greater reward from Allah. The commentator adds further criticisms, pointing out that the arguments are invalidated by the existence of earthly angels and that angels are not abstract entities in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's answer
Notice how he says the arguments are built on philosophical principles that we do not accept, and he redirects the debate to reward.
2
Analyze the commentator's remarks
The commentator points out three flaws: earthly angels disprove the power/knowledge claims; angels are not abstract; and even if arguments were sound, they don't prove reward superiority.
This strengthens the refutation.
3
Understand the key shift
The author moves the discussion from attributes to reward—this is the core theological perspective.
