The Ash'ari Proof for Restoration
Presents the Ash'ari proof with a conditional argument and the principle of essence invariance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that if a thing's second existence were impossible, it would have been impossible from the outset, and thus its possibility follows from its actual initial existence.

Core Idea
Essential properties do not change over time; existence once proves existence again possible.

The Ash'ari theologian begins by presenting the rational argument for the possibility of restoring a non-existent thing. This proof relies on a fundamental principle about invariance of essence over time. The learner will walk through the logical steps of this foundational kalam argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noticing the conditional structure. Understand each clause.
The key phrase is 'لا يختلف بحسب الأزمنة'.
2
Identify the logical flow
The argument moves from assuming impossibility to concluding possibility. Trace the 'if... then' logic.
3
Apply the principle
Consider why essential properties must be timeless. This principle is central to the proof.
This is a core premise of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أنه لا يمتنع وجوده الثاني لذاته ولا للوازمه وإلا لم يوجد ابتداء، بل كان من قبيل الممتنعات لأن مقتضى ذات الشيء أو لوازمه لا يختلف بحسب الأزمنة، وإذا لم يمتنع كذلك كان ممكناً بالنظر إلى ذاته. فإن قيل: العود لكونه وجوداً حاصلاً بعد طريان العدم أخص من الوجود المطلق ولا يلزم من إمكان الأعم إمكان الأخص ولا من امتناع الأخص امتناع الأعم فجاز أن يمتنع وجوده بعد عدمه إما لذاته أو لازمه ولا يمتنع وجوده مطلقا. قلنا: الوجود أمر واحد في حد ذاته لا يختلف ذلك الواحد ابتداء وإعادة بحسب حقيقته وذاته بل بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج عن ماهيته وهو الزمان، وكذلك الإيجاد أمر واحد لا يختلف ابتداء وإعادة إلا بحسب تلك الإضافة فإذن يتلازمان، أي الوجودان المبدأ والمعاد وكذا الإيجادان إمكاناً ووجوباً وامتناعاً لأن الأشياء المتوافقة في المعية يجب اشتراكها في هذه الأمور المستندة إلى ذواتها لا وهو المطلوب.
For us (the proof): its second existence is not impossible in itself nor in its concomitants; otherwise it would not have existed initially, but would have been among impossibilities, because what is required by the essence of a thing or its concomitants does not differ according to times. And if it is not impossible in that way, then it is possible with respect to its essence. If it is said: the return, being an existence occurring after the occurrence of non-existence, is more specific than absolute existence, and the possibility of the general does not necessitate the possibility of the specific, nor does the impossibility of the specific necessitate the impossibility of the general, so it is possible that its existence after its non-existence is impossible either in itself or in its concomitant while its absolute existence is not impossible. We say: existence is one thing in its very self; that one does not differ in initiation and return with respect to its reality and essence, but only with respect to an addition to something external to its quiddity, namely time. Likewise, bringing into existence is one thing; it does not differ in initiation and return except with respect to that addition. Therefore, they entail each other, that is, the two existences (the initial and the return) and likewise the two acts of bringing into existence, in possibility, necessity, and impossibility, because things that agree in essence must share in these matters that are based upon their essences. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof (lana) for the possibility of restoring the non-existent. The argument is that if the second existence of a thing were impossible in itself or in its necessary concomitants, then it would have been impossible from the beginning, and thus could not have existed initially. But since the thing did exist initially, the impossibility must be ruled out. Therefore, the second existence is possible with respect to its essence. The proof relies on the principle that essential properties do not change over time. Then the author presents an objection (fa-in qīla): the return is a specific existence (after non-existence), and the possibility of the general (absolute existence) does not imply the possibility of the specific (restored existence). So it could be that the specific existence after non-existence is impossible even though absolute existence is possible. The response (qulnā) is that existence is one in its essence; the difference between initial existence and restored existence is merely a relational difference (adding time). Therefore, if existence is possible in one case, it is possible in the other. The same applies to the act of bringing into existence. Hence, the two existences and the two acts of bringing into existence entail each other in possibility, necessity, and impossibility because they share the same essence. This is the desired conclusion (wa huwa al-maṭlūb).

Key Terms
الوجود
الإمكان
الامتناع
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the Ash'ari proof for the possibility of restoring the non-existent?
A) The non-existent can be restored because it is a thing.
B) Essential properties of a thing do not change over time.
C) The second existence is impossible in itself.
D) The objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof for the possibility of restoring the non-existent rests on the invariance of essential properties over time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have believed that Allah created you once. Do you truly believe with the same certainty that He will restore you after death? What in your daily actions reveals doubt about the constancy of His power?
?
Consider the invariant essence of your soul as a creation of Allah. If your essence does not change over time, then your accountability before Him remains constant. Does your sense of moral obligation change with circumstances, or do you hold yourself to the same standard regardless of time and place?
?
You affirm the logical principle that essential properties do not change. Yet, have you allowed temporary circumstances to make you think that your relationship with your Lord can be permanently altered? How can you anchor your certainty in the unchanging nature of His decree?

Objection: Specificity of Restored Existence
Presents the objection that restored existence is more specific and not entailed by the general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the possibility of absolute existence does not entail the possibility of a specific existence after non-existence.

Core Idea
General possibility does not imply specific possibility.

Having presented the proof, the text now turns to an objection. The objector argues that the restored existence is a specific kind of existence, distinct from absolute existence, and thus its possibility does not follow from the possibility of absolute existence. This is a crucial point in understanding the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Pay attention to the concepts of 'general' (الأعم) and 'specific' (الأخص).
The objector uses a logical principle.
2
Analyze the distinction
The objection hinges on the fact that 'return' is existence after non-existence, which is a specific case.
3
Consider the implications
If the objection holds, the Ash'ari proof would be insufficient. Think about why.
This sets up the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أنه لا يمتنع وجوده الثاني لذاته ولا للوازمه وإلا لم يوجد ابتداء، بل كان من قبيل الممتنعات لأن مقتضى ذات الشيء أو لوازمه لا يختلف بحسب الأزمنة، وإذا لم يمتنع كذلك كان ممكناً بالنظر إلى ذاته. فإن قيل: العود لكونه وجوداً حاصلاً بعد طريان العدم أخص من الوجود المطلق ولا يلزم من إمكان الأعم إمكان الأخص ولا من امتناع الأخص امتناع الأعم فجاز أن يمتنع وجوده بعد عدمه إما لذاته أو لازمه ولا يمتنع وجوده مطلقا. قلنا: الوجود أمر واحد في حد ذاته لا يختلف ذلك الواحد ابتداء وإعادة بحسب حقيقته وذاته بل بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج عن ماهيته وهو الزمان، وكذلك الإيجاد أمر واحد لا يختلف ابتداء وإعادة إلا بحسب تلك الإضافة فإذن يتلازمان، أي الوجودان المبدأ والمعاد وكذا الإيجادان إمكاناً ووجوباً وامتناعاً لأن الأشياء المتوافقة في المعية يجب اشتراكها في هذه الأمور المستندة إلى ذواتها لا وهو المطلوب.

And if it is not impossible in that way, then it is possible with respect to its essence. Therefore, they entail each other, that is, the two existences (the initial and the return) and likewise the two acts of bringing into existence, in possibility, necessity, and impossibility, because things that agree in essence must share in these matters that are based upon their essences. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof (lana) for the possibility of restoring the non-existent. The argument is that if the second existence of a thing were impossible in itself or in its necessary concomitants, then it would have been impossible from the beginning, and thus could not have existed initially. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been content with a proof for a belief that you later realized was insufficient? How does this objection challenge you to examine the grounds of your own faith in resurrection?
?
If the possibility of general existence does not guarantee the specific case of your own return, on what basis do you personally rely for certainty about your fate after death?
?
This objection shows that logical possibilities alone are not enough. What steps have you taken to ensure that the specific possibility of your own restoration is not just possible but certain in your heart?

Response: Existence is One in Essence
Presents the Ash'ari response that existence is one in essence, so possibility is shared.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response that existence in initiation and return is identical in essence, so their possibilities are linked.

Core Idea
Existence is one in essence; time does not affect its reality.

The Ash'ari theologian now responds to the objection. He argues that existence itself is a single reality; the difference between initial existence and restored existence is merely external (time). Therefore, if existence is possible in one case, it must be possible in the other. This refutes the objection and completes the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Notice the emphasis on existence being 'one thing in its very self' (أمر واحد في حد ذاته).
This is the central claim.
2
Understand the role of time
The response says that the difference between initiation and return is only due to time, which is external to the essence.
Time does not change the essence.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since the essence is the same, the two existences share in possibility, necessity, and impossibility.
This refutes the objection and proves the desired conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أنه لا يمتنع وجوده الثاني لذاته ولا للوازمه وإلا لم يوجد ابتداء، بل كان من قبيل الممتنعات لأن مقتضى ذات الشيء أو لوازمه لا يختلف بحسب الأزمنة، وإذا لم يمتنع كذلك كان ممكناً بالنظر إلى ذاته. فإن قيل: العود لكونه وجوداً حاصلاً بعد طريان العدم أخص من الوجود المطلق ولا يلزم من إمكان الأعم إمكان الأخص ولا من امتناع الأخص امتناع الأعم فجاز أن يمتنع وجوده بعد عدمه إما لذاته أو لازمه ولا يمتنع وجوده مطلقا. قلنا: الوجود أمر واحد في حد ذاته لا يختلف ذلك الواحد ابتداء وإعادة بحسب حقيقته وذاته بل بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج عن ماهيته وهو الزمان، وكذلك الإيجاد أمر واحد لا يختلف ابتداء وإعادة إلا بحسب تلك الإضافة فإذن يتلازمان، أي الوجودان المبدأ والمعاد وكذا الإيجادان إمكاناً ووجوباً وامتناعاً لأن الأشياء المتوافقة في المعية يجب اشتراكها في هذه الأمور المستندة إلى ذواتها لا وهو المطلوب.
For us (the proof): its second existence is not impossible in itself nor in its concomitants; otherwise it would not have existed initially, but would have been among impossibilities, because what is required by the essence of a thing or its concomitants does not differ according to times. And if it is not impossible in that way, then it is possible with respect to its essence. If it is said: the return, being an existence occurring after the occurrence of non-existence, is more specific than absolute existence, and the possibility of the general does not necessitate the possibility of the specific, nor does the impossibility of the specific necessitate the impossibility of the general, so it is possible that its existence after its non-existence is impossible either in itself or in its concomitant while its absolute existence is not impossible. We say: existence is one thing in its very self; that one does not differ in initiation and return with respect to its reality and essence, but only with respect to an addition to something external to its quiddity, namely time. Likewise, bringing into existence is one thing; it does not differ in initiation and return except with respect to that addition. Therefore, they entail each other, that is, the two existences (the initial and the return) and likewise the two acts of bringing into existence, in possibility, necessity, and impossibility, because things that agree in essence must share in these matters that are based upon their essences. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof (lana) for the possibility of restoring the non-existent. The argument is that if the second existence of a thing were impossible in itself or in its necessary concomitants, then it would have been impossible from the beginning, and thus could not have existed initially. But since the thing did exist initially, the impossibility must be ruled out. Therefore, the second existence is possible with respect to its essence. The proof relies on the principle that essential properties do not change over time. Then the author presents an objection (fa-in qīla): the return is a specific existence (after non-existence), and the possibility of the general (absolute existence) does not imply the possibility of the specific (restored existence). So it could be that the specific existence after non-existence is impossible even though absolute existence is possible. The response (qulnā) is that existence is one in its essence; the difference between initial existence and restored existence is merely a relational difference (adding time). Therefore, if existence is possible in one case, it is possible in the other. The same applies to the act of bringing into existence. Hence, the two existences and the two acts of bringing into existence entail each other in possibility, necessity, and impossibility because they share the same essence. This is the desired conclusion (wa huwa al-maṭlūb).

Key Terms
الوجود
الذات
الإضافة
الزمان
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that restoration is a different kind of existence and thus its possibility is a separate issue.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that existence is one in essence, so the possibility of initial existence applies equally to restored existence.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what makes initiation and return different?
A) Their essences are different.
B) They differ only in relation to time.
C) The return is impossible.
D) They differ in terms of possibility.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response establishes that existence's essential unity ensures that the possibility of initial existence entails the possibility of restoration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is one in essence and time only an external relation, then the 'you' that will be resurrected is exactly the same as the 'you' now. What specific thought or action today are you most afraid of having eternally attached to that essence?
?
You often treat your future self as a separate person, but this lesson says existence is the same. In what areas of life do you most feel that distance—procrastination, repentance, or hope—and how would your daily choices change if you truly believed the same essence continues unchanged?
?
When doubts about resurrection come to mind, is it because you imagine God's power limited or because you feel your existence is too fragile to be repeated? Based on this passage, where does the real obstacle lie, and what does your doubt reveal about your understanding of existence?

Critique of the Previous Response and Restatement of the Objection
Critique of the previous response and restatement of the objector's position on qualified existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise nature of the objection regarding qualified existence and why the previous response fails to address it.

Core Idea
Impossibility concerns absolute non-existence, not qualified existence.

The previous response argued that existence is one and therefore the restored existence is the same as the initial. This lesson presents the objector's critique: that argument misses the point because the objection is about a specific qualified existence, not absolute existence. You will see how the objector refines the problem and why the responder's argument is irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its critique
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله: (قلنا: الوجود أمر واحد إلخ)' to 'ولا ينافي هذا...' along with the translation. Follow the dialectical exchange.
Notice that the critic does not dispute the truth of the previous statement but its relevance.
2
Identify the distinction between absolute and qualified existence
The objector argues that the previous response conflates absolute existence with existence qualified by being after non-existence. Understand why this distinction is crucial.
3
Understand why the previous response is irrelevant
The author's argument about existence being one does not address the objector's concern about the specific qualifier. The objector does not deny that existence in itself is one; he denies that possibility transfers to the qualified case.
This shows the subtlety of the dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (قلنا: الوجود أمر واحد إلخ) أورد عليه أنه لا نزاع في حقية هذا المقال لكن لا أثر له في دفع السؤال إذ لا شك أن الامتناع اقتضاء مطلق العدم لا العدم المخصوص ألا يرى أن العدم المسبوق بالوجود لا يمكن أن يتصف به الممتنع فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الوجوب عبارة عن اقتضاء مطلق الوجود لا الوجود المخصوص ألا يرى أن الوجود المسبوق بالعدم لا يمكن أن يتصف به الواجب فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الإمكان عبارة عن لا اقتضائهما مطلقين وقد تقرر أنه يجوز الانقلاب بين هذه المفهومات. وإذا تمهد هذا فنقول: مقصود المعترض أن العود ليس وجوداً مطلقاً على أي وجه كان بل هو وجود مقيد بكونه بعد العدم فلم لا يجوز أن يمتنع اتصاف ماهية المعدوم بهذا الوجود المقيد ولا يمتنع اتصافه بالوجود المطلق من غير لزوم انقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي. فقول المصنف: ولو جوزنا كون الشيء الواحد إلخ لا تعلق له بكلام المعترض لأنه لا يقول بهذا التجويز ولا يلزمه أيضاً ذلك. وكذا قوله: الوجود أمر واحد إلى قوله: ولو جوزنا لأن حاصله أن الوجود المعاد إذا اقتضى لذاته أمراً يجب أن يقتضى الوجود المبتدأ أيضاً لذاته ذلك الأمر بعينه وبالعكس لأنهما متحدان ذاتاً وحقيقة إنما اختلافهما بحسب أمر خارج فالمعترض لم يقل بخلاف ذلك ولم يلزم أيضاً من كلامه خلافه، بل اللازم من كلامه أن الوجود في المبدأ والمعاد متغايران بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج فيجوز أن يقتضي ماهية المعدوم لذاته عدم الاتصاف بأحدهما ولا يقتضي عدم الاتصاف بالآخر. ولا ينافي هذا أن يجوز أن يقتضي أحد الوجودين لذاته أمراً ولا يقتضيه الوجود الآخر. هذا وقد يقال في إتمام دليل إمكان الإعادة: لا شبهة أن اتصاف ذات الممكن بالوجود المطلق غير ممتنع فإذا امتنع اتصافها بالوجود المسبوق بالعدم المسبوق بالوجود لكان هذا الامتناع ناشئاً إما من أحد القيدين أو كليهما لكنا نعلم أن المسبوقية بالعدم لا تكون منشأ لهذا الامتناع وإلا لم يتصف بالحدوث، وكذا المسبوقية بالوجود وإلا لما اتصف بالبقاء على أن الوجود السابق إن لم يفد زيادة استعداد الاتصاف بالوجود فمعلوم بالضرورة أنها لا تقتضي منع ما هو عليه بالذات من قابلية الوجود في الأوقات وكذا نعلم بالضرورة أن لا أثر لاجتماعهما في هذا الامتناع فاتصافها بالوجود المقيد بهذين القيدين أعني العود غير ممتنع. والله أعلم.
His saying: 'We say: existence is one thing, etc.' It was objected against it that there is no dispute about the truth of this statement, but it has no effect in repelling the question, for there is no doubt that impossibility is a requirement of absolute non-existence, not of particular non-existence. Do you not see that non-existence preceded by existence cannot be attributed to the impossible, let alone be required by it? Likewise, necessity is an expression of requirement of absolute existence, not of particular existence. Do you not see that existence preceded by non-existence cannot be attributed to the necessary, let alone be required by it? Likewise, possibility is an expression of the lack of requirement of either of them absolutely, and it has been established that change (inqlāb) among these concepts is permissible. And when this is established, we say: the intention of the objector is that the return is not absolute existence in any way whatever, but rather it is existence qualified by being after non-existence. So why is it not permissible that the attribution of this qualified existence to the quiddity of the non-existent be impossible, while its attribution to absolute existence is not impossible, without necessitating a change from essential possibility to essential impossibility? So the author's saying: 'And if we allow that a single thing, etc.' has no connection to the objector's statement, because he does not say this allowance, nor does it follow from him. Likewise, his saying: 'Existence is one thing' up to his saying: 'And if we allow' because its summary is that the restored existence, if it requires something in itself, then the initial existence must also require that same thing in itself, and vice versa, because they are united in essence and reality; their difference is only in an external matter. The objector did not say contrary to that, nor does the opposite follow from his statement. Rather, what follows from his statement is that existence in the beginning and the return are different according to the addition of an external matter, so it is possible that the quiddity of the non-existent requires in itself the lack of attribution to one of them but does not require the lack of attribution to the other. And this does not contradict that it is possible that one of the two existences requires in itself something while the other existence does not require it. Now it may be said in completing the proof of the possibility of restoration: there is no doubt that the attribution of the possible quiddity to absolute existence is not impossible. So if its attribution to existence preceded by non-existence preceded by existence were impossible, this impossibility would arise either from one of the two qualifiers or from both. But we know that being preceded by non-existence cannot be the source of this impossibility, otherwise it would not be attributed to origination (ḥudūth). Likewise, being preceded by existence, otherwise it would not be attributed to permanence (baqā'). Moreover, the previous existence, if it does not confer an increase of preparedness for the attribution of existence, then it is known by necessity that it does not necessitate prevention of what is in itself a receptivity to existence at times. And likewise we know by necessity that there is no effect of their combination in this impossibility. Therefore, its attribution to existence qualified by these two qualifiers, i.e., the return, is not impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical discussion. It first presents a criticism (qīla) of the previous response. The critic argues that the earlier answer, while true in itself, fails to refute the objection. The objection is that impossibility concerns absolute non-existence, not non-existence qualified by being preceded by existence. The objector's point is that 'return' is a specific existence (after non-existence), and it is possible that this specific existence is impossible for the quiddity even if absolute existence is possible. The author's previous argument that existence is one does not address this because the objector does not deny that existence in itself is one; he only denies that the possibility of absolute existence implies the possibility of this specific qualified existence. The commentary then explains that the author's statement about allowing a single thing to have different properties is irrelevant because the objector does not claim that. The final part (hādhā wa qad yuqālu) presents a complete and definitive proof for the possibility of restoration. It argues that the possible quiddity can be attributed to absolute existence. If it were impossible to attribute to existence qualified by the two qualifiers (being preceded by non-existence and being preceded by existence), that impossibility would arise from one or both qualifiers. But neither qualifier alone can be the source: being preceded by non-existence is not a barrier because otherwise even first-time existence (ḥudūth) would be impossible; being preceded by existence is not a barrier because otherwise permanence (baqā') would be impossible. Their combination also has no effect. Therefore, the qualified existence (return) is not impossible. The passage ends with 'Wallāhu aʿlam' (God knows best).

Key Terms
الامتناع (al-imtināʿ)
الوجود المطلق (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq)
الوجود المقيد (al-wujūd al-muqayyad)
الانقلاب (al-inqilāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the previous response successfully refutes the objection because existence is one and therefore the same in all cases.
Correction: The objection is not about absolute existence but about existence qualified by the condition 'after non-existence'. The fact that existence is one does not guarantee that this specific qualified existence is possible for the quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what is the key distinction that the previous response ignores?
A) The difference between existence and non-existence.
B) The difference between absolute existence and existence qualified by being after non-existence.
C) The difference between the impossible and the necessary.
D) The difference between the quiddity and its attributes.

Key Takeaway
The previous response fails because it addresses absolute existence, while the objection concerns existence qualified by the condition of being after non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector believes that impossibility concerns absolute non-existence, not your particular state of sin. Is there any sin in your past that you think makes it impossible for you to return to a state of obedience? How does this passage challenge that thought? What would it mean for you to act as if that return is possible?
?
If necessity is only about absolute existence, then your own existence after non-existence is not necessary but possible. Does this possibility fill you with hope or anxiety? How does your view of resurrection affect how you live today?
?
The author's previous response was true but irrelevant because it missed the qualification. Are there areas in your life where you cling to a general truth to avoid addressing a specific, qualified issue? What would it look like to honestly confront the qualified existence—your specific situation—instead of staying in the abstract?

Completing the Proof for the Possibility of Restoration
Final argument establishing the possibility of restoring the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitive proof that a possible quiddity can be attributed to existence qualified by being preceded by existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Neither qualifier alone prevents possibility, so restoration is possible.

The previous lesson ended with the objector's critique. This lesson presents a final argument that completes the proof for the possibility of restoration. It systematically shows that neither being preceded by non-existence nor being preceded by existence can be a source of impossibility, nor can their combination. Therefore, the return is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the final proof
Read the passage from 'هذا وقد يقال في إتمام دليل إمكان الإعادة' to the end, along with the translation. Pay attention to the logical structure.
This proof is self-contained and does not reference the previous objection directly.
2
Identify the logical steps
The proof starts with a premise about absolute existence, then considers hypothetical impossibility, examines each qualifier individually, and concludes about their combination.
Notice how it uses reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand why neither qualifier is a barrier
Being preceded by non-existence cannot be a barrier because otherwise origination (first-time existence) would be impossible. Being preceded by existence cannot be a barrier because otherwise permanence would be impossible.
This shows the proof's reliance on basic theological facts.
4
Conclude that the combination also does not prevent possibility
The proof concludes that since neither qualifier alone nor their combination creates impossibility, the qualified existence (return) is possible.
This completes the argument for the possibility of restoring the non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (قلنا: الوجود أمر واحد إلخ) أورد عليه أنه لا نزاع في حقية هذا المقال لكن لا أثر له في دفع السؤال إذ لا شك أن الامتناع اقتضاء مطلق العدم لا العدم المخصوص ألا يرى أن العدم المسبوق بالوجود لا يمكن أن يتصف به الممتنع فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الوجوب عبارة عن اقتضاء مطلق الوجود لا الوجود المخصوص ألا يرى أن الوجود المسبوق بالعدم لا يمكن أن يتصف به الواجب فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الإمكان عبارة عن لا اقتضائهما مطلقين وقد تقرر أنه يجوز الانقلاب بين هذه المفهومات. وإذا تمهد هذا فنقول: مقصود المعترض أن العود ليس وجوداً مطلقاً على أي وجه كان بل هو وجود مقيد بكونه بعد العدم فلم لا يجوز أن يمتنع اتصاف ماهية المعدوم بهذا الوجود المقيد ولا يمتنع اتصافه بالوجود المطلق من غير لزوم انقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي. فقول المصنف: ولو جوزنا كون الشيء الواحد إلخ لا تعلق له بكلام المعترض لأنه لا يقول بهذا التجويز ولا يلزمه أيضاً ذلك. وكذا قوله: الوجود أمر واحد إلى قوله: ولو جوزنا لأن حاصله أن الوجود المعاد إذا اقتضى لذاته أمراً يجب أن يقتضى الوجود المبتدأ أيضاً لذاته ذلك الأمر بعينه وبالعكس لأنهما متحدان ذاتاً وحقيقة إنما اختلافهما بحسب أمر خارج فالمعترض لم يقل بخلاف ذلك ولم يلزم أيضاً من كلامه خلافه، بل اللازم من كلامه أن الوجود في المبدأ والمعاد متغايران بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج فيجوز أن يقتضي ماهية المعدوم لذاته عدم الاتصاف بأحدهما ولا يقتضي عدم الاتصاف بالآخر. ولا ينافي هذا أن يجوز أن يقتضي أحد الوجودين لذاته أمراً ولا يقتضيه الوجود الآخر. هذا وقد يقال في إتمام دليل إمكان الإعادة: لا شبهة أن اتصاف ذات الممكن بالوجود المطلق غير ممتنع فإذا امتنع اتصافها بالوجود المسبوق بالعدم المسبوق بالوجود لكان هذا الامتناع ناشئاً إما من أحد القيدين أو كليهما لكنا نعلم أن المسبوقية بالعدم لا تكون منشأ لهذا الامتناع وإلا لم يتصف بالحدوث، وكذا المسبوقية بالوجود وإلا لما اتصف بالبقاء على أن الوجود السابق إن لم يفد زيادة استعداد الاتصاف بالوجود فمعلوم بالضرورة أنها لا تقتضي منع ما هو عليه بالذات من قابلية الوجود في الأوقات وكذا نعلم بالضرورة أن لا أثر لاجتماعهما في هذا الامتناع فاتصافها بالوجود المقيد بهذين القيدين أعني العود غير ممتنع. والله أعلم.
His saying: 'We say: existence is one thing, etc.' It was objected against it that there is no dispute about the truth of this statement, but it has no effect in repelling the question, for there is no doubt that impossibility is a requirement of absolute non-existence, not of particular non-existence. Do you not see that non-existence preceded by existence cannot be attributed to the impossible, let alone be required by it? Likewise, necessity is an expression of requirement of absolute existence, not of particular existence. Do you not see that existence preceded by non-existence cannot be attributed to the necessary, let alone be required by it? Likewise, possibility is an expression of the lack of requirement of either of them absolutely, and it has been established that change (inqlāb) among these concepts is permissible. And when this is established, we say: the intention of the objector is that the return is not absolute existence in any way whatever, but rather it is existence qualified by being after non-existence. So why is it not permissible that the attribution of this qualified existence to the quiddity of the non-existent be impossible, while its attribution to absolute existence is not impossible, without necessitating a change from essential possibility to essential impossibility? So the author's saying: 'And if we allow that a single thing, etc.' has no connection to the objector's statement, because he does not say this allowance, nor does it follow from him. Likewise, his saying: 'Existence is one thing' up to his saying: 'And if we allow' because its summary is that the restored existence, if it requires something in itself, then the initial existence must also require that same thing in itself, and vice versa, because they are united in essence and reality; their difference is only in an external matter. The objector did not say contrary to that, nor does the opposite follow from his statement. Rather, what follows from his statement is that existence in the beginning and the return are different according to the addition of an external matter, so it is possible that the quiddity of the non-existent requires in itself the lack of attribution to one of them but does not require the lack of attribution to the other. And this does not contradict that it is possible that one of the two existences requires in itself something while the other existence does not require it. Now it may be said in completing the proof of the possibility of restoration: there is no doubt that the attribution of the possible quiddity to absolute existence is not impossible. So if its attribution to existence preceded by non-existence preceded by existence were impossible, this impossibility would arise either from one of the two qualifiers or from both. But we know that being preceded by non-existence cannot be the source of this impossibility, otherwise it would not be attributed to origination (ḥudūth). Likewise, being preceded by existence, otherwise it would not be attributed to permanence (baqā'). Moreover, the previous existence, if it does not confer an increase of preparedness for the attribution of existence, then it is known by necessity that it does not necessitate prevention of what is in itself a receptivity to existence at times. And likewise we know by necessity that there is no effect of their combination in this impossibility. Therefore, its attribution to existence qualified by these two qualifiers, i.e., the return, is not impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical discussion. It first presents a criticism (qīla) of the previous response. The critic argues that the earlier answer, while true in itself, fails to refute the objection. The objection is that impossibility concerns absolute non-existence, not non-existence qualified by being preceded by existence. The objector's point is that 'return' is a specific existence (after non-existence), and it is possible that this specific existence is impossible for the quiddity even if absolute existence is possible. The author's previous argument that existence is one does not address this because the objector does not deny that existence in itself is one; he only denies that the possibility of absolute existence implies the possibility of this specific qualified existence. The commentary then explains that the author's statement about allowing a single thing to have different properties is irrelevant because the objector does not claim that. The final part (hādhā wa qad yuqālu) presents a complete and definitive proof for the possibility of restoration. It argues that the possible quiddity can be attributed to absolute existence. If it were impossible to attribute to existence qualified by the two qualifiers (being preceded by non-existence and being preceded by existence), that impossibility would arise from one or both qualifiers. But neither qualifier alone can be the source: being preceded by non-existence is not a barrier because otherwise even first-time existence (ḥudūth) would be impossible; being preceded by existence is not a barrier because otherwise permanence (baqā') would be impossible. Their combination also has no effect. Therefore, the qualified existence (return) is not impossible. The passage ends with 'Wallāhu aʿlam' (God knows best).

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الحدوث (al-ḥudūth)
البقاء (al-baqā')
الاستعداد (al-istiʿdād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being preceded by non-existence is a barrier because it indicates a change from non-being to being.
Correction: If being preceded by non-existence were a barrier, then first-time creation (ḥudūth) would be impossible, which is false. Thus it cannot be a barrier.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof rule out being preceded by non-existence as a source of impossibility?
A) Because the return is not preceded by non-existence.
B) Because if it were, then origination (ḥudūth) would be impossible.
C) Because the quiddity does not require existence.
D) Because precedence is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of restoration is proven by showing that neither the qualifier of being preceded by non-existence nor that of being preceded by existence individually imply impossibility, nor does their combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a logical proof that restoration is possible. If you truly believe this, how would your certainty about meeting your Lord after death change the way you live today? What is one thing you would do differently if you had no doubt about resurrection?
?
The proof shows that even a combination of two ‘qualifiers’ does not make restoration impossible. What ‘combination’ of sins, failures, or past mistakes do you think might make it impossible for God to restore you? Now that you see the proof, what is the deeper misconception you must uproot from your heart?
?
This argument rests on the idea that your essence (quiddity) never loses its receptivity to existence. Do you truly believe that no matter how far you have fallen, God can bring you back to spiritual life? If you do, are you living as if that is true? If not, what are you afraid to admit?

Restored Existence: Objection and Answer
Al-Isfahani presents an objection and its answer on restored existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that restored existence is more specific than absolute existence, and the answer that existence is one in essence, so initial and restored existence share the same modal status.

Core Idea
Existence is one in essence; restored existence differs only by time, not by nature.

In this passage, al-Isfahani deals with a subtle objection concerning the possibility of restoring non-existent things to existence. The objector claims that 'return' is a special kind of existence—existence after non-existence—and therefore its possibility does not follow from the possibility of absolute existence. The answer is that existence itself is a single reality, and the difference between initial and restored is merely temporal. This lesson unpacks both sides of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage, which presents the objection. Note the logic: the specific does not follow from the general.
2
Identify the answer's key claim
The answer states that existence is one in essence; only the temporal relation differs. Check how this negates the objection.
3
Understand the conclusion of concomitance
The answer concludes that the two existences are concomitant in possibility, necessity, and impossibility. See why this follows from the unity of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : العود لكونه وجوداً حاصلاً بعد طريان العدم أخص من الوجود المطلق ولا يلزم من إمكان الأعم إمكان الأخص ولا من امتناع الأخص امتناع الأعم فجاز أن يمتنع وجوده بعد عدمه إما لذاته أو لازمه ولا يمتنع وجوده مطلقا. قلنا : الوجود أمر واحد في حد ذاته لا يختلف ذلك الواحد ابتداء وإعادة بحسب حقيقته وذاته بل بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج عن ماهيته وهو الزمان، وكذلك الإيجاد أمر واحد لا يختلف ابتداء وإعادة إلا بحسب تلك الإضافة فإذن يتلازمان، أي الوجودان المبدأ والمعاد وكذا الإيجادان إمكاناً ووجوباً وامتناعاً لأن الأشياء المتوافقة في المعية يجب اشتراكها في هذه الأمور المستندة إلى ذواتها.
If it is said: The return, being an existence obtained after the occurrence of non-existence, is more specific than absolute existence. Neither does the possibility of the more general entail the possibility of the more specific, nor does the impossibility of the more specific entail the impossibility of the more general. So it is possible that its existence after its non-existence is impossible, either in itself or due to a necessary concomitant, while its existence absolutely is not impossible. We say: Existence is one thing in itself; that one thing does not differ in beginning and return according to its essence and self, but only according to relation to something external to its quiddity, which is time. Likewise, bringing into existence is one thing, not differing in beginning and return except by that relation. Therefore, they are concomitant—that is, the two existences of beginning and return, and likewise the two acts of bringing into existence—in possibility, necessity, and impossibility, because things that are uniform in essence must share in these matters that are grounded in their selves.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection states that 'return' (al-ʿawd) is a specific kind of existence (existence after non-existence) and is therefore more specific than absolute existence. The possibility of the more general does not guarantee the possibility of the more specific, nor does the impossibility of the specific imply the impossibility of the general. Hence the objector suggests that restored existence could be impossible in itself or due to a necessary concomitant, while absolute existence remains possible.

The answer argues that existence (al-wujūd) is one in essence and does not differ between initial and restored except by temporal relation, which is external to its quiddity. Similarly, the act of bringing into existence (al-ījād) is one. Therefore, the two existences (and the two acts) are concomitant in their modal statuses (possibility, necessity, impossibility). Things that agree in essence must share these attributes that are grounded in their very selves.

Key Terms
عود
وجود مطلق
إمكان
امتناع
وجوب
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Restored existence is a different kind of existence from initial existence.
Correction: The answer emphasizes that existence is one in essence; it only differs in relation to time. Therefore, restored existence is not a different reality.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the objection?
A) Restored existence is impossible because it is specific.
B) Existence is one in essence, so initial and restored share the same modal status.
C) Absolute existence is impossible.
D) The objection is invalid because return is not more specific.

Key Takeaway
Restored existence is not a different reality from initial existence; they are the same in essence, differing only by time, so they share the same modal status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will be brought back to life after death—the very same 'you' that exists now. Are you living today as if that return will be made to the same self? Every habit you build now, every sin you commit, every good deed you do—it all belongs to that self. What will you change starting today to prepare that self for its return?
?
The lesson says existence is one in essence; only time separates your first life from your second. When you look at yourself in the mirror, do you see a person whose existence is continuous and unified, or do you live as if your past, present, and future selves are disconnected? How does this unity demand that you take full ownership of your entire life—past, present, and future?
?
The act of bringing you into existence is the same as the act of resurrecting you. You are entirely dependent on God both for your first breath and for your return. Does your daily life reflect that absolute dependence? Do you pray, repent, and trust God as if He alone holds your existence in every moment? What would it look like to live with that constant awareness starting now?

Critique of the Answer on Restoration
The commentator critiques the previous answer's relevance to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the answer that existence is one does not refute the objection that restored existence might be impossible due to its specific qualification.

Core Idea
A correct answer must address the specific qualification, not just the universal essence.

The commentator examines the author's previous answer that existence is one thing. While the statement is true, it misses the point: the objector does not deny the unity of existence in essence, but rather claims that the specific, qualified existence (being after non-existence) may be impossible. We must see why the answer fails and what the real issue is.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two parts
The passage first critiques the answer that existence is one, then explains the objector's real stance. Pay attention to the distinction between absolute and qualified existence.
The objector does not dispute essential unity.
2
Understand the irrelevance of the answer
The critic argues that impossibility pertains to absolute non-existence, not specific non-existence. Similarly, necessity and possibility are about absolutes. The objector's concern is with a specific qualified existence.
3
See why the author's allowance does not apply
The author mentioned allowing a thing to be possible at one time and impossible at another, but the objector does not claim that; he claims the quiddity cannot receive the specific qualified existence because of the constraint.
The objector's view does not imply conversion of essential possibility.
4
Grasp the objector's actual position
Existence in the beginning and return differ by external relation, so the quiddity might be incapable of one but not the other, without affecting essential possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قلنا : الوجود أمر واحد إلخ قيل: لا نزاع في حقية هذا المقال لكن لا أثر له في دفع السؤال إذ لا شك أن الامتناع اقتضاء مطلق العدم لا العدم المخصوص ألا يرى أن العدم المسبوق بالوجود لا يمكن أن يتصف به الممتنع فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الوجوب عبارة عن اقتضاء مطلق الوجود لا الوجود المخصوص ألا يرى أن الوجود المسبوق بالعدم لا يمكن أن يتصف به الواجب فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الإمكان عبارة عن لا اقتضائهما مطلقين وقد تقرر أنه يجوز الانقلاب بين هذه المفهومات وإذا تمهد هذا فنقول : مقصود المعترض أن العود ليس وجوداً مطلقاً على أي وجه كان بل هو وجود مقيد بكونه بعد العدم فلم لا يجوز أن يمتنع اتصاف ماهية المعدوم بهذا الوجود المقيد ولا يمتنع اتصافه بالوجود المطلق من غير لزوم انقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي فقول المصنف : ولو جوزنا كون الشيء الواحد إلخ لا تعلق له بكلام المعترض لأنه لا يقول بهذا التجويز ولا يلزمه أيضاً ذلك، وكذا قوله : الوجود أمر واحد إلى قوله : ولو جوزنا لأن حاصله أن الوجود المعاد إذا اقتضى لذاته أمراً يجب أن يقتضى الوجود المبتدأ أيضاً لذاته ذلك الأمر بعينه وبالعكس لأنهما متحدان ذاتاً وحقيقة إنما اختلافهما بحسب أمر خارج فالمعترض لم يقل بخلاف ذلك ولم يلزم أيضاً من كلامه خلافه. بل اللازم من كلامه أن الوجود في المبدأ والمعاد متغايران بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج فيجوز أن يقتضي ماهية المعدوم لذاته عدم الاتصاف بأحدهما ولا يقتضي عدم الاتصاف بالآخر ولا ينافي هذا أن يجوز أن يقتضي أحد الوجدين لذاته أمراً ولا يقتضيه الوجود الآخر. هذا وقد يقال في إتمام دليل إمكان الإعادة لا شبهة أن اتصاف ذات الممكن بالوجود المطلق غير ممتنع فإذا امتنع اتصافها بالوجود المسبوق بالعدم المسبوق بالوجود لكان هذا الامتناع ناشئاً إما من أحد القيدين أو كليهما لكنا نعلم أن المسبوقية بالعدم لا تكون منشأ لهذا الامتناع وإلا لم يتصف بالحدوث، وكذا المسبوقية بالوجود وإلا لما اتصف بالبقاء على أن الوجود السابق إن لم يفد زيادة استعداد الاتصاف بالوجود فمعلوم بالضرورة أنها لا تقتضي منع ما هو عليه بالذات من قابلية الوجود في الأوقات وكذا نعلم بالضرورة أن لا أثر لاجتماعهما في هذا الامتناع فاتصافها بالوجود المقيد بهذين القيدين أعني العود غير ممتنع والله أعلم .
His statement: 'We said: existence is one thing, etc.' It is said: There is no dispute about the truth of this statement, but it has no effect in repelling the question, since there is no doubt that impossibility is a demand for absolute non-existence, not specific non-existence. Do you not see that non-existence preceded by existence cannot be attributed to the impossible, let alone be demanded by it? Likewise, necessity is an expression of demand for absolute existence, not specific existence. Do you not see that existence preceded by non-existence cannot be attributed to the necessary, let alone be demanded by it? Likewise, possibility is an expression of the absence of demand for both in an absolute sense, and it has been established that conversion among these concepts is permissible. When this is established, we say: The objector's intent is that return is not absolute existence in any way, but rather existence qualified by being after non-existence. So why is it not allowed that the quiddity of the non-existent is prevented from being characterized by this qualified existence, while it is not prevented from being characterized by absolute existence, without necessitating a conversion from essential possibility to essential impossibility? Thus the author's statement: 'Even if we allow that a single thing...' has no connection to the objector's statement, for he does not make this allowance, nor does it follow from it. Likewise his statement: 'Existence is one thing...' up to his statement: 'Even if we allow...' because its gist is that if restored existence demands something by its essence, initial existence must also demand the same thing by its essence, and vice versa, because they are identical in essence and reality, only differing by something external. The objector does not say contrary to that, nor does contrary follow from his statement. Rather, what follows from his statement is that existence in the beginning and return are different according to relation to something external, so it is permissible that the quiddity of the non-existent by its essence demands lack of characterization by one of them while not demanding lack of characterization by the other, and this does not contradict that it is permissible that one of the two existences by its essence demands something while the other existence does not demand it. This, and it may be said in completing the proof of the possibility of restoration: There is no doubt that the characterization of the essence of the possible by absolute existence is not impossible. If its characterization by existence preceded by non-existence preceded by existence were impossible, this impossibility would arise either from one of the two constraints or from both. But we know that being preceded by non-existence is not a source of this impossibility, otherwise it would not be characterized by origination (ḥudūth). Likewise, being preceded by existence is not a source, otherwise it would not be characterized by permanence (baqāʾ). Moreover, if the previous existence does not confer an increased readiness for characterization by existence, it is known necessarily that it does not necessitate prevention of what is in itself a receptivity to existence at times. Likewise, we know necessarily that there is no effect of their conjunction in this impossibility. So its characterization by existence qualified by these two constraints – i.e., return – is not impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a critical note on the previous answer, followed by a completion of the proof. The critic argues that the answer does not refute the objection because the objector does not deny the unity of existence in essence; rather, he claims that the specific kind of existence (restored existence) may be impossible due to its additional constraint, without affecting absolute existence. The answer misses the point because it only shows that existence is one in essence, but the objector already agrees that the essences are the same; the difference is in the external relation, and that difference could make the specific existence impossible while the general remains possible. The critic then quotes the author's allowance that a thing could be possible in one time and impossible in another, and shows that this does not follow from the objector's view.

Then the passage offers a completion of the proof for the possibility of restoration. It argues that if restored existence were impossible, the impossibility would have to come from one of its constraints (being preceded by non-existence, or by existence). But neither constraint prevents possibility: being preceded by non-existence is common to all originated things, so it cannot be the source; being preceded by existence is common to all permanent things, so it also cannot be the source. Therefore, the conjunction of the two constraints does not create impossibility, so restored existence is possible.

Key Terms
امتناع
وجوب
إمكان
انقلاب
وجود مقيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unity of existence in essence settles all objections about restored existence.
Correction: The objector does not deny essential unity; he questions the possibility of a specific qualified existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the critic say the answer does not refute the objection?
A) Because the answer is false.
B) Because the objector doesn't deny essential unity of existence.
C) Because the objector doesn't understand the argument.
D) Because the answer is too brief.

Key Takeaway
The critique shows that an answer must address the specific claim about qualified existence, not just the general essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt or challenge to your faith, do you tend to give a general answer or do you pinpoint the exact issue? How can you train yourself to address the specific objection instead of reciting broad truths? Commit to practicing this with your next doubt.
?
Think of a time you assumed that a general truth automatically refuted a specific objection. What was the result? How does this lesson compel you to change your approach to engaging with others' arguments? Write down one concrete step you will take to listen more carefully before answering.
?
In your own life, do you treat the same action differently based on context (e.g., when no one is watching vs. when you are being observed)? How does the distinction between absolute and qualified existence challenge your understanding of consistency and personal responsibility? Will you examine one area where your behavior changes with context and seek to align it with absolute truth?

Completion of the Proof for the Possibility of Restoration
The commentator presents a constructive proof that restored existence is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why neither constraint (being preceded by non-existence nor by existence) prevents the possibility of restored existence.

Core Idea
Restored existence is possible because its constraints are common to many possible things.

After critiquing the previous answer, the commentator provides a positive argument for the possibility of restoration. The proof systematically examines the two constraints of restored existence—being preceded by non-existence and being preceded by existence—and shows that each alone does not prevent possibility, nor does their combination.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof structure
The proof begins with an assumption: if restored existence were impossible, the impossibility must come from one of its two constraints (being preceded by non-existence or by existence). Follow the logic step by step.
2
Examine the first constraint
Being preceded by non-existence cannot be the source, because that would make all originated (ḥādith) things impossible, which is false.
Originated things like the universe are preceded by non-existence.
3
Examine the second constraint
Being preceded by existence cannot be the source, because that would make all permanent (bāqī) things impossible, which is false.
Permanent things like the hereafter are preceded by existence.
4
Conclude about the conjunction
If neither constraint alone causes impossibility, their combination also does not. Moreover, previous existence does not affect readiness. Therefore, restored existence is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قلنا : الوجود أمر واحد إلخ قيل: لا نزاع في حقية هذا المقال لكن لا أثر له في دفع السؤال إذ لا شك أن الامتناع اقتضاء مطلق العدم لا العدم المخصوص ألا يرى أن العدم المسبوق بالوجود لا يمكن أن يتصف به الممتنع فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الوجوب عبارة عن اقتضاء مطلق الوجود لا الوجود المخصوص ألا يرى أن الوجود المسبوق بالعدم لا يمكن أن يتصف به الواجب فضلاً عن اقتضائه، وكذا الإمكان عبارة عن لا اقتضائهما مطلقين وقد تقرر أنه يجوز الانقلاب بين هذه المفهومات وإذا تمهد هذا فنقول : مقصود المعترض أن العود ليس وجوداً مطلقاً على أي وجه كان بل هو وجود مقيد بكونه بعد العدم فلم لا يجوز أن يمتنع اتصاف ماهية المعدوم بهذا الوجود المقيد ولا يمتنع اتصافه بالوجود المطلق من غير لزوم انقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي فقول المصنف : ولو جوزنا كون الشيء الواحد إلخ لا تعلق له بكلام المعترض لأنه لا يقول بهذا التجويز ولا يلزمه أيضاً ذلك، وكذا قوله : الوجود أمر واحد إلى قوله : ولو جوزنا لأن حاصله أن الوجود المعاد إذا اقتضى لذاته أمراً يجب أن يقتضى الوجود المبتدأ أيضاً لذاته ذلك الأمر بعينه وبالعكس لأنهما متحدان ذاتاً وحقيقة إنما اختلافهما بحسب أمر خارج فالمعترض لم يقل بخلاف ذلك ولم يلزم أيضاً من كلامه خلافه. بل اللازم من كلامه أن الوجود في المبدأ والمعاد متغايران بحسب الإضافة إلى أمر خارج فيجوز أن يقتضي ماهية المعدوم لذاته عدم الاتصاف بأحدهما ولا يقتضي عدم الاتصاف بالآخر ولا ينافي هذا أن يجوز أن يقتضي أحد الوجدين لذاته أمراً ولا يقتضيه الوجود الآخر. هذا وقد يقال في إتمام دليل إمكان الإعادة لا شبهة أن اتصاف ذات الممكن بالوجود المطلق غير ممتنع فإذا امتنع اتصافها بالوجود المسبوق بالعدم المسبوق بالوجود لكان هذا الامتناع ناشئاً إما من أحد القيدين أو كليهما لكنا نعلم أن المسبوقية بالعدم لا تكون منشأ لهذا الامتناع وإلا لم يتصف بالحدوث، وكذا المسبوقية بالوجود وإلا لما اتصف بالبقاء على أن الوجود السابق إن لم يفد زيادة استعداد الاتصاف بالوجود فمعلوم بالضرورة أنها لا تقتضي منع ما هو عليه بالذات من قابلية الوجود في الأوقات وكذا نعلم بالضرورة أن لا أثر لاجتماعهما في هذا الامتناع فاتصافها بالوجود المقيد بهذين القيدين أعني العود غير ممتنع والله أعلم .
His statement: 'We said: existence is one thing, etc.' It is said: There is no dispute about the truth of this statement, but it has no effect in repelling the question, since there is no doubt that impossibility is a demand for absolute non-existence, not specific non-existence. Do you not see that non-existence preceded by existence cannot be attributed to the impossible, let alone be demanded by it? Likewise, necessity is an expression of demand for absolute existence, not specific existence. Do you not see that existence preceded by non-existence cannot be attributed to the necessary, let alone be demanded by it? Likewise, possibility is an expression of the absence of demand for both in an absolute sense, and it has been established that conversion among these concepts is permissible. When this is established, we say: The objector's intent is that return is not absolute existence in any way, but rather existence qualified by being after non-existence. So why is it not allowed that the quiddity of the non-existent is prevented from being characterized by this qualified existence, while it is not prevented from being characterized by absolute existence, without necessitating a conversion from essential possibility to essential impossibility? Thus the author's statement: 'Even if we allow that a single thing...' has no connection to the objector's statement, for he does not make this allowance, nor does it follow from it. Likewise his statement: 'Existence is one thing...' up to his statement: 'Even if we allow...' because its gist is that if restored existence demands something by its essence, initial existence must also demand the same thing by its essence, and vice versa, because they are identical in essence and reality, only differing by something external. The objector does not say contrary to that, nor does contrary follow from his statement. Rather, what follows from his statement is that existence in the beginning and return are different according to relation to something external, so it is permissible that the quiddity of the non-existent by its essence demands lack of characterization by one of them while not demanding lack of characterization by the other, and this does not contradict that it is permissible that one of the two existences by its essence demands something while the other existence does not demand it. This, and it may be said in completing the proof of the possibility of restoration: There is no doubt that the characterization of the essence of the possible by absolute existence is not impossible. If its characterization by existence preceded by non-existence preceded by existence were impossible, this impossibility would arise either from one of the two constraints or from both. But we know that being preceded by non-existence is not a source of this impossibility, otherwise it would not be characterized by origination (ḥudūth). Likewise, being preceded by existence is not a source, otherwise it would not be characterized by permanence (baqāʾ). Moreover, if the previous existence does not confer an increased readiness for characterization by existence, it is known necessarily that it does not necessitate prevention of what is in itself a receptivity to existence at times. Likewise, we know necessarily that there is no effect of their conjunction in this impossibility. So its characterization by existence qualified by these two constraints – i.e., return – is not impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a critical note on the previous answer, followed by a completion of the proof. The critic argues that the answer does not refute the objection because the objector does not deny the unity of existence in essence; rather, he claims that the specific kind of existence (restored existence) may be impossible due to its additional constraint, without affecting absolute existence. The answer misses the point because it only shows that existence is one in essence, but the objector already agrees that the essences are the same; the difference is in the external relation, and that difference could make the specific existence impossible while the general remains possible. The critic then quotes the author's allowance that a thing could be possible in one time and impossible in another, and shows that this does not follow from the objector's view.

Then the passage offers a completion of the proof for the possibility of restoration. It argues that if restored existence were impossible, the impossibility would have to come from one of its constraints (being preceded by non-existence, or by existence). But neither constraint prevents possibility: being preceded by non-existence is common to all originated things, so it cannot be the source; being preceded by existence is common to all permanent things, so it also cannot be the source. Therefore, the conjunction of the two constraints does not create impossibility, so restored existence is possible.

Key Terms
حدوث
بقاء
وجود مقيد
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conjunction of two possible constraints could create impossibility even if each is possible individually.
Correction: The proof shows that if each constraint individually does not prevent possibility, their combination also does not, because the constraints are independent and do not interact.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof say being preceded by non-existence cannot be the source of impossibility?
A) Because it is a necessary attribute of possible things.
B) Because otherwise nothing could be originated (ḥudūth).
C) Because existence is one.
D) Because impossibility is absolute.

Key Takeaway
The proof establishes that restored existence is possible by showing that its specific constraints are not inherently preventatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen a rational proof that your return after death is possible. Do you truly live as if this is certain? What would change in your daily choices if you fully internalized that your existence will be restored and you will be held accountable? Write down one specific action you will take today because of this conviction.
?
This argument shows that the constraints of restoration are not preventatives. But your own heart may still resist the idea of accountability. What is the deeper doubt that holds you back from surrendering completely to the reality of resurrection? Pause and ask yourself: am I more afraid of the proof being false, or of what it demands of me?
?
The passage states that previous existence does not affect inherent receptivity. Your past sins do not make you incapable of returning to Allah. What sin or habit have you used as an excuse to delay repentance? Confront it now, and commit to turning back, because your capacity for return is not diminished.

Refuting the Necessity of Impossible Interposition
Author's response to opponent's necessity argument on interposition of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the opponent's claim that non-existence cannot intervene between a thing and itself as a necessary truth.

Core Idea
The interposition of non-existence is about time, not about the thing itself.

The opponent argues that it is self-evidently impossible for non-existence to come between a thing and itself, concluding that resurrection cannot restore the very same entity. The author counters by clarifying what 'interposition of non-existence' actually means—it is merely a temporal gap between two instances of the same existence. This lesson unpacks that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the clear structure: opponent's claim from necessity, then author's response.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that interposition of non-existence requires two distinct things, so the restored thing cannot be the same as the initial thing. Mark this as the core objection.
They appeal to 'necessity' (al-ḍarūra).
3
See the author's redefinition
The author redefines 'interposition of non-existence' as the interposition of the time of non-existence between two times of a single existence. This dissolves the need for two distinct things.
Key phrase: 'تخلل زمان العدم بين زماني الوجود الواحد'.
4
Understand the refutation of necessity
The author notes that the claim of necessity contradicts what most rational people hold, and thus is not accepted. This weakens the opponent's appeal to self-evidence.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument preserves the possibility of bodily resurrection: the same person can be restored after death by regaining the same individual existence.
Connect to kalām discussions on maʿād.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخصم يدعي الضرورة تارة ويلتجئ إلى الاستدلال يتصور أخرى، أما الضرورة فقالوا: تخلل العدم بين الشيء ونفسه محال بالضرورة إذ لا بد للتخلل من طرفين متغايرين فيكون حينئذ الوجود بعد العدم غير الوجود قبله، حتى يتصور تخلل العدم بينهما وعلى هذا فلا يكون المعاد هو المبتدأ بعينه لأن كلا منهما موجوداً بوجود مغاير لوجود صاحبه فهما موجودان متغايران فلا يكون الموجود الأول بعينه معاداً بعد عدمه والجواب أنه لا معنى لتخلل العدم هاهنا سوى أنه كان موجوداً زماناً ثم زال عنه ذلك الوجود في زمان آخر، ثم اتصف به في زمان ومن هذا تبين أن التخلل بحسب الحقيقية إنما هو لزمان العدم بين زماني الوجود الواحد، وإذا اعتبر نسبة هذا التخلل إلى العدم مجازاً كفاه اعتبار التغاير في الوجود الواحد بحسب زمانيه على أن دعوى الضرورة في حكم خالفه جمهور من العقلاء غير مسموعة.
And the opponent sometimes claims necessity and other times resorts to inferential reasoning. As for necessity, they said: The interposition of non-existence between a thing and itself is impossible by necessity, because interposition requires two distinct extremities. Then at that point, existence after non-existence is not the same as existence before it, so that interposition of non-existence between them can be conceived. On this basis, the restored thing is not the very same as the initial thing, because each of them exists by an existence different from the existence of the other; thus they are two distinct existents, so the first existent is not restored after its non-existence. The answer is that there is no meaning to 'interposition of non-existence' here except that it existed for a time, then that existence ceased from it in another time, and then it became characterized by it in a time. From this it becomes clear that the interposition, in reality, is the interposition of the time of non-existence between the two times of a single existence. And if this interposition is attributed metaphorically to non-existence, then the consideration of the difference in the single existence with respect to its two times suffices. Moreover, the claim of necessity in a ruling that contradicts the majority of rational people is not accepted.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's argument from necessity and the author's response. The opponent claims it is a necessary truth that non-existence cannot intervene between a thing and itself, because such interposition requires two distinct things. Hence the restored thing would be different from the original. The author responds by redefining 'interposition of non-existence' metaphorically: it simply means that the same existence is interrupted by a time of non-existence between its two instances. There is no literal interposition between the thing and itself, but rather between two temporal instances of the same existence. Furthermore, the claim of necessity is invalid because many rational thinkers disagree with it.

Key Terms
تخلل العدم
الضرورة
المعاد
المبتدأ
الوجود الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent believes that any interposition of non-existence between a thing and itself would make the two existences numerically distinct, preventing identity across resurrection.
Correction: The author shows that interposition of a time span of non-existence does not preclude the same existence from recurring; identity is preserved because the same existence is reinstated after a temporal gap.

Checkpoint
How does the author reinterpret 'interposition of non-existence' (تخلل العدم)?
A) As a literal separation of the thing from itself
B) As the interposition of the time of non-existence between two times of a single existence
C) As a logical impossibility
D) As proof that resurrection is impossible

Key Takeaway
The same single existence can be reinstated after a temporal gap of non-existence; thus the opponent's appeal to necessity fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson reinterprets a seemingly impossible concept by shifting perspective from literal to metaphorical. What deeply held beliefs about yourself or the world might be based on a literal reading of something that Allah intends as metaphorical? Are you willing to question your own assumptions for the sake of truth?
?
If your identity can survive a total interruption of existence, then your current actions are not just for this life—they define the 'you' that will be restored. How would your day change if you truly believed that every moment is being recorded for a return after absolute absence?

Time is Not a Distinguishing Characteristic
First argument against time as a distinguishing characteristic with anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time is not considered a distinguishing characteristic for the restoration of the non-existent, through the example of Zayd and the anecdote of Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
If time made you a new person each moment, debate itself would be impossible.

The author begins by asserting that time is not a distinguishing characteristic (mushakhkhiṣāt) in the restoration of the non-existent. He uses the example of Zayd to show that a person remains the same over time, and a famous anecdote about Ibn Sina to illustrate the absurdity of the opposing view. This lesson unpacks that core argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author states the claim and then defends it.
The passage includes a real historical anecdote.
2
Understand the Zayd example
The author argues that if time were a distinguishing characteristic, Zayd would be a different person at each moment, which is patently false. Reflect on why this is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the Ibn Sina anecdote
Ibn Sina's student insisted time causes real external variation. Ibn Sina's retort showed that if true, the debaters themselves would be different persons, making the debate meaningless. See how this illustrates the point.
The student conceded immediately.
