The Second Observatory - The First Objective: On the Restoration of the Non-Existent. And time is not among them (the distinguishing characteristics) of necessity, because Zayd who exists at this hour is (the same as) the one who existed before it according to the external consideration, i.e. according to what is considered in his existence externally; there is no difference or variation in that. For if time were among the distinguishing characteristics considered in his existence externally, then he would be a different person at every time, which is absolutely false. And what is said that we know necessarily that the existent with the restriction of being in this time is other than the existent with the restriction of being before this time is an imaginary matter; the variation judged in this case is only according to the mind and consideration, not externally. It is related that this discussion occurred between Ibn Sina and one of his students, and that student insisted on variation according to externals based on the view that time is among the distinguishing accidental qualities. So Ibn Sina said to him: If the matter is as you claim, then I am not obligated to answer, because I am not the one who was debating you, and you are also not the one who was debating me. So the student was confounded, returned to the truth, and acknowledged that there is no variation in reality and that time is not among the distinguishing characteristics. And even if we grant that this time is included among the distinguishing accidental qualities, and that the non-existent is restored with its first time, then why do you say that what occurs in its first time is absolutely a beginning so that it would be both beginning and restored together? It would only be so if its time were not also restored with it. In other words, what occurs in its first time is only a beginning if it is not preceded by another occurrence; but if it is preceded by it, then it is restored, not a beginning. The second: If we assume restoration and suppose that it is restored identically, and God is able to create its like anew without doubt, then let us also suppose it exists alongside that restored one. Then the restored one would not be distinguished from the new one, and duality without distinction would follow, which is necessarily false. The answer: The lack of distinction at that time is not granted; rather they are distinguished by identity, i.e. by the distinguishing accidental qualities, while agreeing in essence, just as one beginner is distinguished from another beginner. Necessarily, what is restricted by one restriction is other than what is restricted by another; and this is sufficient for the validity of the substitution. And if by it is meant that it does not repel substitution in them even if with variation, then its falsity is not granted. He said: 'Then he would be a different person at every time.' It is said: This only applies if one claimed that time absolutely is among the distinguishing characteristics. But if one claimed that the time of occurrence is among them but not the time of duration, then this does not apply, nor does the Sheikh's refutation of his student, and the difference is clear. For the time of occurrence has influence on the fortune or misfortune of a thing, therefore one seeks a favorable time at the beginnings of matters, such as setting out on a journey, beginning books, and the like, not at the time of their duration. And the source of differing accidental qualities according to them may be made the source of variation in essence, like the differentiae and specific forms. And it was answered that it is a speech on the chain of transmission, refuted by the fact that what is considered in existence is that which cannot be conceived without it. Then what does not harm its absence in duration does not harm it in restoration.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against time being a distinguishing characteristic (mushakhkhiṣāt) for the restoration of the non-existent. It begins by asserting that time is not a distinguishing characteristic because a person like Zayd is the same individual before and after a time interval; if time were a distinguishing factor, he would be a different person at each moment, which is absurd. The text then addresses a potential objection: that we necessarily perceive the existent at one time as different from the existent at another time. It dismisses this as imaginary, stating that any perceived variation is mental, not external. An anecdote about Ibn Sina and a student illustrates the point: the student argued that time is an accidental quality (ʿaraḍ mushakhkhiṣ) causing external variation. Ibn Sina retorted that if that were true, the debaters themselves would be different persons, making the debate moot—the student conceded. Next, the text grants for argument's sake that time is a distinguishing quality but asks: why would the restored thing at its first time be considered an absolute beginning? It explains that something is only a beginning if not preceded by another occurrence; if preceded, it is restored. Then it transitions into a second argument (included here) about distinguishing the restored from a new similar creation. The response maintains that they are distinguished by identity (al-huwiyya), i.e., accidental qualities, even if they share essence. Finally, a marginal commentary discusses the possibility that only the time of occurrence (not duration) might be a distinguishing characteristic, citing practices like choosing auspicious times for beginnings, and concludes that what does not matter for duration does not matter for restoration.

Key Terms
مشخصات (mushakhkhiṣāt)
عوارض مشخصة (ʿawāriḍ mushakhkhiṣa)
ابتداء (ibtidāʾ)
إعادة (iʿāda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that because we perceive change over time, time must be a real distinguishing characteristic externally.
Correction: The passage shows that any perceived variation over time is mental, not external; otherwise, identity would be impossible.

Checkpoint
How did Ibn Sina refute his student who claimed time causes external variation in the restored thing?
A) He argued that time is not a real quality.
B) He said that if time caused variation, then the debaters themselves would be different persons, making the debate meaningless.
C) He used a logical proof from the eternity of the world.
D) He appealed to scripture.

Key Takeaway
Time is not a real distinguishing factor for identity; any perceived change over time is only in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time does not make you a new person each moment, then you are the same soul who committed sins years ago. Does this realization change how you view repentance and Allah's mercy? What will you do today to seek forgiveness for past deeds?
?
The anecdote shows that assuming time distinguishes persons would make debate impossible. Think of a recent disagreement with someone. Did you treat them as fundamentally the same person as before, or did you assume time had changed them? How can this lesson guide you to hold others accountable consistently?

Distinguishing the Restored from a New Creation
Second argument and response, plus marginal commentary on time of occurrence vs. duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against time as a distinguishing characteristic: that even if God restores a thing identically and creates a similar new thing, they are distinguished by identity (huwiyya) and not by time.

Core Idea
Two identical things are distinguished by their accidental qualities, not by time.

After establishing that time is not a distinguishing characteristic, the author presents a second argument: if God were to restore a non-existent thing exactly and also create a similar new thing, there would be a problem of duality without distinction. The answer is that they are distinguished by identity (huwiyya), i.e., their accidental qualities. This lesson unpacks that argument and also addresses a marginal commentary that refines the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument and the marginal note
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional scenario.
The marginal note comments on an earlier phrase.
2
Understand the problem of duality
If the restored thing is identical to a new creation, they would be two things without a distinguishing feature—an absurdity. The text provides a solution.
3
Grasp the concept of identity (huwiyya)
The response says they are distinguished by identity, meaning by their accidental qualities (ʿawāriḍ mushakhkhiṣa), even if they share essence. See how this preserves distinction.
This is similar to how two individuals of the same species differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المستأنف
ضرورة أن المقيد بقيد غير المقيد بآخر وهذا القدر يكفي لصحة التخلل وإن أريد به أنه لا يندفع به التخلل فيهما وإن كان مع تغاير ما فبطلانه ممنوع قوله : لكان هو في كل وقت شخصاً آخر) قيل : هذا إنما يرد لو ادعى أن الوقت مطلقاً من المشخصات اما لو أدعى أن زمان الحدوث منها دون زمان البقاء فلا يرد هذ ولا إلزام الشيخ لتلميذه والفرق ظاهر فإن لزمان الحدوث دخلاً في سعادة الشيء نحوستة ولذا يتحرى طيب الوقت في بداية الأمور كالخروج إلى السفر وبداية الكتب ونحوهما لا في زمان بقائها، ومنشأ إختلاف العوارض عندهم قد يجعل منشأ التغاير الذات كالفصول والصور النوعية، وأجيب بأنه بأنه كلام على السند مدفوع بأن المعتبر في الوجود ما لا يتصور هو بدونه ثم إن ما لا يضر
عدمه في البقاء لا يضر في الإعادة .
The Second Observatory - The First Objective: On the Restoration of the Non-Existent. And time is not among them (the distinguishing characteristics) of necessity, because Zayd who exists at this hour is (the same as) the one who existed before it according to the external consideration, i.e. according to what is considered in his existence externally; there is no difference or variation in that. For if time were among the distinguishing characteristics considered in his existence externally, then he would be a different person at every time, which is absolutely false. And what is said that we know necessarily that the existent with the restriction of being in this time is other than the existent with the restriction of being before this time is an imaginary matter; the variation judged in this case is only according to the mind and consideration, not externally. It is related that this discussion occurred between Ibn Sina and one of his students, and that student insisted on variation according to externals based on the view that time is among the distinguishing accidental qualities. So Ibn Sina said to him: If the matter is as you claim, then I am not obligated to answer, because I am not the one who was debating you, and you are also not the one who was debating me. So the student was confounded, returned to the truth, and acknowledged that there is no variation in reality and that time is not among the distinguishing characteristics. And even if we grant that this time is included among the distinguishing accidental qualities, and that the non-existent is restored with its first time, then why do you say that what occurs in its first time is absolutely a beginning so that it would be both beginning and restored together? It would only be so if its time were not also restored with it. In other words, what occurs in its first time is only a beginning if it is not preceded by another occurrence; but if it is preceded by it, then it is restored, not a beginning. The second: If we assume restoration and suppose that it is restored identically, and God is able to create its like anew without doubt, then let us also suppose it exists alongside that restored one. Then the restored one would not be distinguished from the new one, and duality without distinction would follow, which is necessarily false. The answer: The lack of distinction at that time is not granted; rather they are distinguished by identity, i.e. by the distinguishing accidental qualities, while agreeing in essence, just as one beginner is distinguished from another beginner. Necessarily, what is restricted by one restriction is other than what is restricted by another; and this is sufficient for the validity of the substitution. And if by it is meant that it does not repel substitution in them even if with variation, then its falsity is not granted. He said: 'Then he would be a different person at every time.' It is said: This only applies if one claimed that time absolutely is among the distinguishing characteristics. But if one claimed that the time of occurrence is among them but not the time of duration, then this does not apply, nor does the Sheikh's refutation of his student, and the difference is clear. For the time of occurrence has influence on the fortune or misfortune of a thing, therefore one seeks a favorable time at the beginnings of matters, such as setting out on a journey, beginning books, and the like, not at the time of their duration. And the source of differing accidental qualities according to them may be made the source of variation in essence, like the differentiae and specific forms. And it was answered that it is a speech on the chain of transmission, refuted by the fact that what is considered in existence is that which cannot be conceived without it. Then what does not harm its absence in duration does not harm it in restoration.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against time being a distinguishing characteristic (mushakhkhiṣāt) for the restoration of the non-existent. It begins by asserting that time is not a distinguishing characteristic because a person like Zayd is the same individual before and after a time interval; if time were a distinguishing factor, he would be a different person at each moment, which is absurd. The text then addresses a potential objection: that we necessarily perceive the existent at one time as different from the existent at another time. It dismisses this as imaginary, stating that any perceived variation is mental, not external. An anecdote about Ibn Sina and a student illustrates the point: the student argued that time is an accidental quality (ʿaraḍ mushakhkhiṣ) causing external variation. Ibn Sina retorted that if that were true, the debaters themselves would be different persons, making the debate moot—the student conceded. Next, the text grants for argument's sake that time is a distinguishing quality but asks: why would the restored thing at its first time be considered an absolute beginning? It explains that something is only a beginning if not preceded by another occurrence; if preceded, it is restored. Then it transitions into a second argument (included here) about distinguishing the restored from a new similar creation. The response maintains that they are distinguished by identity (al-huwiyya), i.e., accidental qualities, even if they share essence. Finally, a marginal commentary discusses the possibility that only the time of occurrence (not duration) might be a distinguishing characteristic, citing practices like choosing auspicious times for beginnings, and concludes that what does not matter for duration does not matter for restoration.

Key Terms
هوية (huwiyya)
مشخصات (mushakhkhiṣāt)
عوارض مشخصة (ʿawāriḍ mushakhkhiṣa)
ابتداء (ibtidāʾ)
إعادة (iʿāda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That time of occurrence can serve as a distinguishing characteristic for restoration.
Correction: The passage argues that what is not essential for existence in duration cannot be essential for restoration, rejecting time of occurrence as a genuine distinguisher.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the second argument, how are the restored thing and the new creation distinguished?
A) By time of occurrence
B) By identity (huwiyya), i.e., accidental qualities
C) They cannot be distinguished
D) By essence (māhiyya)

Key Takeaway
Duality without distinction would be absurd, but distinction is preserved by identity (accidental qualities), not by time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you define yourself more by when you started (your past or beginnings) or by the accidental qualities—your habits, actions, and character—that persist through time? Which does this passage suggest is truly identity? How might you shift your self-definition today?
?
The passage notes that people seek auspicious times for beginnings like journeys or books. Are you tempted to rely on 'starting at the right time' rather than on the quality of your deeds? How does the distinction between time of occurrence and duration challenge you to focus on what you actually do rather than when you do it?
?
If God were to restore you identically after death, what accidental qualities would distinguish you from a new creation? Are you content with those qualities? What would you change so that your identity is marked by virtue rather than transient traits?

Refuting the Distinction Problem: Restored vs. New Creation
Refutation of the claim that restoration entails indistinguishable similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument that restoration is impossible due to indistinguishable similarity is refuted by showing that the same logic applies to any two similar things, including beginners.

Core Idea
The problem of distinguishing restored from new creation is no worse than distinguishing any two similar things.

In this passage, the author continues the second argument against the possibility of bodily restoration. The objection claimed that a restored body cannot be distinguished from a new, similar creation, implying impossibility. The response is clever: the same reasoning would also make two beginners impossible, yet they exist. Therefore, the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the fragmentary beginning; it continues from earlier.
The passage begins with 'المبتدأ يتصور له' which sets the context.
2
Identify the Core Claim
The passage states that any two similar things are distinguished by identity (huwiyya), regardless of being beginner or restored.
This is the basis for the refutation.
3
Understand the Refutation
The author asks: what special applicability does the impossibility have to the beginner and restored? It would also apply to two beginners. If that were valid, it would entail the impossibility of existence.
The refutation is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the Counter-Objection
If someone says 'new similar' means absolutely indistinguishable, the author denies its possibility because multiplicity always involves distinction. The same holds for two beginners.
This strengthens the refutation.

Source Text

المبتدأ
يتصور
له
المعاد
التماثل في الحقيقة ( وكل اثنين متماثلين ( متمايزان بالهوية سواء كانا مبتدئين أو معادين أو أحدهما مبتدأ والآخر معاداً، وأي اختصاص لهذا) الذي ذكروه من المحال ( بالمبتدأ والمعاد) بل هو جار في المبتدأين أيضاً فلو صح لزم امتناع وجود بعين ذلك الدليل، فإن قيل : المراد بالمثل المستأنف ما لا يتميز عن بوجه من الوجوه قلنا : إمكان وجوده بهذا المعنى ممنوع، إذ لا تعدد بلا تمايز على أن النقض بالمبتدأ إذا فرض له مثل كذلك وارد
The beginner is conceivable for it. The restored: similarity in reality. And every two similar things are distinguished by identity, whether they are beginners or restored, or one is beginner and the other restored. And what special applicability does this (impossibility) that they mentioned have to the beginner and the restored? Rather, it applies also to two beginners. For if it were valid, it would entail the impossibility of existence by that same proof. For if it is said: By the new similar is meant that which is not distinguished in any way, we say: The possibility of its existence in this sense is denied, because there is no multiplicity without distinction; moreover, the objection applies to a beginner if a similar is supposed for it, likewise.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage continues the second argument. It begins with a fragment about the beginner (al-mubtadaʾ) being conceivable. It then states that two similar things are distinguished by identity (huwiyya), whether they are beginners, restored, or one of each. The objection that this leads to impossibility is refuted by pointing out that the same objection would apply to two beginners, which is accepted. Therefore, the impossibility does not specifically affect the case of restoration. The text then addresses a possible definition of 'new similar' as something absolutely indistinguishable; it denies that such a thing can exist, as multiplicity always implies distinction, and this also applies to two beginners.

مثل مستأنف (mithl mustaʾnaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The restored thing cannot be identical to a previous thing because it would be indistinguishable from a new creation, thus impossible.
Correction: The same issue would apply to two beginners, yet they are possible; thus the argument does not prove impossibility.

Checkpoint
How does the text refute the claim that the restored thing cannot be distinguished from a new similar creation?
A) By denying that God can create such a similar thing.
B) By affirming that multiplicity necessitates distinction, and the same objection applies to two beginners.
C) By stating that the restored thing is identical in all respects.
D) By appealing to revelation.

Key Takeaway
The argument that restoration is impossible due to indistinguishable similarity fails because the same problem would apply to any two similar things, including beginners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can restore you after death, or does a hidden doubt linger in your heart? This lesson shows the logical flaw in denying resurrection—but is your faith based on logic or on certainty? What would it take for you to remove that doubt today?
?
If identity holds even for two similar things, then every soul is uniquely distinguished by Allah's knowledge. How does this truth affect your sense of accountability before Him? Are you living as if your identity matters eternally?
?
The argument here refutes an intellectual objection, but what about the deeper resistance in your soul? Are you really ready to stand before your Lord as the same person you are now—restored? What change must you make now to face that day with hope?

Third Argument: Identity Requires Distinction During Non-Existence
Third argument: need for distinction during non-existence and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument regarding the necessity of distinction during non-existence for identity judgments, and how the Mu'tazilite and Ash'ari schools respond to this challenge.

Core Idea
Identity judgment forces distinction during non-existence; Ash'aris evade it by denying external distinction.

This passage presents a pivotal argument in the debate over whether the non-existent is a thing. The argument claims that if a thing returns identical to itself, it must be distinguished during non-existence. But distinction in pure non-existence seems impossible. Two theological schools offer contrasting solutions. We will examine the reasoning and see how the Ash'ari response uses mental attributes to sidestep the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument and the two answers.
Notice the structure: argument, conditional, then two answers.
2
Unpack the argument
The argument states that a correct judgment of identity requires distinction during non-existence. Identify why this is claimed and why distinction is deemed impossible.
The impossibility is because pure negation has no differentiation.
3
Analyze the conditional
The judgment requires that the non-existent be described with 'validity of return'. This description necessitates distinction; otherwise, it would not be specific to that thing.
This conditional is key for the Ash'ari denial.
4
Compare the two answers
The Mu'tazilite answer accepts the distinction because the non-existent is a fixed entity. The Ash'ari answer denies the conditional: the judgment does not require external distinction because 'validity of return' is a mental attribute.
Focus on the concept of 'mental attribute' (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya).
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how the Ash'ari answer treats distinction during non-existence as imaginary, similar to possibles not yet existent.
This upholds the principle that non-existence is pure negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث الحكم الصحيح ( بأن هذا الذي وجد الآن ( عين الأول يستدعي تميزه حال العدم، وأنه ) أي التميز حال العدم ( محال ) لأن النفي الصرف لا تميز وأما الشرطية فلأن صحة ذلك الحكم تستدعي اتصاف ذلك المعدوم حال عدمه بصحة العود إذ لو لم يتصف بصحة العود، لما أمكن عوده فلا يصح ذلك الحكم عليه واتصافه بصحة العود يقتضي امتيازه وإلا لم يكن ذلك الاتصاف أولى به من غيره، (الجواب : على أصل المعتزلة وهو كون المعدوم شيئاً ) أي أمراً ثابتاً متقرراً حال العدم (ظاهر)، لأن إبطال التالي حينئذ ممنوع وما ذكر في بيانه مردود (و) الجواب على أصلنا) منع الشرطية (لأنا) نمنع استدعاءه ) أي استدعاء ذلك الحكم وصحته (للتميز) في الخارج فإن صحة العود صفة اعتبارية هي امكان الوجود بعد زواله فلا يكون الاتصاف بها مقتضياً للامتياز الخارجي، بل التميز في الخارج (إنما يحصل حال الإعادة أعني زمان الوجود الثاني (وهو) أي التميز الحاصل للمعدوم حال عدمه، واتصافه بصحة العود ) أمر وهمي لا حقيقة (له بحسب الخارج كالتميز الحاصل في الممكنات التي لم توجد بعد فإن قيل: نحن ندعي لزوم هذا التميز قلنا فبطلانه ممنوع حينئذ لأن مثل هذا التميز حاصل للمعدومات الصرفة كالممتنعات .
The third: The correct judgment that what exists now is identical to the first requires its distinction during non-existence, and that (i.e., distinction during non-existence) is impossible because pure negation has no distinction. As for the conditional, because the validity of that judgment requires that the non-existent at the time of non-existence be described with the validity of return, for if it were not described with the validity of return, then its return would be impossible, so that judgment would not be valid for it. And its being described with the validity of return necessitates its distinction; otherwise, that description would not be more appropriate to it than to another. The answer: According to the Mu'tazila's principle—that the non-existent is a thing, i.e., a fixed determinate entity during non-existence—it is obvious, because then the falsity of the consequent is not granted, and what was mentioned in its explanation is refuted. And the answer according to our principle (Ash'ari) is to deny the conditional, because we deny that that judgment and its validity require distinction externally. For the validity of return is a mental attribute, which is the possibility of existence after its removal, so being described with it does not necessitate external distinction. Rather, external distinction is only attained at the time of restoration, i.e., the time of the second existence. And what is (claimed as) distinction for the non-existent during non-existence and its being described with the validity of return is an imaginary matter with no reality externally, like the distinction attained in the possibles that have not yet come into existence. For if it is said: We claim the necessity of this distinction, we say: Its falsity is not granted then, because such distinction is attained for pure non-existents, like impossible things.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument: if we judge that the now-existing thing is the same as the first (restored), then it must be distinguished during its state of non-existence. But distinction during pure non-existence is impossible because pure negation has no differentiations. The argument then explains a conditional: the validity of the judgment requires that the non-existent be described with 'validity of return' (ṣiḥḥat al-ʿawd), which in turn requires distinction, otherwise that description would not be specific to it. Two answers are given. The first is according to the Mu'tazilite principle: the non-existent is a 'thing' (shayʾ) with fixed reality, so distinction is possible. The second is according to the Ash'arite principle: they deny the conditional—the judgment does not require external distinction because 'validity of return' is a mental attribute (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya) that does not entail external distinction. External distinction occurs only at the time of restoration. The claim that distinction occurs during non-existence is imaginary, like the distinction among possibles before existence or even among impossibles.

Key Terms
حكم صحيح (ḥukm ṣaḥīḥ)
تميز (tamyīz)
عدم (ʿadam)
صحة العود (ṣiḥḥat al-ʿawd)
المعدوم شيئاً (al-maʿdūm shayʾan)
صفة اعتبارية (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'aris simply deny any distinction for non-existents, leading to incoherence.
Correction: Ash'aris do not deny distinction altogether; they hold that distinction occurs at the time of restoration (second existence), not during non-existence. The 'validity of return' is a mental attribute that does not require external distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari answer, why does the judgment of identity not require distinction during non-existence?
A) Because the non-existent is a thing with fixed reality.
B) Because the validity of return is a mental attribute that does not necessitate external distinction.
C) Because distinction is impossible altogether.
D) Because the restored thing is not identical.

Key Takeaway
The third argument forces a choice: either accept that the non-existent has some reality (Mu'tazila) or deny that identity judgment requires external distinction during non-existence (Ash'ari), using mental attributes to avoid the problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your identity—who you are—is preserved by Allah even when you are nothing? How does this affect your trust that He will restore you on the Day of Resurrection exactly as you are, with all your deeds and intentions? Will you live today as if that restoration is certain?
?
The Ash'ari say that distinction during non-existence is imaginary—your essence is not a fixed thing outside of Allah's knowledge. If you have no independent reality, what is the basis of your worth before Allah? Does that humble you to the point that your only boast is in His mercy?
?
If the non-existent has no reality (as Ash'aris hold), then your past sins—though recorded—are ontologically nothing. Can you truly believe that your sins are erased by repentance, not because they have some fixed existence that is removed, but because they never had any reality apart from Allah's knowledge? What would it mean to treat them as real no more?

Objection to the Third Argument and the Parallel with Creation
Objection and response to the third argument's formulation and a parallel with creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the third argument's formulation and how a parallel with creation ex nihilo is used to refine and defend the argument.

Core Idea
The third argument's formulation is refined by a parallel with creation ex nihilo.

The commentary critiques the third argument's original formulation, arguing that inserting the impossibility of judgment about the validity of return is unnecessary. It suggests a more direct proof and then raises a parallel objection regarding creation, demonstrating that the same reasoning applies to both restoration and original creation, thus strengthening the third argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: objection, reformulation, and parallel with creation.
The passage uses technical terms like ḥukm, ṣiḥḥat al-ʿawd, and iḥdāth.
2
Understand the objection to the original formulation
The commentary argues that the original third argument's insertion of 'impossibility of judgment due to lack of distinction' is useless. The return is possible through the agent's power without prior judgment.
The better formulation is direct: when an existent becomes non-existent, it cannot be described with the validity of return, so its return is impossible.
3
See the parallel with creation ex nihilo
If the original argument's logic were accepted, it would also preclude creation from nothing, because before creation the thing is non-existent and would need distinction. The same answer that saves creation also saves restoration.
This parallel shows that the third argument is consistent with standard kalām thinking on creation.

Source Text

قوله : ( الثالث الحكم الصحيح إلخ يرد عليه أن توسيط امتناع الحكم بصحة العود لانتفاء التميز لغو لا فائدة له أصلا، إذ لا يمتنع وقوع العود بتأثير الفاعل من غير أن يتصور ويحكم عليه بشيء من الأحكام فالأولى أن يقرر الدليل بأنه إذا عدم الموجود لا يتصف بصحة العود فلا يمكن عوده، وإنما قلنا : لا يتصف بها لأن اتصافه بها يقتضي امتيازه . قوله: (والجواب إلخ) وأيضاً لو صح ما ذكر لزم أن لا يجوز إحداث شيء ووجوده أصلاً وإلا يصدق الحكم عليه حال عدمه قبل إحداثه أنه يجوز إحداثه، ويتصف بجواز الحدوث وذلك يستدعي التميز فما هو الجواب في جواب الإحداث فهو الجواب في جواب الإعادة .
His saying: (The third: the correct judgment etc.) is objected to: The insertion of the impossibility of the judgment of the validity of return due to lack of distinction is useless, having no benefit at all, because the occurrence of return through the agent's effect is not impossible without conceiving and judging it with any judgment. So it is better to formulate the proof as: When the existent becomes non-existent, it is not described with the validity of return, so its return is impossible. And we only said: It is described with it because its being described with it necessitates its distinction. His saying: (And the answer etc.) Also, if what was mentioned were valid, it would necessitate that it is not permissible to originate a thing and bring it into existence at all; otherwise, the judgment would apply to it during its non-existence before its origination that it is permissible to originate it, and it would be described with the possibility of origination, which requires distinction. So whatever answer is given in the case of origination is the same answer in the case of restoration.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a commentary objecting to the third argument's formulation. It argues that the insertion of the impossibility of the judgment (ḥukm) about the validity of return due to lack of distinction is redundant because the return itself is possible through the agent's power without any prior judgment. The better formulation is directly: when an existent ceases, it cannot be described with the validity of return, so its return is impossible; the description itself would require distinction. Then it raises a parallel: if the third argument's logic were accepted, it would also preclude creation ex nihilo (iḥdāth), because before creation, the thing is non-existent and would need distinction for the possibility of creation. The same answer that avoids this in creation (iḥdāth) should apply to restoration (iʿāda).

Key Terms
حكم (ḥukm)
صحة العود (ṣiḥḥat al-ʿawd)
توسيط (tawsīṭ)
إحداث (iḥdāth)
جواز الحدوث (jawāz al-ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument is sound as originally stated.
Correction: The commentary shows a more direct formulation and uses a parallel with creation to refine the argument.

Checkpoint
How does the commentary suggest reformulating the third argument?
A) Keep the same but add more premises.
B) Directly state that the non-existent cannot have the validity of return, so return is impossible.
C) Eliminate the need for distinction entirely.
D) Use a scriptural proof.

Key Takeaway
The third argument is strengthened by reformulating it directly and showing it aligns with the logic of creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the objection that the third argument is 'useless,' does it stir any doubt in your heart about the rationality of resurrection? How does the commentator's reformulation remove that doubt, and are you willing to trust it fully?
?
The parallel with creation ex nihilo shows that God’s power does not depend on prior judgments. Can you honestly say you rely on this same power for your own resurrection, even when you cannot conceive how it will happen?
?
If the non-existent cannot be described with attributes, then your own future restoration depends entirely on God’s will, not on any inherent quality in you. How does this truth challenge your sense of self-sufficiency, and will you let it deepen your reliance on Him?

The Logical Contradiction in Mu'tazila Nullification
Ash'ari reductio against Mu'tazila nullification via dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma the Ash'aris use to refute the Mu'tazila theory of nullification (al-ihbat).

Core Idea
Two equal entitlements cannot nullify each other without contradiction.

Having already refuted the foundation of the Mu'tazila theory—that sin necessarily merits punishment and obedience necessarily merits reward—the Ash'aris now turn to the branch built upon it: nullification. They present a precise logical dilemma specifically targeting the balancing model of the Bahshamiyya. This argument shows that the very concept of mutual nullification leads to an absurdity: something existing and not existing at the same time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: first a statement of refutation, then a conditional argument about two equal entitlements.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the two options of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities for how two equal entitlements could nullify each other: simultaneously or one after the other. Identify each and consider the consequence.
Focus on the simultaneous case first—it leads to a clear contradiction.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Ask yourself: why is simultaneous nullification impossible? The passage says each would be existent and non-existent at the same time. Write down why this is a logical impossibility.
This establishes the Ash'ari refutation as logically sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما أبطلنا الأصل الذي هو استحقاق العقاب والثواب بالمعصية والطاعة بطل الفرع المبني عليه وهو الإحباط مطلقاً سواء كان بطريق الموازنة أو غيرها ثم نقول لهم أي للبهشمية: كل واحد من الاستحقاقين المتساويين لو أبطل الآخر فإما معاً فيكون الشيء موجوداً حال كونه معدوماً لأن وجود كل منهما يقارن عدم الآخر فيلزم عدمهما معاً حال وجودهما معاً أو لا معاً بل ينعدم أحدهما فيبطل الآخر ثم يكر الآخر.
And when we have invalidated the foundation, which is the entitlement to punishment and reward by sin and obedience, the branch built upon it is invalidated, namely nullification absolutely, whether by way of balancing or otherwise. Then we say to them, i.e., to the Bahshamiyya: Each one of the two equal entitlements, if it nullifies the other, then either both together, in which case a thing would be existent while it is non-existent, because the existence of each is concomitant with the non-existence of the other, so it follows that both are non-existent while both are existent; or not together, but rather one ceases and then nullifies the other, then the other returns.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) refuting the Mu'tazila theory of nullification. It begins by stating that since the Ash'aris have already refuted the foundational principle – that sin merits punishment and obedience merits reward in a necessary sense – the entire branch of nullification (al-ihbat) collapses. Then it addresses specifically the Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim) who proposed a balancing model. The argument is a dilemma: if we suppose two equal entitlements (to reward and punishment) are present, and each is supposed to nullify the other, then either they nullify each other simultaneously or one after the other. If simultaneously, then each would be existent (since it exists at the moment of nullification) and non-existent (since it is being nullified), which is a contradiction: something cannot be both existent and non-existent at the same time. If they nullify one after the other, say the reward-entitlement is nullified first by the punishment-entitlement, then the punishment-entitlement would have no opposite to nullify it, so it would remain, and then the reward-entitlement would return? This is incoherent. Thus the balancing theory is logically impossible.

Key Terms
أصل
فرع
استحقاق
موازنة
متساويان
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari argument assumes reward and punishment are always equal in magnitude.
Correction: The argument only assumes equality for the sake of the dilemma; it does not depend on actual equality. It shows that even if they were equal, nullification is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory consequence the Ash'ari argument derives from simultaneous nullification of two equal entitlements?
A) Both entitlements remain
B) One entitlement is negated completely
C) Something exists and does not exist at the same time
D) The entitlements become unequal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila theory of nullification leads to a logical contradiction because two equal entitlements cannot mutually cancel without implying something is both existent and non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage exposes the logical fallacy of 'balancing' good and bad deeds. In your own life, do you subconsciously rely on a mental ledger, thinking your good deeds can cancel out sins? How does this lesson shatter that assumption, and what must you now place your trust in instead of a 'balance'?
?
The proof here rests on the principle that a thing cannot be both existent and non-existent. Yet, do you sometimes hold contradictory beliefs about your spiritual state—claiming faith while persisting in major sins? How does this logical truth demand consistency in your faith and actions?
?
If your sins cannot be nullified by your good deeds, then what is your only hope for forgiveness? Have you truly surrendered to the fact that salvation depends solely on Allah's mercy, not on a scale of deeds? What practical change will you make today to live in light of that reality?

Mu'tazilite Agreement on Inequality and Equal Acts Canceling
Al-Jurjani presents the Mu'tazilite view on equal acts canceling and gives a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that equal reward and punishment would cancel each other, and al-Jurjani's response based on the multiplication of good deeds and the possibility of gratuitous reward.

Core Idea
Equal good and evil do not necessarily cancel; divine grace multiplies good deeds.

This passage examines a Mu'tazilite line of reasoning about the impossibility of equal reward and punishment, and then provides a decisive response from the Sunni perspective. It highlights a key difference between rationalist and scriptural approaches to divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
Notice the parentheses indicating inserted commentary.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify al-Jubba'i's intellectual impossibility argument and Abu Hashim's consensus-based objection.
Al-Jubba'i says simultaneous or successive cancellation is impossible; Abu Hashim says intellect allows it but consensus forbids.
3
Analyze the response's two points
Focus on the response: first, good deeds are multiplied tenfold; second, even if cancellation occurs, gratuitous reward is possible.
This shows how divine grace overcomes strict equality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب . قد اتفق المعتزلة ) أي الجبائيان وأتباعهما ) على أنه لا يتساوى الثواب والعقاب ) أي لا يتساوى الطاعات والزلات وإلا) تساقطا إذ لا يجوز بقاؤهما معاً لما مر من التنافي بين الثواب والعقاب وبين استحقاقيهما أيضاً ولا يجوز إسقاط أحدهما بالآخر لتساويهما فرضاً، وإذا تساقطا معاً ( فلا يكون ثمة ثواب ولا عقاب وإنه محال فعند الجبائي (عقلاً لأن إبطال كل منهما للآخر إما معاً أو على التعاقب وكلاهما محال لما عرفت ( وعند أبي هاشم إِن العقل لا يدل على امتناع التساوي إذ ما من مرتبة من مراتب الطاعات إلا ويجوز العقل بلوغ المعاصي إليها وبالعكس ولا استحالة العقل في تساقطهما أيضا لأن كل واحد من العملين يؤثر في استحقاق الآخر كما مر إنما استحالته ( للإجماع على أن لا خروج ) للمكلف ( عنهما ) بل كل مكلف إما من أهل الجنة أو النار ، ولا بد له من الخلود في أحدهما ولا يتصور وقوع أحد الخلودين من جهة أن مع التساوي في الموجب وإنما فسرنا المعتزلة بالجبائيين وأتباعهما لما سلف من جمهورهم ذهبوا إلى إحباط جميع الطاعات بمعصية واحدة وحينئذ فإحباط المعصية للطاعة المساوية لها يكون عندهم أولى . ( والجواب : لم لا يجوز) على تقدير تساوي الطاعات والمعاصي ( أن يثاب لما مر من جانب الثواب أرجح ) فإن الحسنة تجزى بعشر أمثالها والسيئة لا تجزى إلا بمثلها، (و) أيضاً على تقدير التساوي والتساقط معاً لا يلزم خلو المكلف عن الثواب والعقاب لجواز التفضل بالثواب عندنا
Appendix. The Mu'tazilites, meaning al-Jubba'i and his followers, have agreed that reward and punishment are not equal, that is, acts of obedience and slips are not equal; otherwise they would cancel each other, because it is not permissible for both to remain together due to the previously mentioned incompatibility between reward and punishment and between their deserving, and it is not permissible to drop one by the other because they are equal by assumption. And if they cancel together, then there would be neither reward nor punishment, and that is impossible. According to al-Jubba'i, intellectually, because the annulment of each by the other is either simultaneous or successive, both being impossible for what you know. According to Abu Hashim, the intellect does not indicate the impossibility of equality, for there is no rank of ranks of acts of obedience except that the intellect allows sins to reach it and vice versa, and there is no intellectual impossibility in their mutual cancellation either, because each of the two actions affects the deserving of the other as mentioned. Its impossibility is only due to the consensus that there is no exit for the obligated from them, rather every obligated is either among the people of Paradise or the Fire, and he must be eternal in one of them, and the occurrence of one of the two eternities cannot be imagined given the equality in the cause. And we only limited the Mu'tazilites to al-Jubba'i and his followers because of what preceded: that the majority of them went to the nullification of all acts of obedience by a single sin, and then the nullification of the sin for the obedience equal to it would be more appropriate according to them. And the answer: why is it not permissible, on the assumption of equality of acts of obedience and sins, that he be rewarded because the side of reward is more preponderant? For a good deed is recompensed by ten times its like, and an evil deed is only recompensed by its like. And also, on the assumption of equality and mutual cancellation together, the obligated is not necessarily devoid of reward and punishment because it is permissible for God to bestow reward gratuitously according to us.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is an appendix and response. It begins by stating the agreement of the Mu'tazilites (specifically al-Jubba'i and his followers) that reward and punishment are not equal. They argue that if they were equal, they would cancel each other because they cannot coexist and neither can be dropped by the other if equal. Al-Jubba'i says this mutual cancellation is intellectually impossible whether simultaneous or successive. Abu Hashim disagrees, saying the intellect does not forbid equality, because the intellect can conceive of sins reaching any rank of obedience and vice versa, and mutual cancellation is not impossible; the impossibility is only due to consensus that every obligated person is destined for either Paradise or Hell eternally, so one cannot be devoid of both. The author then explains why he restricts 'Mu'tazilites' to al-Jubba'i's school: because the majority hold that a single sin nullifies all previous good deeds, so nullifying a sin by an equal good deed is even more appropriate for them. The response argues that even assuming equality, reward is still possible because a good deed is multiplied tenfold, outweighing the sin. Also, even if they cancel, the obligated person may still be granted gratuitous reward by God.

Key Terms
إحباط (ihbat)
استحقاق (istihqaq)
تساقط (tasaqut)
تفضل (tafaddul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equal good and bad deeds necessarily cancel out, leaving the person with neither reward nor punishment.
Correction: The response shows that reward is multiplied (ten times) so it outweighs punishment, and even if cancellation occurs, God may grant gratuitous reward.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, why is mutual cancellation of equal reward and punishment impossible?
A) Because the intellect proves equality is impossible
B) Because it would require simultaneous or successive annulment, both impossible
C) Because consensus forbids it
D) Because good deeds are always multiplied

Key Takeaway
Even if good and evil deeds are equal, divine grace through multiplication of good deeds and gratuitous reward ensures that believers are not left without reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own balance of good and bad deeds, do you secretly hope for a strict accounting or for God's multiplication of good deeds? How does today's lesson confront your hidden assumptions about divine fairness?
?
If God's reward is ultimately a gift (tafaddul) and not merely a transaction, how should that reshape your motivation for obedience—from fear of cancellation to hope in generosity?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted that equal deeds must cancel, leaving the person without reward. In your life, are there areas where you act as if your good deeds are cancelled by your sins, forgetting the tenfold multiplication? What would it look like to trust in God's grace instead?

The First Gloss: Critique of Mutual Cancellation
Al-Jurjani critiques the assumption that equal actions mutually cancel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani critiques the assumption that two equal actions mutually cancel, and how al-Jubba'i's school avoids this conclusion.

Core Idea
Equal acts do not necessarily cancel; the later act can nullify the earlier.

Al-Jurjani begins his glosses by examining an assumption in the nullification argument. He points out that the reasoning about mutual cancellation depends on which theological presentation one follows, and that al-Jubba'i's school holds that the later action has strength to nullify the earlier according to its measure. This lesson walks through his first critical point.

Guided Path
1
Read the first gloss carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Jurjani starts by quoting a previous statement ('otherwise they cancel each other') and then investigates it.
2
Identify the two presentations
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between the presentation of al-Amidi and that of the Imam (al-Iji). Note their implications for mutual cancellation.
3
Understand al-Jubba'i's position
According to al-Jubba'i, the later action has strength (quwwa) to nullify the earlier according to its measure, so the later remains; thus no cancellation occurs.
4
Ask why this matters
Reflect on how this gloss shows that theological assumptions affect arguments about reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإلا تساقطا فيه بحث لأن هذا الكلام إنما يلائم تقرير الأمدي لا تقرير الإمام الذي اختاره المصنف لأن مذهب الجبائي على هذا قوة المتأخر حتى يحبط السابق بقدره ويبقى نفسه فلا يلزم على تقدير تساويهما تساقطهما بل التأخر يحبط السابق ويبقى نفسه قوله : ( بأن كل واحد من العملين يؤثر في الاستحقاق إلخ والحق أنه ليس هاهنا تأثير وتأثر حقيقي بل معنى إحباط الطاعة أن الله تعالى لا يثيب عليها ومعنى الموازنة أنه تعالى لا يثيب عليها ويترك العقوبة على المعصية بقدرها فلا محذور. قوله : ( فإن الحسنة تجزى بعشر أمثالها إلخ) فيه بحث أشرنا إليه سابقاً، وهو أن المساواة والمفاوتة عندهم ليست باعتبار العدد بل بالنظر إلى مقادير الأجور والأوزار فالمساواة الممنوعة عندهم هي هذه بعينها يعني مقابلة عشر سيئات بحسنة مثلاً وإذا حمل التساوي الممنوع على هذا لم يتجه الوجه الأول من الجواب، فتأمل.
His saying: (otherwise they cancel each other) there is investigation in it, because this speech only suits the presentation of al-Amidi, not the presentation of the Imam which the author chose, because the school of al-Jubba'i according to this is the strength of the later such that it nullifies the earlier according to its measure and remains itself, so on the assumption of their equality, their mutual cancellation is not necessary; rather the later nullifies the earlier and remains itself. His saying: (that each of the two actions affects the deserving etc.) And the truth is that there is no real affecting and being affected here; rather the meaning of nullification of obedience is that God does not reward for it, and the meaning of balancing is that He does not reward for it and leaves punishment for the sin according to its measure, so there is no problem. His saying: (for a good deed is recompensed by ten times its like etc.) there is investigation in it which we pointed to earlier, and that is that equality and disparity according to them are not with respect to number but with respect to the measures of rewards and sins; so the prohibited equality is exactly this, meaning the comparison of ten sins by one good deed for example. And if the prohibited equality is interpreted in this way, the first aspect of the answer does not apply, so reflect.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage contains three glosses on the preceding arguments. The first gloss critiques the assumption that equal acts would cancel, noting that this reasoning fits al-Amidi's presentation, not that of the Imam (al-Iji) chosen by the author (al-Jurjani). According to al-Jubba'i, the later action has strength to nullify the earlier according to its measure and remains itself, so mutual cancellation is not necessary. The second gloss clarifies that there is no real causal effect between the two actions; rather nullification merely means that God does not reward that good deed, and balancing means He does not reward but punishes the sin proportionally, so no contradiction. The third gloss notes that the objection assuming multiplication of good deeds begs the question, because equality and disparity are based on the magnitude of rewards and sins, not mere counting; the prohibited equality is when a sin of ten units is compared to a good deed of one unit, and if so, the first answer fails.

Key Terms
تساقط (tasāquṭ)
تقرير (taqrīr)
قوة المتأخر (quwwat al-muta'akhkhir)
إحباط (ihbāṭ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first gloss, what does al-Jubba'i's school say about two equal actions?
A) They cancel each other completely
B) The later nullifies the earlier according to its measure and remains itself
C) They both remain effective
D) The earlier nullifies the later

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani's first gloss shows that the assumption of mutual cancellation depends on which theological model one adopts; al-Jubba'i's model allows the later act to prevail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own deeds, do you assume that your later good deeds cancel out earlier sins, or do you fear that the later acts themselves might be nullified? Which school’s logic do you unconsciously follow, and how does that affect your daily efforts to repent and do good?
?
Has there been a time when you relied on the idea that your past mistakes are 'cancelled' by later obedience, only to feel uncertain about your sincerity? How does Al-Jurjani’s point that the later act must have 'strength' challenge your reliance on mere quantity over quality?
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you secretly bargain that one big good deed will wipe out many small sins? What would it mean to truly accept that the later act must prevail on its own merit, not just as a eraser—and how might that change the way you approach your next action?

The Second Gloss: No Real Causal Effect
Al-Jurjani clarifies that nullification and balancing involve no real causal effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's clarification that nullification and balancing do not involve real causal interaction but simply describe God's decision not to reward or to proportion punishment.

Core Idea
Nullification and balancing are descriptive, not causal.

The second gloss addresses a potential misunderstanding: that one action literally influences another. Al-Jurjani clarifies that there is no real affecting (ta'thir) between actions; rather, nullification (ihbat) means God refrains from rewarding the good deed, and balancing (muwazanah) means He does not reward but punishes the sin proportionally. This lesson explains this important distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the second gloss
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the claim that each action 'affects' deserving, and al-Jurjani's corrective.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'ihbat' (nullification) and 'muwazanah' (balancing). Al-Jurjani redefines them without causal language.
3
Grasp the core point
There is no real influence between actions; it is simply God's decision. This removes the earlier problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإلا تساقطا فيه بحث لأن هذا الكلام إنما يلائم تقرير الأمدي لا تقرير الإمام الذي اختاره المصنف لأن مذهب الجبائي على هذا قوة المتأخر حتى يحبط السابق بقدره ويبقى نفسه فلا يلزم على تقدير تساويهما تساقطهما بل التأخر يحبط السابق ويبقى نفسه قوله : ( بأن كل واحد من العملين يؤثر في الاستحقاق إلخ والحق أنه ليس هاهنا تأثير وتأثر حقيقي بل معنى إحباط الطاعة أن الله تعالى لا يثيب عليها ومعنى الموازنة أنه تعالى لا يثيب عليها ويترك العقوبة على المعصية بقدرها فلا محذور. قوله : ( فإن الحسنة تجزى بعشر أمثالها إلخ) فيه بحث أشرنا إليه سابقاً، وهو أن المساواة والمفاوتة عندهم ليست باعتبار العدد بل بالنظر إلى مقادير الأجور والأوزار فالمساواة الممنوعة عندهم هي هذه بعينها يعني مقابلة عشر سيئات بحسنة مثلاً وإذا حمل التساوي الممنوع على هذا لم يتجه الوجه الأول من الجواب، فتأمل.
His saying: (otherwise they cancel each other) there is investigation in it, because this speech only suits the presentation of al-Amidi, not the presentation of the Imam which the author chose, because the school of al-Jubba'i according to this is the strength of the later such that it nullifies the earlier according to its measure and remains itself, so on the assumption of their equality, their mutual cancellation is not necessary; rather the later nullifies the earlier and remains itself. His saying: (that each of the two actions affects the deserving etc.) And the truth is that there is no real affecting and being affected here; rather the meaning of nullification of obedience is that God does not reward for it, and the meaning of balancing is that He does not reward for it and leaves punishment for the sin according to its measure, so there is no problem. His saying: (for a good deed is recompensed by ten times its like etc.) there is investigation in it which we pointed to earlier, and that is that equality and disparity according to them are not with respect to number but with respect to the measures of rewards and sins; so the prohibited equality is exactly this, meaning the comparison of ten sins by one good deed for example. And if the prohibited equality is interpreted in this way, the first aspect of the answer does not apply, so reflect.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage contains three glosses on the preceding arguments. The first gloss critiques the assumption that equal acts would cancel, noting that this reasoning fits al-Amidi's presentation, not that of the Imam (al-Iji) chosen by the author (al-Jurjani). According to al-Jubba'i, the later action has strength to nullify the earlier according to its measure and remains itself, so mutual cancellation is not necessary. The second gloss clarifies that there is no real causal effect between the two actions; rather nullification merely means that God does not reward that good deed, and balancing means He does not reward but punishes the sin proportionally, so no contradiction. The third gloss notes that the objection assuming multiplication of good deeds begs the question, because equality and disparity are based on the magnitude of rewards and sins, not mere counting; the prohibited equality is when a sin of ten units is compared to a good deed of one unit, and if so, the first answer fails.

Key Terms
إحباط (ihbāṭ)
موازنة (muwāzanah)
تأثير (ta'thīr)
استحقاق (istiḥqāq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that nullification involves a causal interaction between actions.
Correction: The gloss explains it is simply God's decision not to reward, not a real causal effect.

Checkpoint
According to the second gloss, what does 'ihbat' (nullification) mean?
A) One action physically cancels another
B) God does not reward the good deed
C) God forgives the sin
D) Both actions are canceled

Key Takeaway
The second gloss teaches that nullification and balancing are descriptive terms for God's decision, not causal processes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own good deeds that may be overshadowed by sins, do you fear that your sins will 'cancel out' your good deeds in a causal way? How does this lesson—that nullification is simply God's decision not to reward rather than a causal process—change your view of God's justice and mercy? Will you now trust more in His wisdom or feel more complacent?
?
If nullification and balancing are purely divine decisions with no causal effect from the actions themselves, how does that affect the way you seek forgiveness and repent? Does it make you more hopeful that God can still accept your good deeds, or does it remind you of His absolute sovereignty over reward and punishment? How will you respond to that sovereignty today?
?
This lesson implies that a good deed does not 'lose' its intrinsic merit due to a sin; rather, God simply chooses not to reward it in certain circumstances. How does this reshape your understanding of your intentions? Will you now strive more earnestly to purify your intentions, knowing that no sin can automatically destroy your good deeds unless God wills it? What is one concrete step you will take to act on this truth?

The Third Gloss: Equality by Magnitude, Not Number
Al-Jurjani explains that equality is based on magnitudes, not numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's point that equality and disparity between actions are based on the magnitude of rewards and sins, not merely on the number of good and bad deeds.

Core Idea
Equality is about magnitude, not count.

The third gloss addresses an objection based on the multiplication of rewards: if one good deed is rewarded ten times, then how can equality be established? Al-Jurjani notes that equality (musawah) among theologians is based on the magnitudes (aqdar) of rewards and sins, not on mere numerical count. So comparing ten sins to one good deed is an example of prohibited equality. This lesson explains this critical nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the third gloss
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the critique of the first answer.
2
Understand the distinction
Equality is measured by the magnitude of reward/punishment, not by the number of deeds.
3
See why the first answer fails
If equality is about magnitude, then comparing ten sins to one good deed is equality, so the first answer is invalid.

Time-Specific and Detailed Repentance Defined
Al-Āmidī defines time-specific and detailed repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of repentance—time-specific and detailed—and the central premise that remorse due to sin's ugliness should be universal.

Core Idea
Repentance can be limited in time or scope, but true remorse for ugliness demands universality.

This lesson introduces the fourth ruling on repentance. Scholars discuss whether repentance must cover all sins and all times. We begin by defining two types: time-specific (repenting only for a period) and detailed (repenting only from some sins). The passage sets up the key premise: if remorse is because the act is ugly (qubḥ), it should extend to all ugly acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definitions
Read the lines defining time-specific and detailed repentance, along with the premise that universal remorse follows if based on ugliness.
2
Identify the two types
Note the examples: repenting after a year of sin (time-specific) and repenting from adultery but not drinking (detailed).
3
Reflect on the premise
Consider why remorse based on ugliness should cover all times and sins, as the text says 'since its general nature does not cover both'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : من أحكام التوبة لهم في التوبة المؤقتة مثل أن يذنب سنة و في التوبة المفصلة نحو أن يتوب عن الزنا دون شرب الخمر على أن الندم إذا كان لكونه ذنباً عم الأوقات والذنوب جميعاً، إذ لا يعم عمومه لهما فذهب بعضهم إلى أنه يجب العموم لأنه إذا ندم على ذنب في وقت ولم يندم على ذنب آخر، أو في وقت آخر ظهر أنه لم يندم عليه لقبحه، وإلا ندم على قبائحه كلها لاشتراكها في العلة المقتضية للندم، وندم أيضاً في جميع الأوقات، وإذا لم يكن ندمه لقبحه لم يكن توبة. وذهب آخرون منهم إلى أنه لا يجب العموم كما في الواجبات فإنه قد يأتي المأمور ببعضها دون بعض، وفي بعض الأوقات دون بعضها ويكون المأتي به صحيحا في نفسه بلا توقف على غيره، مع أن العلة المقتضية للإتيان بالواجب هي كون الفعل حسناً واجباً. فإن قيل: مراتب الحسن مختلفة في الأفعال ويتفاوت أيضاً اقتضاؤها بحسب الأوقات. قلنا: مراتب القبح أيضاً كذلك. والأشاعرة وافقوا هؤلاء في صحة التوبتين. قوله: (كما في الواجبات) قيل: لا يتم القياس على الواجب للفرق بين المقيس والمقيس عليه، فإن ترك القبيح لكونه نفياً لا يحصل إلا بترك جميع القبائح بخلاف الإتيان بالواجب، فإنه لكونه إثباتاً يحصل بإثبات واجب دون واجب. ورد بأن الكلام في الواجبات التي صدرت عن الشارع الأمر بكل واحد منها على حدة كالصلاة والصوم والزكاة مثلاً لا في إفراد واجب أمر الشارع بالإتيان بواحد منها لا على التعيين كإعتاق رقبة أي رقبة كانت، وظاهر أن تمام الامتثال بالأمر لا يحصل بإتيان واحد منها دون آخر بل بإتيان الجميع، كما أن تمام الامتثال بالنهي لا يحصل بترك قبيح دون آخر من غير فرق. فلو لم تصح التوبة عن بعض القبائح دون بعض لم يصح إتيان بعض الواجبات دون بعض، لكن التالي باطل اتفاقاً فكذا المقدم.
Fourth: Among the rulings of repentance, they have regarding time-specific repentance, such as one who sins for a year, and detailed repentance, such as one who repents from adultery but not from wine drinking, on the basis that if remorse is because it is a sin, it encompasses all times and all sins, since its general nature does not cover both. So some of them held that universality is obligatory because if he regrets a sin at a time and does not regret another sin, or at another time, it appears that he did not regret it for its ugliness; otherwise he would have regretted all its ugly deeds due to their sharing the cause necessitating remorse, and he would also regret at all times. And if his remorse is not due to its ugliness, it is not repentance. Others among them held that universality is not obligatory, as in the obligations (wājibāt), for one may perform some of what is commanded without others, and at some times without others, and what is performed is valid in itself without depending on others, despite the cause necessitating the performance of the obligation being that the act is good (ḥasan) and obligatory. If it is said: the levels of goodness differ in acts, and their motivations also vary according to times. We say: the levels of ugliness (qubḥ) are likewise. And the Ash'aris agreed with these (latter ones) regarding the validity of the two repentances. His statement: (as in the obligations). It was said: the analogy to the obligation is not complete due to the difference between what is compared and what it is compared to, for abandoning the ugly, being a negation, does not occur except by abandoning all ugly deeds, unlike performing the obligation, for being an affirmation, it occurs by affirming one obligation without another. And it was refuted by saying: the discussion concerns obligations for which the Lawgiver issued a command for each one separately, like prayer, fasting, and zakāt for example, not a single obligation that the Lawgiver commanded to perform one unspecified one like freeing a slave, any slave. And it is apparent that full compliance with a command is not achieved by performing one without another but by performing all, just as full compliance with a prohibition is not achieved by abandoning one ugly deed without another without difference. So if repentance from some ugly deeds without others were not valid, then performing some obligations without others would not be valid—but the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth ruling on repentance: whether repentance must be time-specific (muwaqqata) or detailed (mufaṣṣala), i.e., covering only certain sins or times. Some argued that if remorse is due to the sin's ugliness (qubḥ), it must be universal, as all sins share the same cause. Others argued universality is not required, analogizing to obligations (wājibāt) where performing one duty suffices without doing all. The Ash'aris agreed with the latter. A gloss adds that the analogy with obligations holds only for separate commands; for a single command, fulfillment requires doing all parts.

Key Terms
توبة مؤقتة (tawba muwaqqata)
توبة مفصلة (tawba mufaṣṣala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two types of repentance mentioned in this passage?
A) Time-specific and detailed
B) Universal and partial
C) Major and minor
D) Voluntary and obligatory

Key Takeaway
Repentance can be restricted to a time or a sin, but genuine remorse for ugliness demands universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever repented from a sin you committed yesterday but felt no regret for a similar sin last year? If your remorse is genuine because both are sins in God's sight, what holds you back from repenting from both?
?
When you ask God for forgiveness, do you examine whether your remorse is truly because the act was ugly and displeasing to Him, or is it just because of its consequences? Be honest: what would change if your repentance were driven by ugliness alone?
?
Right now, think of a sin you continue to excuse or minimize. If you truly believe all sins are ugly to God, what specific step will you take today to turn away from it completely, so that your repentance leaves no room for detailed convenience?

The Debate on Universal Remorse
Al-Āmidī presents two views on universal remorse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on whether repentance must cover all sins, as well as their reasoning.

Core Idea
Some say universal remorse is mandatory; others say it isn't, like obligations where partial performance is valid.

After defining the types, the text presents two opinions. One group argues that if remorse is due to ugliness, it must be universal; otherwise it is not true repentance. The other group argues that universality is not required, drawing an analogy to obligations (wājibāt) where one can perform some duties without others.

Guided Path
1
Read the two opinions
Identify the argument for universality and the counterargument using obligations.
The first group says all sins share the same cause; the second says obligations can be fulfilled individually.
2
Compare the reasoning
Note how each side uses the cause: qubḥ (ugliness) vs. ḥusn (goodness) and obligation.
3
Ask why the analogy matters
Consider if performing one duty is truly similar to repenting from one sin.
This is the core of the debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
ندم (nadam)
قبح (qubḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all repentance must be universal; the second opinion shows it can be partial.
Correction: The text presents a valid view that repentance from one sin can be genuine even if other sins remain unrepented, analogous to partial performance of obligations.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion, why is universality not required?
A) Because ugliness varies in degree
B) Because one can perform some obligations without others
C) Because time is irrelevant
D) Because remorse is subjective

Key Takeaway
The debate hinges on whether the cause of remorse (ugliness) necessitates universality, with the obligation analogy suggesting it does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you regret a sin you committed, do you examine your heart to see if your regret is truly because the act was ugly in the sight of Allah, or because of its worldly consequences? If only some of your sins cause you remorse, does that reveal something about the depth of your awareness of Allah's commands?
?
Consider a sin you have repented from but still find yourself committing again. Could it be that your initial remorse was not for the ugliness of the sin itself, but for its temporary harm? What would it look like to cultivate a remorse that is rooted in the very nature of the act being displeasing to Allah, regardless of outcomes?
?
The second opinion suggests that partial repentance is valid. Is there a sin you have never truly regretted because you have downplayed its ugliness? Will you today, in light of this lesson, bring that sin before Allah and repent from it solely because He hates it, even if you have felt no previous remorse?
