Ash'ari Position and the Obligation Analogy
Al-Āmidī presents Ash'ari agreement and rebuttal of the obligation analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on partial repentance and the refutation of the obligation analogy.

Core Idea
Ash'aris accept partial repentance, and the analogy to obligations is defended against criticism.

The passage continues with an objection that the levels of goodness vary, to which the reply is that ugliness also varies. Then the Ash'ari agreement is stated. A marginal note critiques the analogy, but a rebuttal defends it by distinguishing between separate commands and a single command. The lesson clarifies that the analogy holds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Notice how the objection about varying goodness is answered by varying ugliness.
2
Identify the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris agree that both types of repentance are valid.
This is a key theological stance.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The marginal note says the analogy fails because abandoning is negation; the rebuttal says it works for separate commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : من أحكام التوبة لهم في التوبة المؤقتة مثل أن يذنب سنة و في التوبة المفصلة نحو أن يتوب عن الزنا دون شرب الخمر على أن الندم إذا كان لكونه ذنباً عم الأوقات والذنوب جميعاً، إذ لا يعم عمومه لهما فذهب بعضهم إلى أنه يجب العموم لأنه إذا ندم على ذنب في وقت ولم يندم على ذنب آخر، أو في وقت آخر ظهر أنه لم يندم عليه لقبحه، وإلا ندم على قبائحه كلها لاشتراكها في العلة المقتضية للندم، وندم أيضاً في جميع الأوقات، وإذا لم يكن ندمه لقبحه لم يكن توبة. وذهب آخرون منهم إلى أنه لا يجب العموم كما في الواجبات فإنه قد يأتي المأمور ببعضها دون بعض، وفي بعض الأوقات دون بعضها ويكون المأتي به صحيحا في نفسه بلا توقف على غيره، مع أن العلة المقتضية للإتيان بالواجب هي كون الفعل حسناً واجباً. فإن قيل: مراتب الحسن مختلفة في الأفعال ويتفاوت أيضاً اقتضاؤها بحسب الأوقات. قلنا: مراتب القبح أيضاً كذلك. والأشاعرة وافقوا هؤلاء في صحة التوبتين. قوله: (كما في الواجبات) قيل: لا يتم القياس على الواجب للفرق بين المقيس والمقيس عليه، فإن ترك القبيح لكونه نفياً لا يحصل إلا بترك جميع القبائح بخلاف الإتيان بالواجب، فإنه لكونه إثباتاً يحصل بإثبات واجب دون واجب. ورد بأن الكلام في الواجبات التي صدرت عن الشارع الأمر بكل واحد منها على حدة كالصلاة والصوم والزكاة مثلاً لا في إفراد واجب أمر الشارع بالإتيان بواحد منها لا على التعيين كإعتاق رقبة أي رقبة كانت، وظاهر أن تمام الامتثال بالأمر لا يحصل بإتيان واحد منها دون آخر بل بإتيان الجميع، كما أن تمام الامتثال بالنهي لا يحصل بترك قبيح دون آخر من غير فرق. فلو لم تصح التوبة عن بعض القبائح دون بعض لم يصح إتيان بعض الواجبات دون بعض، لكن التالي باطل اتفاقاً فكذا المقدم.
Fourth: Among the rulings of repentance, they have regarding time-specific repentance, such as one who sins for a year, and detailed repentance, such as one who repents from adultery but not from wine drinking, on the basis that if remorse is because it is a sin, it encompasses all times and all sins, since its general nature does not cover both. So some of them held that universality is obligatory because if he regrets a sin at a time and does not regret another sin, or at another time, it appears that he did not regret it for its ugliness; otherwise he would have regretted all its ugly deeds due to their sharing the cause necessitating remorse, and he would also regret at all times. And if his remorse is not due to its ugliness, it is not repentance. Others among them held that universality is not obligatory, as in the obligations (wājibāt), for one may perform some of what is commanded without others, and at some times without others, and what is performed is valid in itself without depending on others, despite the cause necessitating the performance of the obligation being that the act is good (ḥasan) and obligatory. If it is said: the levels of goodness differ in acts, and their motivations also vary according to times. We say: the levels of ugliness (qubḥ) are likewise. And the Ash'aris agreed with these (latter ones) regarding the validity of the two repentances. His statement: (as in the obligations). It was said: the analogy to the obligation is not complete due to the difference between what is compared and what it is compared to, for abandoning the ugly, being a negation, does not occur except by abandoning all ugly deeds, unlike performing the obligation, for being an affirmation, it occurs by affirming one obligation without another. And it was refuted by saying: the discussion concerns obligations for which the Lawgiver issued a command for each one separately, like prayer, fasting, and zakāt for example, not a single obligation that the Lawgiver commanded to perform one unspecified one like freeing a slave, any slave. And it is apparent that full compliance with a command is not achieved by performing one without another but by performing all, just as full compliance with a prohibition is not achieved by abandoning one ugly deed without another without difference. So if repentance from some ugly deeds without others were not valid, then performing some obligations without others would not be valid—but the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth ruling on repentance: whether repentance must be time-specific (muwaqqata) or detailed (mufaṣṣala), i.e., covering only certain sins or times. Some argued that if remorse is due to the sin's ugliness (qubḥ), it must be universal, as all sins share the same cause. Others argued universality is not required, analogizing to obligations (wājibāt) where performing one duty suffices without doing all. The Ash'aris agreed with the latter. A gloss adds that the analogy with obligations holds only for separate commands; for a single command, fulfillment requires doing all parts.

Key Terms
حسن (ḥusn)
واجبات (wājibāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the obligation analogy is invalid because abandoning sins requires abandoning all.
Correction: The rebuttal shows that for separate commands, partial performance is valid, just as partial repentance is valid.

Checkpoint
How do Ash'aris view repentance from only some sins?
A) Invalid
B) Valid
C) Only if the sin is minor
D) Only if all sins are abandoned

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris uphold the validity of partial repentance, supported by an analogy to separate divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that repenting from one sin is valid even if you still commit others. Does this truth bring you relief, or does it expose a complacency in your heart? Be honest: are you using this as an excuse to hold onto a pet sin while claiming to repent?

Imagine yourself standing before Allah with a record of sins—some repented, some not. Based on this lesson, how does your view of His mercy change when you realize that each individual repentance is accepted? What sin have you not brought yourself to leave, and what is the real barrier?
?
The analogy shows that performing one obligation (like prayer) is accepted even if you miss another (like fasting). How does this encourage you to start with the acts of worship you can manage, rather than being overwhelmed by the rest? What one obligation will you commit to perfecting this week as a practical step?

Is Repentance an Act of Obedience? Al-Āmidī's Discussion
Al-Āmidī discusses whether repentance is an act of obedience and thus rewarded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether repentance is an act of obedience (ṭā'a) and the evidence Al-Āmidī uses to argue it is obligatory and rewarded.

Core Idea
Repentance is commanded, but is it an act of obedience or a concession?

Al-Āmidī examines a key theological question: is repentance itself an act of obedience that earns reward? He presents the apparent view that it is obligatory, supported by the Qur'anic command to repent, but also acknowledges a nuance: the command might be a concession to prevent despair rather than a strict obligation. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation first to grasp the issue and Al-Āmidī's argument.
Notice the two Quranic citations serve different purposes.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The command to repent could imply obligation or be a concession. Find where Al-Āmidī presents these.
3
Examine the evidence for obligation
Consider the imperative 'Turn to God' and its apparent meaning of obligation.
The verse is clear but not decisive.
4
Reflect on the qualification
Understand why the command might not be decisive: it could be to remove despair, not to impose a duty.
This nuance affects the ruling on reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : اختلف في كون التوبة طاعة قال الآمدي : الظاهر أن التوبة طاعة واجبة فيثاب عليها لأنها مأمور بها قال الله تعالى : وتوبوا إلى الله جميعاً أيها المؤمنون [النور: 31]، والأمر ظاهر في الوجوب لكنه غير قاطع لجواز أن يكون رخصة وإيذاناً بقبولها ودفعاً للقنوط لقوله تعالى : ولا تقنطوا من رحمة الله [الزمر: 53] ولا تيأسوا من روح الله إن الله يغفر الذنوب جميعاً [يوسف: 87].
Sixth: There is disagreement on whether repentance is an act of obedience. Al-Āmidī said: The apparent view is that repentance is an obligatory act of obedience, so one is rewarded for it because it is commanded. God said: 'And turn to God all together, O believers' [Quran 24:31]. The imperative is apparent in obligation, but it is not decisive, as it could be a concession and an indication of acceptance and removal of despair, as in His statement: 'And do not despair of the mercy of God' [Quran 39:53] and 'Do not lose hope of God's relief; indeed, God forgives all sins' [Quran 12:87].
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage examines whether repentance is an act of obedience (ṭā'a). Al-Āmidī considers the apparent view that it is obligatory, hence rewarded. He cites the command 'Turn to God' which implies obligation, but notes it could be a concession to remove despair. Thus the command is not absolutely decisive for obligation.

Key Terms
طاعة (ṭā'a)
ثواب (thawāb)
أمر (amr)
وجوب (wujūb)
رخصة (rukhṣa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance is purely optional and not an act of obedience.
Correction: Al-Āmidī argues the apparent view is that repentance is an obligatory act of obedience, so it is rewarded.

Checkpoint

B) Because it could be a concession to prevent despair.
C) Because the verse is abrogated.
D) Because repentance is not a legal act.

Key Takeaway
Repentance is apparently an obligatory act of obedience that earns reward, though the command could also be interpreted as a concession to prevent despair.

Assent Can Increase in Strength and Weakness
Proves that assent increases in strength and weakness, addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first aspect of increase—strength and weakness—proves that assent can vary, and how the author refutes objections that variation requires doubt or pertains only to quantity.

Core Idea
Assent varies in strength and weakness without implying doubt, as shown by the Quran.

This lesson tackles the first argument for the increase of faith: that assent (taṣdīq) can be stronger or weaker as a psychological quality. The author explains that variation does not require the possibility of the opposite, and he cites the example of Prophet Abraham to show that even certain assent can intensify. Then he addresses an objection that the dispute is about quantitative increase, not qualitative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author begins by stating that assent is among psychological qualities that vary in strength and weakness. This is the first aspect of increase.
The author is defending that faith can increase in quality, not just quantity.
2
Understand the objection and its refutation
The objector says variation can only come from possibility of the opposite. The author rejects that and shows the consequence would make the Prophet's faith equal to common believers, which is false. Then he uses Abraham's verse as evidence.
The key point: variation can occur by strength/weakness without any possibility of the opposite.
3
Reflect on the marginal comment about quantity vs quality
The passage ends with a comment that the dispute is about quantitative increase (kammiyya), not qualitative (kayfiyya). Consider how this objection has been addressed elsewhere or in the broader debate.
This comment is included though not answered here; it highlights a common confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: القوة والضعف فإن التصديق من الكيفيات النفسانية المتفاوتة قوة وضعفاً، قولكم: الواجب اليقين والتفاوت لا يكون إلا لاحتمال النقيض قلنا: لا نسلم أن التفاوت لذلك الاحتمال فقط إذ يجوز أن يكون بالقوة والضعف بلا احتمال للنقيض ثم ذلك الذي ذكرتموه يقتضي أن يكون إيمان النبي وآحاد الأمة سواء وإنه باطل إجماعاً، ولقول إبراهيم عليه السلام ولكن ليطمئن قلبي فإنه يدل على قبول التصديق اليقيني للزيادة كما سلف تقريره والظاهر أن الظن الغالب الذي لا يخطر معه احتمال النقيض بالبال حكمه حكم اليقين في كونه إيماناً حقيقياً فإن إيمان أكثر هذا القبيل، وعلى هذا فكون التصديق الإيماني قابلاً للزيادة واضح وضوحاً تاماً. قوله: (الأول: القوة والضعف قيل: هذا مسلم لكن لا طائل تحته إذ النزاع إنما هو في تفاوت الإيمان بحسب الكمية، أعني القلة والكثرة فإن الزيادة أكثر ما تستعمل في الأعداد، وأما التفاوت في الكيفية، أعني القوة والضعف فخارج عن محل النزاع.
The first: strength and weakness. For assent (taṣdīq) is among the psychological qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya) that vary in strength and weakness. Your statement: ‘The obligatory is certainty, and variation is only due to the possibility of the opposite.’ We say: we do not grant that variation is only for that possibility, for it is permissible that it be by strength and weakness without possibility of the opposite. Then that which you mentioned would require that the faith of the Prophet and the common people be equal, and that is false by consensus. And because of the saying of Abraham (peace be upon him): ‘but that my heart may be reassured,’ for it indicates that certain assent accepts increase, as previously established. And it is apparent that the dominant opinion (al-ẓann al-ghālib) with which no possibility of the opposite occurs to the mind—its ruling is the ruling of certainty in being true faith, for the faith of most people is of this kind. On this basis, that faith-assent is capable of increase is fully clear. His statement: (The first: strength and weakness. It was said: this is conceded but has no benefit, for the dispute is about variation of faith in terms of quantity, i.e., fewness and manyness, since increase is mostly used in numbers, while variation in quality, i.e., strength and weakness, is outside the point of dispute.)
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the first aspect by which assent (taṣdīq) can increase: strength and weakness (al-quwwa wa al-ḍuʿf). It begins by stating that assent is among the psychological qualities that vary in intensity. The objector claims that the obligatory degree is certainty (yaqīn) and variation can only arise from the possibility (iḥtimāl) of the opposite. The author rejects that variation is exclusively due to possibility, asserting that variation can occur through strength and weakness without any possibility of the opposite. Then the author refutes a consequence: if the objection were true, it would imply that the faith of the Prophet and common believers are equal, which is false by consensus. The verse of Abraham (al-Baqara 2:260) is cited as evidence that even certain assent can increase. The passage also argues that the prevalent opinion (al-ẓann al-ghālib) that never even entertains the possibility of the opposite is equivalent to certainty in constituting true faith, and such is the faith of most believers. Thus the capacity for increase is clear. A marginal comment (qawluhu) raises a further point: the objection that the dispute is about quantitative increase (kammiyya) not qualitative (kayfiyya). This comment is included as part of the lesson, though it is not answered here—it serves to highlight the distinction.

Key Terms
القوة والضعف
الكيفيات النفسانية
اليقين
احتمال النقيض
الظن الغالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Variation in assent must imply doubt or possibility of the opposite.
Correction: Variation can occur through strength and weakness without any possibility of the opposite, as shown by psychological qualities.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author use to prove that even certain assent can increase?
A) The hadith about branches of faith
B) The verse of Abraham 'that my heart may be reassured'
C) Consensus of the Salaf
D) The definition of faith as works

Key Takeaway
The first aspect of increase in faith is through strength and weakness, which does not require doubt and is supported by scripture and common experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Way to You alone do we turn' in prayer, do you feel the strength of that assent in your heart, or is it mere repetition? What would change if you truly experienced the increase in strength the passage describes?

?
The passage says most believers have faith that never even entertains the opposite. When calamity strikes or doubts whisper, does your heart remain steady, or does the possibility of the opposite creep in? What concrete action can you take to strengthen your certainty so that no opposite even occurs to your mind?

Refuting the Second Scriptural Argument on Major Sins
Refutes a claim that a verse proves major sinners are disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent meaning of a verse can be abandoned and why a scriptural argument that major sinners are disbelievers is refuted.

Core Idea
A verse's apparent meaning is overridden by broader context and other verses.

The author now tackles a second scriptural proof used to claim that major sinners from the people of prayer become disbelievers. The refutation hinges on classic principles of Qur'anic interpretation: the apparent meaning (zāhir) may be set aside when contextual or scriptural evidence demands it. In this passage, you will see how the argument unfolds by examining the scope of the word "recompense" and by invoking another verse that generalizes it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full literal translation. Note how the author structures the refutation: first stating the opposing claim, then giving two reasons to abandon the apparent meaning, and finally offering a contextual interpretation.
Pay attention to the key terms: مرتكب الكبيرة, الجزاء, الكفور.
2
Identify the two rebuttals
The refutation uses two main points: (1) recompense includes both reward and punishment, so it is not restricted to disbelievers; (2) another verse (Ghafir 40:17) shows recompense is for every soul. Find these in the text.
The second rebuttal reinforces the first by citing a clear Qur'anic statement.
3
Understand the contextual resolution
The author then explains that the verse must refer to a specific kind of recompense unique to disbelievers, as indicated by its context (Saba 34:17). The major sinner may receive a different recompense, so the verse does not prove disbelief.
This is a key move: interpreting a general statement as referring to a specific instance.
4
Reflect on the principle of 'munkar' al-zahir
The phrase 'munkar al-zahir' (abandoned in its apparent meaning) is a standard interpretive tool. Note that the author does not reject the verse but reinterprets it harmoniously with other texts.
This lesson illustrates how apparent meaning is not absolute in Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع: في أن مرتكبي الكبيرة من أهل الصلاة [ النساء : ٩٣ ] ، فيكون كافراً ، قلنا : ( هو ) متروك الظاهر) لأن ظاهره حصر الجزاء في الكفور وهو متروك قطعاً ( إذ يجازى غير الكفور وهو المثاب) لأن الجزاء يعم الثواب والعقاب، (و) أيضاً ذلك الحصر متروك لقوله تعالى : ﴿ اليوم تجزى كل نفس بما كسبت ﴾ [غافر : ۱۷] ، فوجب حمل الآية على جزاء مخصوص بالكافر كما يدل عليه سياق الآية أعني قولك ذلك : ﴿ جزيناهم بما كفروا ﴾ [سبأ: ١٧]، فالمعنى وهل يجازى ذلك الجزاء إلا الكفور وصاحب الكبيرة جاز أن يجازى جزاء مغايراً لما يختص بالكافر .
The third observatory - the fourth objective: concerning that the committers of major sins from the people of prayer [al-Nisa 4:93] — so he becomes a disbeliever. We said: (It is) abandoned in its apparent meaning, because its apparent meaning restricts recompense to the disbelievers, and it is abandoned definitively, since there is recompense for other than the disbeliever, namely the rewarded one, because recompense includes reward and punishment. (And) also that restriction is abandoned by His statement: 'Today every soul will be recompensed for what it earned' [Ghafir 40:17]. So the verse must be interpreted as referring to a specific recompense for the disbeliever, as indicated by the context of the verse, namely His statement: 'We recompensed them for their disbelief' [Saba 34:17]. So the meaning is: 'And is that recompense recompensed except to the disbelievers?' And the committer of a major sin may be recompensed with a recompense different from that specific to the disbeliever.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a scriptural argument claiming that a certain verse (probably involving recompense) proves that a major sinner is a disbeliever. The refutation begins by stating that the apparent meaning of the verse is abandoned. It explains that the verse appears to restrict recompense to disbelievers, but that restriction is not absolute because recompense is given to others as well, such as the rewarded believers. Additionally, the restriction is contradicted by another verse (Ghafir 40:17) which states that every soul is recompensed for its deeds. Therefore, the verse in question must be understood as referring to a specific kind of recompense that is particular to disbelievers, as supported by the context of another verse (Saba 34:17). Thus, the meaning is: 'Is that specific recompense given except to disbelievers?' and the major sinner may receive a different recompense that is not exclusive to disbelievers.

Key Terms
مرتكب الكبيرة
الجزاء
الكفور
ظاهر الآية
متروك الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse in question proves that any major sinner from the people of prayer is a disbeliever.
Correction: The apparent meaning is abandoned because recompense is not exclusive to disbelievers; the verse must refer to a specific recompense for disbelievers, and major sinners may receive a different recompense.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is the apparent meaning of the verse abandoned?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because recompense is not restricted to disbelievers; it includes believers as well.
C) Because the verse is metaphorical.
D) Because the verse only applies to hypocrites.

Key Takeaway
A verse that appears to restrict recompense to disbelievers is reinterpreted through context and other verses, showing that major sinners are not thereby proven to be disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken a single verse out of context to judge another Muslim harshly? How can you apply the lesson's principle of considering broader context and other verses before concluding someone's faith?
?

?
If a fellow believer falls into a major sin, how do you react? Does this lesson challenge you to see them as still capable of being a believer, and if so, what concrete action will you take to support them rather than condemn them?

The Hajj Verse and Its Correct Interpretation
Presents the third argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a key Quranic verse about Hajj is correctly interpreted in the context of faith and disbelief, and why the opponent's objection fails.

Core Idea
The Hajj verse condemns denial of obligation, not mere omission.

In theological debates, opponents sometimes use Quranic verses to argue that major sins cause disbelief. One such argument cites the Hajj verse that says 'whoever disbelieves' after making Hajj obligatory. In this lesson, we will examine this argument and see how scholars of Ahl al-Sunna refute it by clarifying the intended meaning of 'disbelief' in the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: the opponent's argument and the refutation are in two short sentences.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the part where the opponent presents the verse and interprets 'whoever disbelieves' as referring to anyone who does not perform Hajj. Ask yourself: why would this be a problem for Ahl al-Sunna?
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation reinterprets the verse: the 'disbelief' here means denying the obligation (juhud wujub), not simply omitting Hajj. Notice how this distinction protects the position that major sins do not remove faith.
This connects to the broader topic of defining disbelief in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قوله تعالى بعد إيجاب الحج ﴿ ومن كفر ﴾ [آل عمران : ٩٧] أي لم يحج ( فإن الله غني عن العالمين) فقد جعل ترك الحج كفراً، (قلنا : المراد من جحد وجوبه ولا شك في كفره .
Third: His statement, after making Hajj obligatory: 'And whoever disbelieves' [Al-i-Imran 3:97], i.e., does not perform Hajj, 'then indeed Allah is free from need of the worlds.' So He made abandoning Hajj disbelief. We said: The intended meaning is [one who] denies its obligation, and there is no doubt about his disbelief.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument from the opponent and its refutation. The opponent cites the verse from Al-i-Imran 3:97, which says 'And whoever disbelieves' after the obligation of Hajj, interpreting it as referring to one who does not perform Hajj. The opponent argues that this verse explicitly labels a person who abandons Hajj as a disbeliever. The refutation responds by clarifying that the verse actually refers to someone who denies the obligation of Hajj (juhud al-wujub), not merely someone who omits it due to negligence. There is no doubt that such a denier is a disbeliever, but the major sinner who omits Hajj without denying its obligation is not included.

Correction: The verse specifically refers to those who deny the obligation of Hajj, not to those who omit it out of laziness or sin.

B) It refers to one who denies the obligation of Hajj.
C) It refers to one who performs Hajj hypocritically.
D) It refers to one who performs Hajj without sincerity.

Key Takeaway
The Hajj verse does not call negligent Muslims disbelievers, but only those who deny the obligation.

The Fourth Argument: Punishment Limited to Deniers
Refutes the claim that only deniers are punished, using the example of wine-drinkers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of the claim that punishment is only for deniers is based on consensus that wine-drinkers and adulterers are punished despite not being deniers.

Core Idea
Punishment is not limited to deniers; major sinners are punished too.

This lesson examines a scriptural argument that seems to restrict punishment to deniers. The refutation shows that the apparent meaning of the verse must be set aside because other texts and consensus affirm punishment for non-deniers like wine-drinkers and adulterers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the passage and find the verse that the opponent uses to argue that punishment is only for deniers.
The verse is from Taha 20:48.
2
Understand the refutation's first move
Notice that the refutation says the apparent meaning is abandoned and specified.
This is a common technique in usul al-fiqh.
3
Examine the consensus example
The refutation points to the consensus that wine-drinkers and adulterers are punished, even though they are not deniers.
This shows the verse cannot be taken literally.
4
Distinguish between denier and led to denial
The refutation clarifies that Jews and Christians are not deniers, though they may be led to denial.
This is a subtle but important point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : ( قوله تعالى حكاية عن موسى وهارون ﴿ إنا قد أوحي إلينا ﴾ أن العذاب على من كذب وتولى ﴾ [طه: ٤٨] فإنه يدل على انحصار العذاب في المكذب وهو كافر، ولا شك أن الفاسق معذب لما ورد فيه من الوعيد (قلنا :) هو أيضاً ( متروك الظاهر) ومخصوص (للاتفاق على عذاب شارب الخمر والزاني مع أنه غير مكذب لله تعالى بل اليهود والنصارى) لا يكذبون الله تعالى ( وربما يلزمهم التكذيب لكن فرق بين المكذب ومن يلزمه التكذيب .
Fourth: His statement narrated from Moses and Aaron: 'Indeed, it has been revealed to us that the punishment is upon whoever denies and turns away' [Taha 20:48]. It indicates that punishment is restricted to the denier, and he is a disbeliever. And there is no doubt that the sinner is punished due to the threats that have come regarding him. We said: It is also abandoned in its apparent meaning and specified, because of the consensus that the wine-drinker and the adulterer are punished, even though they are not deniers of Allah; rather, the Jews and Christians do not deny Allah, and perhaps they are necessarily led to denial, but there is a difference between a denier and one who is necessarily led to denial.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument and its refutation. The opponent cites the verse from Taha 20:48, which states that punishment is upon those who deny and turn away. The opponent argues that this restricts punishment to deniers, and since major sinners are punished (based on other threats), they must be deniers. The refutation responds that the apparent meaning of the verse is abandoned and specified. It points out that there is consensus that wine-drinkers and adulterers are punished, yet they are not deniers of Allah. The example of Jews and Christians is given: they do not explicitly deny Allah, but some interpretations may lead to denial. However, the key distinction is between one who actually denies and one who is necessarily led to denial. So the verse does not prove that all punished sinners are deniers.

C) The verse about the fire that only the wretched enter.

Key Takeaway
The apparent meaning of a verse can be set aside due to consensus and other texts, as seen with the punishment of wine-drinkers.

Fifth Argument: The Blazing Fire and the Denier
Refutes the claim that every entrant into fire is a denier, by distinguishing a specific fire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the linguistic nuance of indefiniteness (tanakir) in the Quranic verse is used to refute the claim that all who enter Hell are disbelievers.

Core Idea
One indefinite word can change the whole meaning of a verse.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth argument put forward by those who claim that every person who enters the Fire is a denier (kāfir). They cite a powerful verse from Surah al-Layl. The refutation, however, draws on a subtle point of Arabic grammar: the indefiniteness of the word 'fire'. This linguistic insight shows that the verse may refer to a specific type of fire, not Hell in general. Follow along to see how this simple grammatical observation dismantles the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the opponent's claim
Read the Quranic verse (92:14-16) and understand the opponent's deduction that everyone who enters the fire is a denier.
2
Recognize the refutation's linguistic move
Notice how the refutation points out that the word 'fire' (nar) is indefinite, which can indicate a specific kind.
3
Understand the scope
Realize that the refutation does not deny that major sinners enter Hell, but argues that this particular verse is about a special fire only for deniers.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how careful attention to Quranic language (like tanakir) is essential in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : قوله تعالى : ﴿ فأنذرتكم ناراً تلظى لا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى ﴾ [الليل : ١٤-١٦] فإنه يدل على أن كل من يصلى النار فهو كافر، ) والفاسق ) أي مرتكب الكبيرة ) يصلاها ) أي النار للآيات العامة الموعدة بدخولها، ( قلنا : لعل ذلك نار خاصة ) يعني أن الضمير في يصلاها عائد إلى نار منكرة فلعل تنكيرها للوحدة النوعية فتكون ناراً مخصوصة لا يصلاها إلا الكافر .
Fifth: His statement: 'So I have warned you of a blazing fire. None shall enter it except the most wretched, who denies and turns away' [al-Layl 92:14-16]. It indicates that everyone who enters the fire is a disbeliever. And the sinner, i.e., the committer of a major sin, enters it, i.e., the fire, due to the general verses threatening entry into it. We said: Perhaps that is a specific fire. Meaning that the pronoun in 'enters it' refers back to an indefinite fire, so perhaps its indefiniteness indicates a specific type, so it is a particular fire that only the disbeliever enters.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument and its refutation. The opponent cites the verse from al-Layl 92:14-16, which states that only the most wretched (the denier and turner-away) enters the blazing fire. The opponent argues that since major sinners enter the fire (based on other verses threatening punishment), they must be deniers. The refutation offers a linguistic response: the word 'fire' (nar) in the verse is indefinite, which can indicate a specific type or kind. The pronoun in 'enters it' (yaslaha) refers back to this indefinite fire. Thus, the verse may be referring to a particular fire that is exclusive to disbelievers, not the general fire of Hell. Therefore, major sinners who enter Hell do not necessarily belong to this specific fire.

Key Terms
النار
التنكير
الوحدة النوعية
خاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that all who enter Hell are deniers.
Correction: The verse may refer to a specific fire, so it does not apply to all sinners in Hell.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation argue that the verse does not prove all entrants to fire are deniers?
A) Because the fire is metaphorical.
B) Because the indefinite noun can indicate a specific fire not for all sinners.
C) Because the verse is abrogated.
D) Because the denier is not actually punished.

Key Takeaway
The indefinite nature of the word 'fire' in the Quranic verse allows for a specific interpretation that undermines the claim that all entrants to Hell are deniers.

Sixth Argument: Light Scales Do Not Prove Disbelief
Refutes the claim that having light scales proves denial by asserting that faith makes scales heavy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation uses the weight of faith to counter the claim that a major sinner's light scales imply disbelief.

Core Idea
Faith outweighs sins in the scales, so the believer's scales are never light.

This passage presents a key objection against the view that major sinners are not eternally punished: if a sinner's scales are light due to few good deeds, does that make him a denier? The refutation clarifies that faith itself makes the scales heavy, removing the sinner from the verse's scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then follow with the English translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's argument is presented first, followed by the refutation.
The verse quoted is from Surah al-Mu'minun.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent uses the verse to argue that those with light scales are deniers, and since the major sinner has few good deeds, his scales are light, making him a denier (kafir). Trace the logical steps: light scales → denier (from verse), sinner → light scales, therefore sinner → denier.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation asserts that the scales of the sinner are actually heavy due to faith. This blocks the opponent's syllogism because the sinner does not fall under 'light scales'. Understand why faith is considered weighty in the balance.
Faith is a decisive factor that outweighs sins.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader theological point: that faith fundamentally changes the spiritual balance. This refutation protects the view that major sinners remain believers and are not eternally doomed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : قوله تعالى : في حق من خفت موازينه ﴿ ألم تكن آياتي تتلى عليكم فكنتم بها تكذبون ﴾ [المؤمنون : ١٠٥] ، و ( حينئذ نقول : (الفاسق ممن خفت موازينه ) لقلة حسناته، وكل من خفت موازينه فهو مكذب بالآية المذكورة وكل مكذب كافر، قلنا : بل ثقلت) موازين الفاسق (بالإيمان فلا يندرج فيمن خفت موازينه .
Sixth: His statement concerning those whose scales are light: 'Were not My verses recited to you, and you used to deny them?' [al-Mu'minun 23:105]. And then we say: The sinner is among those whose scales are light, due to his few good deeds. And everyone whose scales are light is a denier of the mentioned verse, and every denier is a disbeliever. We said: Rather, the scales of the sinner are heavy due to faith, so he is not included among those whose scales are light.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument and its refutation. The opponent cites the verse from al-Mu'minun 23:105, which states that those whose scales are light are asked whether they denied the verses. The opponent argues that the major sinner has light scales due to few good deeds, and the verse says such people are deniers. Therefore, the sinner is a denier and thus a disbeliever. The refutation counters that the scales of the sinner are actually heavy because of his faith (iman). Faith outweighs sins in the balance, so the sinner is not among those whose scales are light. Consequently, the verse does not apply to him.

Key Terms
الموازين
خفيف
ثقيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies that the sinner has sins or that his scales are light.
Correction: The refutation does not deny that the sinner has sins; it argues that faith outweighs them, making the scales heavy overall.

Checkpoint

A) Because he has no sins.
B) Because his faith makes his scales heavy.
C) Because the verse only applies to polytheists.
D) Because he will not be judged.

Key Takeaway
The major sinner's faith outweighs his sins, so his scales are not light and the verse does not apply to him.

Supplementary Analysis of Refutations for Arguments 3, 5, 6
Provides further justification and linguistic alternatives for refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional linguistic and interpretive alternatives used to refute arguments about the relationship between faith and deeds.

Core Idea
Metaphor and linguistic nuance can overturn seemingly clear proofs about faith and deeds.

In this lesson, we examine the commentator's additional remarks on the refutations of arguments three, five, and six. These remarks provide alternative linguistic explanations—such as metaphor and restrictive attributes—that show how apparent scriptural evidence can be reinterpreted. They also highlight a problem with the interpretation of the 'heavy scales' verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the three distinct points being discussed: the metaphor for Hajj abandonment, the restrictive attribute interpretation, and the problem with the heavy scales verse.
The passage is a commentary on earlier refutations, so keep in mind it supplements previous arguments.
2
Analyze each alternative explanation
For each point, note the alternative linguistic or interpretive move (metaphor, restrictive attribute, and the consequence of the heavy scales interpretation). Understand how each undermines the original proof.
3
Reflect on the interpretive principle
Consider the broader lesson: scriptural verses can be understood in multiple ways, and fine-grained analysis of language is essential in theological reasoning.
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قلنا : المراد من جحد وجوبه) وأيضاً يجوز أن يكون التعبير عن ترك الحج بالكفر بطريق الاستعارة استعظاماً له أو تغليظاً في الوعيد عليه . قوله : (قلنا : لعل ذلك إلخ) وأيضاً يحتمل أن يكون قوله : لا يصلاها صفة مخصصة لا كاشفة فحينئذ لا الاستدلال أيضاً. يتم قوله : (ثقلت بالإيمان) فيه بحث لأنه يؤدي إلى نفي جزاء المعصية مع الإيمان لأن من ثقلت موازينه فهو في عيشة راضية على ما نطق به القرآن وحمله على أن عاقبته ذلك لم يقل به
His statement: 'We said: The intended meaning is one who denies its obligation.' And also it is possible that the expression of abandoning Hajj as disbelief is metaphorical, as an exaggeration or a severe warning. His statement: 'We said: Perhaps that etc.' And also it is possible that His statement 'none shall enter it' is a restrictive attribute, not explanatory; in which case the inference also fails. It is completed. His statement: '(The scales are) heavy due to faith' — there is discussion about it because it leads to negation of punishment for sin along with faith, since the one whose scales are heavy is in a pleasant life, according to the Qur'anic text, and interpreting it as referring to his final outcome has not been said by anyone.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage provides additional commentary on the refutations given earlier for the third, fifth, and sixth arguments. For the third argument, it suggests that calling a person who abandons Hajj a 'disbeliever' could be metaphorical, intended to emphasize the severity of the sin. For the fifth argument, it offers an alternative linguistic analysis: the phrase 'none shall enter it' could be a restrictive attribute (sifa mukhasisa) rather than an explanatory one, meaning it narrows the reference rather than merely describing; thus the verse does not imply that all entrants to fire are deniers. For the sixth argument, it notes a problem: if the sinner's scales are heavy due to faith, it would imply that he receives no punishment for his sins, because the Qur'an says that those with heavy scales are in a pleasant life. The commentator observes that no one has interpreted the verse as referring only to the final outcome.

Correction: These are supplementary critical evaluations of the refutations, not the refutations themselves.

Blackened Faces and Disbelief: Refuting a Misapplication of Qur'an 3:106
Refutes the claim that blackened face at resurrection proves disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse about blackened faces (Qur'an 3:106) is correctly interpreted to apply only to those who disbelieved after having faith, not to all sinners.

Core Idea
Not every blackened face means disbelief; the verse is limited to apostates.

The opponent tries to use a vivid Qur'anic image—faces blackened on the Day of Resurrection—to argue that major sinners are disbelievers. But the refutation shows that the verse itself specifies a particular group: those who disbelieved after believing. In this lesson, we examine the argument and its precise rebuttal, learning how careful attention to Qur'anic context protects sound doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Start by reading the opening claim: the opponent cites Qur'an 3:106 and argues that any sinner whose face is blackened by sin is therefore a disbeliever.
Note the opponent's logical leap from 'blackened face' to 'disbeliever'.
2
Identify the refutation's strategy
The refutation does not deny that sinners may have blackened faces; it denies that the verse applies to all such people. Look for the key phrase 'لا نسلم' (we do not grant).
The refutation restricts the verse's application by pointing to the phrase 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?'
3
Examine the Qur'anic context
Notice that the verse itself specifies 'after your faith'. This shows the verse addresses those who turned away from belief, not believers who commit sins.
This is the core exegetical point: the verse is about apostasy, not major sins.
4
Summarize the lesson learned
Reflect on how this argument illustrates the importance of not taking Qur'anic verses out of context when making theological claims.
The same principle applies to many other disputed verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع) قوله تعالى : ﴿ يوم تبيض وجوه وتسود وجوه فأما الذين اسودت وجوههم أكفرتم بعد إيمانكم ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٠٦] والفاسق ممن وجهه مسود بالمعصية فيكون كافراً ( قلنا : لا نسلم أن كل فاسق كذلك ) أي مسود الوجه يوم القيامة فإن الآية لا تقتضي ذلك، بل هي واردة في بعض الكفار الذين كفروا بعد إيمانهم ( لقوله : ﴿ أكفرتم بعد إيمانكم ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٠٦ ]
Seventh: His statement: 'On the Day when faces become white and faces become black. As for those whose faces become black: Did you disbelieve after your faith?' [Al-i-Imran 3:106]. And the sinner is among those whose face is blackened by sin, so he is a disbeliever. We said: We do not grant that every sinner is like that, i.e., black-faced on the Day of Resurrection, for the verse does not necessitate that. Rather, it is revealed concerning some disbelievers who disbelieved after their faith, because of His statement: 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?' [Al-i-Imran 3:106].
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument and its refutation. The opponent cites the verse from Al-i-Imran 3:106, which speaks of faces being blackened and then asks if they disbelieved after faith. The opponent argues that the major sinner has a face blackened by sin, so he is included in the verse and thus is a disbeliever. The refutation denies that every sinner is black-faced on that Day. It states that the verse specifically refers to those who disbelieved after being believers, as indicated by the phrase 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?' Therefore, it does not apply to the major sinner who remains a believer.

Key Terms
الوجوه
المسودة
الكفر بعد الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic verse about blackened faces (3:106) proves that all major sinners (those with blackened faces due to sin) are disbelievers.
Correction: The verse specifically addresses those who disbelieved after having faith, restricting its application to apostates, not to all sinners.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation restrict the application of the verse about blackened faces?
A) It applies only to hypocrites.
B) It applies only to those who disbelieved after having faith.
C) It applies to all sinners.
D) It applies only to polytheists.

Key Takeaway
The verse about blackened faces (3:106) specifically addresses apostates, not all sinners, so it cannot be used to label major sinners as disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, what is your immediate inner judgment? Based on this lesson, does that judgment align with the Qur'an's own limited scope of condemnation? What would it take for you to replace that judgment with compassion and hope for their repentance?
?
Have you ever used a general verse of the Qur'an to condemn someone without considering its specific context? How can you train yourself to pause and ask, 'Is this verse really about this person's situation?' before forming a verdict?
?
This verse specifically rebukes apostates with 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?'—how would you feel if Allah addressed that question to you personally? What would be your answer? Are there areas in your life where your actions don't match your claim of faith?

Eighth Argument: Companions of the Left Hand Refuted
Refutes the claim that major sinners are left-hand companions implies disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'anic mention of 'companions of the left hand' does not entail that major sinners are disbelievers, and how logical fallacy (reversing implication) is exposed.

Core Idea
The verse proves every disbeliever is a left-hand companion, not that every left-hand companion is a disbeliever.

This lesson tackles the eighth argument raised by those who claim that major sinners are among the 'companions of the left hand' mentioned in the Qur'an, and thus are disbelievers. We will see how the verse actually speaks about disbelievers, and how the opponent's logic commits the fallacy of reversing implication. The refutation also provides counterexamples that undermine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully to grasp the flow of the argument and refutation.
Notice the use of parentheses and brackets in the Arabic.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent cites a verse and argues that major sinners are among the companions of the left hand, so they are disbelievers. Locate this claim in the passage.
The opponent uses the verse from al-Balad 90:19.
3
Understand the first point of refutation
The refutation states that the verse actually shows the reverse implication: every disbeliever is a left-hand companion, but not vice versa. This is the fallacy of إيهام العكس (reversing implication).
This is a logical fallacy where one assumes the converse of a true statement is also true.
4
Understand the second point of refutation
The refutation provides counterexamples: the adulterer and thief, who affirm religion, are considered left-hand companions but are not deniers. This contradicts the opponent's inference.
These examples show that being a left-hand companion does not necessarily mean disbelief.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Think about how careful reasoning is required in theological arguments. The lesson shows the importance of distinguishing between necessary and sufficient conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن أنه أي مرتكب الكبيرة ) من أصحاب المشأمة وقال تعالى : ﴿ والذين كفروا بآياتنا هم أصحاب المشأمة ﴾ [ البلد : ١٩]، فدل على أنه ﴿ الآية كان من أصحاب المشأمة فهو كافر، قلنا: (هو ) أي ما ذكرتم من معنى من باب إيهام العكس فإنها تدل على أن كل من كفر كان أصحاب المشأمة، من وذلك لا ينعكس كلياً كما توهمتموه (و) أيضاً ( ينتقض) استدلالكم بهذه الآية ( بالزاني والسارق ( المصدقين بما هو من ضرورات الدين فإنهما من أصحاب المشأمة ) مع عدم تكذيبهما .
Eighth: That he, i.e., the committer of a major sin, is from the companions of the left hand. And He said: 'And those who disbelieve in Our verses are the companions of the left hand' [al-Balad 90:19]. So it indicates that if one is from the companions of the left hand, he is a disbeliever. We said: That which you mentioned is a case of reversing implication, for it indicates that every disbeliever is a companion of the left hand, and that is not universally reversible as you imagined. And also your inference by this verse is contradicted by the adulterer and the thief, who affirm the essentials of religion, yet they are from the companions of the left hand, despite their not being deniers.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument and its refutation. The opponent cites the verse from al-Balad 90:19, which states that those who disbelieve in God's verses are the companions of the left hand. The opponent argues that since major sinners are included among the companions of the left hand (based on other verses or traditions), they must be disbelievers. The refutation makes two points. First, the verse establishes that every disbeliever is a companion of the left hand, but it does not follow that every companion of the left hand is a disbeliever (this is the fallacy of affirming the consequent). Second, the inference is contradicted by the examples of the adulterer and the thief, who are considered companions of the left hand (according to some interpretations) yet they are not deniers of God's verses; they affirm the essentials of religion.

Key Terms
أصحاب المشأمة
إيهام العكس
الزاني
السارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being a 'companion of the left hand' means one is a disbeliever.
Correction: The verse states that disbelievers are companions of the left hand, but the reverse is not necessarily true; major sinners may also be described as such without being disbelievers.

Checkpoint
What fallacious reasoning does the refutation identify in the eighth argument?
A) Begging the question.
B) Reversing the implication (إيهام العكس).
C) False analogy.
D) Circular reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The verse about the companions of the left hand does not imply that all such companions are disbelievers, as shown by the examples of the adulterer and thief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a Muslim you know who struggles with a major sin. In your heart, have you ever doubted their faith because of that sin? After this lesson, how will you correct that assumption starting today?
?
When you read Quranic verses that describe disbelievers with negative traits, do you ever find yourself thinking 'that sounds like some Muslims I know'? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of such thoughts, and what will you do the next time they arise?
?
The lesson shows how easily we can reverse logical implications in our judgments. Can you recall a time you judged someone's inner faith based on an external action? What specific words from the source_translation will you keep in mind to avoid that error in the future?

Refuting the Ninth Argument: Verse al-Nur
Ninth argument from verse 24:55 and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ninth argument from Surah al-Nur is refuted by denying the claimed logical restriction, and how the verse can be interpreted to not imply that every fasiq is a kafir.

Core Idea
A verse does not automatically invert its meaning; restriction must be grammatically sound.

This lesson addresses a key proof used to equate sin with disbelief based on the verse 'And whoever disbelieves after that, then those are the transgressors.' The opponent argues that this verse implies every transgressor is a disbeliever. We will see how the response undercuts this inference by denying that the verse establishes the claimed restriction, and offers a more nuanced interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent presents the argument, then the refutation follows.
The passage is dense; don't worry if you don't grasp everything immediately.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
Find where the opponent claims that the verse implies 'every fasiq is a kafir.' What grammatical basis do they use? Look for the word 'حصر' (restriction).
3
Understand the refutation
The response denies that the verse performs restriction. Why? Because the disbeliever is originally such, and the verse simply describes them. The commentator adds that even if the verse included transgressors, it would be metaphorical.
The refutation uses examples from other verses to show precedent.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader principle: a grammatical construction of restriction does not automatically imply the converse relationship. This is a key logical tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : ( قوله تعالى : ﴿ ومن كفر بعد ذلك فأولئك هم الفاسقون ﴾ [النور: ٥٥]، وإنه يقتضي حصر المبتدأ في الخبر)، والصحيح المطابق للنهاية حصر الخبر في المبتدأ فيصدق حينئذ أن كل فاسق كافر، قلنا: الحصر) الذي ذكرتموه ( ممنوع) كونه مستفاداً من الآية ( لأن الكافر ابتداء كذلك ) . أحد من المفسرين فالأقرب أن يقال : إن لتنزيل المخالف منزلة المكذب إذا جعلت الآية شاملة للفساق مساغاً على قاعدة المعاني كما في قوله تعالى ﴿ إنهم لا أيمان لهم ﴾ [التوبة : ١٢]، : وقوله تعالى : ﴿ ولو كانوا يعلمون ﴾ [البقرة : ١٠٢] للقطع بأن بعض الفاسقين ليس بمكذب . قوله : (الفاسق ممن وجهه مسود بالمعصية إن قلت : قد ورد في الصحيح من أن الأمة تبعث غراً من آثار الوضوء فكيف يكون الفاسق مسود الوجه في ذلك اليوم، قلت : لعلهم يوردون الكلام في فاسق يكون فسقه بترك الوضوء. الأحاديث قوله : ( الثامن : أنه من أصحاب المشأمة قيل : هم الذين يعطون كتابهم بشمالهم فيمكن منع كون مرتكب الكبيرة منهم كما سيجيء مثله وقيل : هم الذين هم شوم وشر على أنفسهم، فكذلك يمكن المنع لأن الشؤم والشر بالكفر ، وقيل : الذين يسلك بهم إلى النار شمالاً، والظاهر إمكان المنع حينئذ أيضاً. قوله : ( من باب إيهام العكس قيل : في كونه من هذا الباب بحث إذا الظاهر من أصحاب المشأمة على الذين كفروا فلو لم يكن صاحب الكبيرة المحكوم عليه أيضاً بكونه منها كافراً لم يصح ذلك الحصر، والجواب أن هم ليس ضمير الفصل لأنه شرطه على ما بين في النحو أن يكون الخبر معرفاً باللام أو أفعل من أو فعلاً مضارعاً فعدم الحصر حينئذ ظاهر. الآية حصر قوله : ( مع عدم تكذيبهما فيه خلل مبني على توهم نظم الآية هكذا : أو الذين كذبوا بآياتنا وتبديله بقوله مع عدم كفرهم لا يجدي نفعا لأنه غير مسلم عند الخصم.
Ninth: His statement: 'And whoever disbelieves after that, then those are the transgressors' [al-Nur 24:55]. And it necessitates restricting the subject to the predicate. The correct view, corresponding to the final analysis, is that the predicate is restricted to the subject; so then it would be true that every transgressor is a disbeliever. We said: The restriction you mentioned is denied, as it is not derived from the verse, because the disbeliever is originally such. One of the commentators said: The closer view is that treating the opponent as equivalent to the denier, if the verse is made to include transgressors, is permissive on the basis of figurative meaning, as in His statement 'They have no faith' [al-Tawba 9:12], and His statement 'And if they were to know' [al-Baqarah 2:102], because it is certain that some transgressors are not deniers. His statement: 'The sinner is among those whose face is blackened by sin.' If you say: It has been reported in the authentic hadith that the nation will be raised with white marks from the traces of ablution; so how can the sinner have a blackened face on that Day? I say: Perhaps they are referring to a sinner whose sin is the abandonment of ablution. The hadiths. His statement: 'The eighth: that he is from the companions of the left hand.' It is said: They are those who are given their book in their left hand, so it is possible to deny that the committer of a major sin is among them, as a similar example will come. And it is said: They are those who are ill-omened and evil to themselves, so likewise it is possible to deny, because ill-omen and evil are due to disbelief. And it is said: Those who are led to the fire on the left. The apparent meaning also allows denial. His statement: 'From the category of reversal of implication.' It is said: There is discussion about whether it is from this category. Because the apparent meaning of 'companions of the left hand' applies to those who disbelieve; so if the committer of a major sin was not also judged to be from them and a disbeliever, that restriction would not be valid. The answer is that 'hum' is not a separating pronoun, because its condition, as explained in grammar, is that the predicate be definite with 'al', or an elative, or a present verb; so the lack of restriction is then apparent. The verse contains restriction. His statement: 'While they do not deny' — there is a flaw in it based on imagining the order of the verse as: 'or those who deny Our verses,' and replacing it with his statement 'while they do not disbelieve' does not avail, because it is not accepted by the opponent.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage covers the ninth argument and then a broad discussion of various points. The ninth argument is based on the verse from al-Nur 24:55: 'And whoever disbelieves after that, then those are the transgressors.' The opponent argues that the verse restricts the predicate (transgressors) to the subject (those who disbelieve), and by inverting, that every transgressor is a disbeliever. The refutation denies that such a restriction is derived from the verse, because the disbeliever is originally such. Then the text goes into a series of comments attributed to a commentator, addressing various issues: on the seventh argument, a hadith about the ummah being raised with traces of ablution is cited to question how a sinner could have a blackened face; the answer is that it refers to a sinner who neglects ablution. On the eighth argument, three interpretations of 'companions of the left hand' are given, each allowing that the major sinner might not be included. Then there is a critique of the reversal-of-implication argument, with a grammatical analysis of the pronoun 'hum'. Finally, a flaw is noted in the refutation concerning the denial of the denier, suggesting a misconstrual of the verse's wording. The passage is highly technical and engages with multiple layers of scholarly debate.

Key Terms
حصر
المبتدأ
الخبر
المخالف
المكذب
الاستعارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's claim about the verse from al-Nur?
A) The verse proves that every kafir is a fasiq.
B) The verse shows that every fasiq is a kafir due to restriction.
C) The verse is unclear and cannot be used as proof.
D) The verse is metaphorical and has no real meaning.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's inference that the verse proves every fasiq is a kafir is refuted by denying the claimed restriction and offering a metaphorical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that speaks about disbelief, do you ever assume it applies to all sinners without considering the context? How might that lead to unjust judgments about others?
?
Have you ever been quick to label someone as a transgressor based on a single verse, ignoring the broader principles of interpretation? What would it cost you to be more cautious?
?
If you were to apply the principle of metaphorical interpretation to verses you struggle with, what assumptions would you need to let go of? Are you willing to do that even if it challenges your comfort zone?

Arguments on Physical Descriptions: Blackened Face and Left Hand
Refutation of seventh and eighth arguments on physical marks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how arguments based on physical descriptions (blackened face, companions of left hand) are refuted by showing they do not necessarily apply to all sinners.

Core Idea
Physical descriptions in scripture do not automatically include every sinner.

This lesson examines two further arguments that attempt to identify sinners with certain physical characteristics described in scripture: a blackened face and being among the companions of the left hand. The refutation shows that these descriptions are either metaphorical or apply only to specific categories, not to all who commit major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the two arguments and their refutations
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that there are two separate arguments: one about a blackened face, another about being with the companions of the left hand.
2
Analyze the seventh argument refutation
The seventh argument claims that the sinner has a blackened face. The refutation points out that the hadith about traces of ablution contradicts this. The answer reinterprets the blackened face as referring to a specific sin (abandoning ablution), not all sins.
This shows that physical descriptions can be context-specific.
3
Analyze the eighth argument refutation
The eighth argument uses 'companions of the left hand'. Three different interpretations are given, and each allows that a major sinner might not be included. The refutation denies that the description applies universally.
Multiple interpretations show the description is not definitive.
4
Consider the principle
These refutations demonstrate that scriptural descriptions of disbelievers should not be automatically extended to all sinners. Interpretation must respect context and nuance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a Muslim committing a major sin, do you immediately assume they are among those described with blackened faces or as companions of the left hand? What would it take for you to hold back that judgment and remember that scriptural descriptions are not blanket condemnations?
?
Have you ever used a harsh description from scripture to justify writing someone off as a lost cause? How can this lesson change the way you view others' sins and your own tendency to judge?
?
If you were to be judged by the same standards you apply to others—taking every apparent sin as proof of ultimate loss—what would that mean for your own hope of mercy? How will you actively extend the same interpretive mercy you want for yourself?

Reversal-of-Implication and Final Flaw
Grammatical critique of reversal-of-implication and flaw in refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical critique of the reversal-of-implication argument and the flaw in a particular refutation regarding the wording of the verse.

Core Idea
A pronoun is not a separating pronoun unless the predicate is definite or an elative.

This lesson addresses two technical points. First, the opponent argues that the phrase 'companions of the left hand' would logically include major sinners unless they are considered disbelievers; the response uses a grammatical rule about the pronoun 'hum' to show no such restriction is implied. Second, a flaw is identified in a refutation that misconstrues the verse's wording, rendering it ineffective against the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the reversal-of-implication argument and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The opponent claims that the phrase 'companions of the left hand' must include major sinners, otherwise the restriction in the verse is invalid. The response uses a grammatical point about the pronoun 'hum'.
This argument is about logical implication (إيهام العكس).
2
Understand the grammatical rule about 'hum'
The response says 'hum' is not a separating pronoun (ضمير الفصل). A separating pronoun requires the predicate to be definite with al-, an elative, or a present verb. Since the predicate here is not, no restriction is implied.
This shows that grammar can affect theological arguments.
3
Analyze the final flaw
The passage ends with a criticism of a refutation that tries to change the wording of the verse from 'those who disbelieve' to 'those who do not disbelieve'. This is not accepted because it misrepresents the opponent's position.
The flaw is that the refutation is based on a misreading of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : ( قوله تعالى : ﴿ ومن كفر بعد ذلك فأولئك هم الفاسقون ﴾ [النور: ٥٥]، وإنه يقتضي حصر المبتدأ في الخبر)، والصحيح المطابق للنهاية حصر الخبر في المبتدأ فيصدق حينئذ أن كل فاسق كافر، قلنا: الحصر) الذي ذكرتموه ( ممنوع) كونه مستفاداً من الآية ( لأن الكافر ابتداء كذلك ) . أحد من المفسرين فالأقرب أن يقال : إن لتنزيل المخالف منزلة المكذب إذا جعلت الآية شاملة للفساق مساغاً على قاعدة المعاني كما في قوله تعالى ﴿ إنهم لا أيمان لهم ﴾ [التوبة : ١٢]، : وقوله تعالى : ﴿ ولو كانوا يعلمون ﴾ [البقرة : ١٠٢] للقطع بأن بعض الفاسقين ليس بمكذب . قوله : (الفاسق ممن وجهه مسود بالمعصية إن قلت : قد ورد في الصحيح من أن الأمة تبعث غراً من آثار الوضوء فكيف يكون الفاسق مسود الوجه في ذلك اليوم، قلت : لعلهم يوردون الكلام في فاسق يكون فسقه بترك الوضوء. الأحاديث قوله : ( الثامن : أنه من أصحاب المشأمة قيل : هم الذين يعطون كتابهم بشمالهم فيمكن منع كون مرتكب الكبيرة منهم كما سيجيء مثله وقيل : هم الذين هم شوم وشر على أنفسهم، فكذلك يمكن المنع لأن الشؤم والشر بالكفر ، وقيل : الذين يسلك بهم إلى النار شمالاً، والظاهر إمكان المنع حينئذ أيضاً. قوله : ( من باب إيهام العكس قيل : في كونه من هذا الباب بحث إذا الظاهر من أصحاب المشأمة على الذين كفروا فلو لم يكن صاحب الكبيرة المحكوم عليه أيضاً بكونه منها كافراً لم يصح ذلك الحصر، والجواب أن هم ليس ضمير الفصل لأنه شرطه على ما بين في النحو أن يكون الخبر معرفاً باللام أو أفعل من أو فعلاً مضارعاً فعدم الحصر حينئذ ظاهر. الآية حصر قوله : ( مع عدم تكذيبهما فيه خلل مبني على توهم نظم الآية هكذا : أو الذين كذبوا بآياتنا وتبديله بقوله مع عدم كفرهم لا يجدي نفعا لأنه غير مسلم عند الخصم.
Ninth: His statement: 'And whoever disbelieves after that, then those are the transgressors' [al-Nur 24:55]. And it necessitates restricting the subject to the predicate. The correct view, corresponding to the final analysis, is that the predicate is restricted to the subject; so then it would be true that every transgressor is a disbeliever. We said: The restriction you mentioned is denied, as it is not derived from the verse, because the disbeliever is originally such. One of the commentators said: The closer view is that treating the opponent as equivalent to the denier, if the verse is made to include transgressors, is permissive on the basis of figurative meaning, as in His statement 'They have no faith' [al-Tawba 9:12], and His statement 'And if they were to know' [al-Baqarah 2:102], because it is certain that some transgressors are not deniers. His statement: 'The sinner is among those whose face is blackened by sin.' If you say: It has been reported in the authentic hadith that the nation will be raised with white marks from the traces of ablution; so how can the sinner have a blackened face on that Day? I say: Perhaps they are referring to a sinner whose sin is the abandonment of ablution. The hadiths. His statement: 'The eighth: that he is from the companions of the left hand.' It is said: They are those who are given their book in their left hand, so it is possible to deny that the committer of a major sin is among them, as a similar example will come. And it is said: They are those who are ill-omened and evil to themselves, so likewise it is possible to deny, because ill-omen and evil are due to disbelief. And it is said: Those who are led to the fire on the left. The apparent meaning also allows denial. His statement: 'From the category of reversal of implication.' It is said: There is discussion about whether it is from this category. Because the apparent meaning of 'companions of the left hand' applies to those who disbelieve; so if the committer of a major sin was not also judged to be from them and a disbeliever, that restriction would not be valid. The answer is that 'hum' is not a separating pronoun, because its condition, as explained in grammar, is that the predicate be definite with 'al', or an elative, or a present verb; so the lack of restriction is then apparent. The verse contains restriction. His statement: 'While they do not deny' — there is a flaw in it based on imagining the order of the verse as: 'or those who deny Our verses,' and replacing it with his statement 'while they do not disbelieve' does not avail, because it is not accepted by the opponent.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage covers the ninth argument and then a broad discussion of various points. The ninth argument is based on the verse from al-Nur 24:55: 'And whoever disbelieves after that, then those are the transgressors.' The opponent argues that the verse restricts the predicate (transgressors) to the subject (those who disbelieve), and by inverting, that every transgressor is a disbeliever. The refutation denies that such a restriction is derived from the verse, because the disbeliever is originally such. Then the text goes into a series of comments attributed to a commentator, addressing various issues: on the seventh argument, a hadith about the ummah being raised with traces of ablution is cited to question how a sinner could have a blackened face; the answer is that it refers to a sinner who neglects ablution. On the eighth argument, three interpretations of 'companions of the left hand' are given, each allowing that the major sinner might not be included. Then there is a critique of the reversal-of-implication argument, with a grammatical analysis of the pronoun 'hum'. Finally, a flaw is noted in the refutation concerning the denial of the denier, suggesting a misconstrual of the verse's wording. The passage is highly technical and engages with multiple layers of scholarly debate.

Key Terms
إيهام العكس
ضمير الفصل
الخبر معرف باللام

Checkpoint
According to the response, what condition must be met for 'hum' to be a separating pronoun?
A) The predicate must be indefinite.
B) The predicate must be definite with 'al', an elative, or a present verb.
C) The subject must be a proper noun.
D) The verb must be in the past tense.

Key Takeaway
The grammatical rule that 'hum' only functions as a separating pronoun under specific conditions is used to refute the reversal-of-implication argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted an argument that seemed plausible at first glance, only to discover it rested on a flawed premise? How can you train yourself to verify conditions before accepting conclusions?
?
When defending a belief you hold dear, have you ever been tempted to reinterpret scripture or evidence to suit your view? This passage warns against misreading the verse. What safeguards can you put in place to ensure your interpretations remain faithful to the text?
?
The opponent’s refutation failed because it was not accepted by the other side. In your own debates, do you seek common ground and agreed-upon premises, or do you rely on reinterpretations that others may reject? How can you build arguments that truly engage the opposition?
