Eleventh Proof: The Sinner Is Disgraced in Hellfire
Sanusi presents the eleventh proof from Quran 3:192 and 16:27 and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eleventh proof against sinful believers and the two Ashʿarī refutations based on the scope of the definite article and the meaning of 'disgrace'.

Core Idea
A definite noun does not automatically imply universality in Ashʿarī logic.

Sanūsī presents a proof that attempts to use two Quranic verses to argue that every sinful believer entering Hellfire must be a disbeliever. The proof relies on the assumption that 'disgrace' in the first verse is universal and the second verse states that disgrace is only for disbelievers. However, the author offers two powerful refutations that dismantle this syllogism by challenging the universality of the definite article and the intended meaning of 'disgrace'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Focus on grasping the overall argument structure.
2
Identify the syllogism
The proof combines Quran 3:192 and 16:27. Identify the two premises and the conclusion.
Premise 1: Whoever enters the Fire is disgraced. Premise 2: Disgrace is for disbelievers. Conclusion: The sinful believer must be a disbeliever.
3
Understand the first refutation
The first response denies that the definite noun 'al-khizy' implies universality. According to Ashʿarī uṣūl, the definite article does not automatically mean 'all'.
This refutation does not deny the first verse's truth, only its scope.
4
Understand the second refutation
The second response concedes generality for argument's sake but clarifies that only 'complete disgrace' applies to disbelievers. The partial disgrace of believers is not included.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these refutations matter for the doctrine that sinful believers may be temporarily punished in Hellfire without becoming disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادي عشر: ( قوله تعالى: ( إنك من تدخل النار فقد أخزيته ﴾ [آل عمران: ۱۹۲]، مع قوله: إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين [النحل: ٢٧])، وتقريره إن الفاسق يدخل النار للآيات العامة الموعدة، وكل من يدخل النار فهو مخزي للآية الأولى، وكل مخزي كافر للآية الثانية، قلنا: المفرد المحلى باللام) وهو الخزي هاهنا ( لا عموم (له عندنا فلا يلزم انحصار الخزي مطلقاً الكافر، (أو) نقول : المراد) (به على تقدير عمومه (الخزي الكامل) فيلزم حينئذ انحصار أفراده في الكافر لا انحصار أفراد الخزي مطلقاً فيه.
The eleventh: His saying, exalted be He: 'Indeed, whoever You admit to the Fire, You have disgraced him' [Aal-e-Imran 3:192], along with His saying: 'Indeed, disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' [an-Nahl 16:27]. Its formulation is that the sinner enters the Fire due to the general threatening verses, and everyone who enters the Fire is disgraced by the first verse, and every disgraced person is a disbeliever by the second verse. We said: The definite noun (al-mufrad al-muḥallā bi al-lām) – i.e., 'disgrace' here – does not have generality (ʿumūm) according to us, so it is not necessary that disgrace is exclusively for the disbeliever. Or we say: What is meant by it, assuming its generality, is complete disgrace (al-khizy al-kāmil), so then the exclusivity of its individuals to the disbeliever follows, not the absolute exclusivity of individuals of disgrace.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof. It combines Quran 3:192 (whoever enters the Fire is disgraced) and 16:27 (disgrace is for disbelievers) to argue that the sinner, who enters the Fire, must be a disbeliever. The response offers two refutations. First, the definite noun 'al-khizy' (disgrace) does not imply universality (ʿumūm) in the Ashʿarī view, so the first verse does not entail that all who enter the Fire are disgraced in the same way. Second, even if it were universal, the intended meaning is 'complete disgrace' (al-khizy al-kāmil), which applies only to disbelievers, not to the incomplete disgrace of sinful believers. Thus, the syllogism fails.

Key Terms
المفرد المحلى باللام
الخزي
الكافر
العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definite article (al-) always indicates universality/generality.
Correction: According to Ashʿarī uṣūl, the definite noun does not automatically imply generality; it can be specific or generic. Thus, al-khizy does not mean 'all disgrace'.

Checkpoint
What are the two responses given to the argument from Quran 3:192 and 16:27?
A) The verses are abrogated and the sinner does not actually enter the Fire.
B) The definite noun does not imply universality, and the intended meaning is complete disgrace.
C) The sinner is not disgraced and the verses refer only to hypocrites.
D) The verses are metaphorical and the sinner repents before death.

Key Takeaway
The eleventh proof fails because the definite article does not guarantee universality, and 'disgrace' in the verse refers to complete disgrace specific to disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to stand before Allah today, would the disgrace you fear be that of a disbeliever or a believer? How does your daily life reflect that distinction?
?
The lesson distinguishes between complete disgrace (for disbelievers) and incomplete (for believers). Are you living as if your sins will only bring temporary disgrace, or have you become complacent about the reality of Hellfire even for believers?
?
Allah says, 'Whoever You admit to the Fire, You have disgraced him.' Knowing that even a believer may face disgrace in the Fire, how does that change your urgency to repent from sins you consider 'minor'?

The Rational Argument for Obligation and Its Rebuttal
Al-Iji presents a rational argument for obligation and then rebuts it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for the obligation of appointing an Imam and why it is rejected by Ashʿarī scholars.

Core Idea
Reason cannot impose obligations; they come only from revelation.

Al-Iji presents the rationalist case for the obligation of appointing an Imam, based on the principle of repelling harm. He then rebuts it by denying that reason alone can impose obligations. This lesson unpacks the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that it contains both an argument and an answer.
The argument uses analogy with poison and falling walls.
2
Understand the argument's structure
The argument: general principle (repelling harm is obligatory by reason), then application to probable harm (appointing an Imam prevents probable harm), then analogy with poison and falling wall.
3
Examine the rebuttal
The rebuttal denies that reason can impose obligation; obligations are derived only from revelation.
This is a key Ashʿarī position.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for Islamic theology: it limits the role of reason in determining religious duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الموجبون : لنصب الإمام على الأنام عقلاً إن أصل دفع المضرة واجب بحكم العقل قطعاً، فكذلك المضرة المظنونة يجب دفعها عقلا وذلك لأن الجزئيات المظنونة المندرجة تحت أصل قطعي الحكم يجب اندراجها في ذلك الحكم قطعاً مثل أن يعرف الإنسان أن كل مسموم يجب اجتنابه ثم يظن أن هذا الطعام مسموم فإن العقل الصريح يقضي بوجوب اجتنابه وكذا من علم أن الحائط الساقط لا يجوز الوقوف تحته، ثم ظن أن هذا الحائط يسقط فالعقل الصريح يقضي بوجوب أن لا يقف تحته، والجواب : منع حكم العقل بالوجوب وأخواته بل هي لا تستفاد إلا من الشرع .
Then the proponents of obligation said: Appointing an Imam upon the people is rationally obligatory. Indeed, the principle of repelling harm is obligatory by rational judgment absolutely. Likewise, probable harm must be repelled rationally, because particular probable instances subsumed under a certain principle must be included in that judgment certainly. For example, if a person knows that every poison must be avoided, then thinks this food is poisoned, sound reason judges that he must avoid it. Similarly, if one knows that a falling wall should not be stood under, then thinks this wall is falling, sound reason judges that he must not stand under it. The answer: The ruling of reason by obligation and its like is denied; rather, they are only derived from revelation.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument for the obligation of appointing an Imam, then immediately rebuts it. The argument is that since repelling harm is rationally obligatory in general, and appointing an Imam repels probable harm (the likelihood of harm from anarchy), it must be rationally obligatory by analogy with clear cases like avoiding poison or falling walls. The rebuttal denies that reason alone can impose obligation; such rulings come only from divine law. The passage thus critiques the use of reason as a source for positive obligations in Islamic law.

Key Terms
دفع المضرة
وجوب عقلي
أصل قطعي
ظن
حكم العقل
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that reason alone can establish religious obligations like appointing an Imam.
Correction: The rebuttal shows that reason cannot impose obligations; they are only derived from revelation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the rational argument for the obligation of appointing an Imam?
A) Because the principle of repelling harm is not known by reason
B) Because reason cannot impose obligations; obligations come only from revelation
C) Because the analogy with poison and falling walls is invalid
D) Because the harm repelled is not certain but only probable

Key Takeaway
Reason alone cannot establish religious obligations; they require divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied solely on your reason to decide what is morally required, ignoring what Allah has commanded? In what area of your life today are you trusting your judgment over revelation?
?
The passage shows that reason cannot impose obligations. Does this confront your tendency to justify sins by saying 'it makes sense' or 'it's not harming anyone'? What one habit are you rationalizing right now that needs to be evaluated by revelation instead?
?
If obligations come only from revelation, how seriously do you take learning the Shariah? Do you spend more time following your own logic or studying what Allah has commanded? What step will you take today to prioritize divine commands over your own reasoning?

Proof from Intellectual Obligation to Repel Harm and Its Refutation
Proponents argue that repelling probable harm is intellectually obligatory via analogy; author refutes by denying rational obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that repelling probable harm is intellectually obligatory and the author's refutation that reason does not impose moral obligations.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot impose obligations; only revelation can.

In this passage, we examine a key argument for the intellectual obligation of repelling harm, used by those who claim the imamate is rationally required. The proponents employ analogies from everyday life, but the author offers a sharp refutation: moral categories like obligation are not known by reason but only through divine law. This lesson unpacks the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: claim, proof by analogy, and refutation.
2
Identify the proponents' claim and principle
Find the principle that repelling certain harm is obligatory by reason. Understand how they extend this to probable harm.
The key term is 'دفع المضرة' (repelling harm).
3
Analyze the analogies
The proponents give two analogies: poison and falling wall. Ask yourself why these analogies are meant to show rational obligation.
Both involve a certain general principle and a probable particular case.
4
Understand the refutation
The author denies that reason judges obligation at all. Moral judgments come only from revelation (الشرع). This is a fundamental point in the debate.
The phrase 'منع حكم العقل بالوجوب' is the core of the refutation.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this means for the imamate debate: if reason cannot oblige, then the obligation to appoint an imam must come from revelation or not at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الموجبون : ( لنصب الإمام على الأنام عقلاً ( إن أصل دفع المضرة واجب ( بحكم العقل ( قطعاً، فكذلك المضرة المظنونة ) يجب دفعها عقلا ) وذلك ) لأن الجزئيات المظنونة المندرجة تحت أصل قطعي الحكم يجب اندراجها في ذلك الحكم قطعاً ( مثل أن يعرف الإنسان أن كل مسموم يجب اجتنابه ثم يظن أن هذا الطعام مسموم فإن العقل الصريح يقضي بوجوب اجتنابه وكذا من علم أن الحائط الساقط لا يجوز الوقوف تحته، ثم ظن أن هذا الحائط يسقط فالعقل الصريح يقضي بوجوب أن لا يقف تحته، والجواب : منع حكم العقل ) بالوجوب وأخواته بل هي لا تستفاد إلا من الشرع .
Then the obligers said: The appointment of an imam over the people is intellectually obligatory, because the principle of repelling harm is absolutely obligatory by the judgment of reason, and similarly a probable harm must be repelled intellectually, because the probable particulars subsumed under a certain principle must be subsumed under that judgment certainly—like a person knowing that every poison must be avoided, then suspecting that this food is poison: sound reason judges that he must avoid it. Likewise, one who knows that a falling wall may not be stood under, then suspects that this wall is falling: sound reason judges that he must not stand under it. The answer is the denial that reason judges obligation and its likes; rather, these are only derived from revelation.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from those who hold that appointing an imam is intellectually obligatory (wajib 'aqlan). The proof is based on the principle of repelling harm (daf' al-madarrah): just as repelling certain harm is intellectually obligatory, so is repelling probable harm, because particulars of probable harm are subsumed under the certain general principle. Two analogies are given: knowing that every poison must be avoided, and then suspecting a particular food is poison, reason obliges avoidance; knowing that a falling wall must not be stood under, and then suspecting a particular wall is falling, reason obliges not standing there. The author's refutation is a simple denial: reason does not judge obligation or similar moral categories; these are only derived from divine law (shar'). Thus the proof fails because it assumes moral obligation is known by reason, which the author rejects.

Key Terms
دفع المضرة
مضرة مظنونة
حكم العقل
الوجوب العقلي
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rational probability always creates moral obligation to act.
Correction: The author argues that reason does not judge obligation; only revelation does, so probable harm does not necessarily create a rational obligation.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that repelling probable harm is intellectually obligatory?
A) By denying that the principle of repelling harm is certain
B) By denying that reason can judge obligation and similar concepts; they come only from revelation
C) By arguing that the harm is not probable
D) By stating that the imamate is not a probable harm

Key Takeaway
The author rejects the claim that reason alone can impose moral obligations; such obligations come only from divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you acted based solely on your own rational calculation of harm rather than seeking what Allah has commanded. How does the author's refutation challenge that mindset? What would it mean for you to submit to revelation even when your reason seems certain?
?
The obligers used the example of poison to argue that reason demands avoidance. But the author says reason cannot obligate. How often do you assume that your own reasoning about right and wrong is sufficient? Will you allow the Quran and Sunnah to override your own judgments, even when they seem obvious?
?
Are there areas in your life where you feel that 'common sense' or 'rational self-interest' dictate a certain action, but you have not checked whether revelation commands something else? What step will you take today to align your decisions with the Sharia rather than your own reason?
